 <p align="CENTER"><font size="-1" color="GREEN">An Arthurian Miscellany at sacred-texts.com</font></p><hr> <center> <h2 align="CENTER">SAMOR, LORD OF THE BRIGHT CITY</h2>  <h3 align="CENTER">by</h3>  <h2 align="CENTER">H. H. MILMAN</h2>  </center>  <br>  <br>  <b> PREFACE.</b> <br>  <br>  The Historians  [Note 1] of the Empire near the period of time, at which this Poem commences, make mention of a Constantine, who assumed the purple of the western empire, gained possession of Gaul and Spain, but was defeated and slain at the battle of Arles.  He had a son named Constans, who became a monk, and was put to death at Vienna.<br>            About the same time a Constantine appears in the relations of the old British Chronicles and Romances.  He was brother of the king of Armorica, and became himself King, or rather an elected sovereign of the petty Kings of Britain  [Note 2], who continued their succession under the Roman dominion.  He was called Vendigard  [Note 3] and Waredur, the Defender and Deliverer.  He had three sons, Constans, who became a hermit, and was murthered, either (for the traditions vary) by the Picts, by Vortigern, or by the Saxons; Emrys, called by the Latin writers Aurelius Ambrosius; and Uther Pendragon, the father of Arthur.  These two Constantines are here identified, and Vortigern supposed to have been named King of Britain, as the person of greatest authority and conduct in the wreck of the British army, defeated at Arles.  Many, however, of the chiefs in the Island advancing the hereditary right, before formally settled on the sons of Constantine, Vortigern, mistrusting the Britons, and prest by invasions of the Caledonians, introduced the Saxons to check the barbarians and strengthen his own sovereignty.<br>            The Hero of the Poem is an historical character, as far as such legends can be called History.  He appears in most of the Chronicles, as Edol, or Eldol, but the fullest account of his exploits is in Dugdale's Baronage under his title of Earl of Gloucester.  William Harrison, however, in the Description of Britain prefixed to Holinshed, calls him Eldulph de Samor.  But all concur in ascribing to him the acts which make the chief subject of the fifth and last Books of this Poem.<br>            Most of our present names of places being purely Saxon, and the old British having little of harmony or association to recommend them, I have frequently, on the authority of Camden and others, translated them.  Thus the Saxon Gloucester, called by the Britons, Caer Gloew, is the Bright City.  The Dobuni, the inhabitants of the Vales, are called by that name.  Some few sanctioned by old usage of Poetry and Romance I retain, as Kent, Thanet, Cornwall.  London is Troynovant, as the City of the Trinobantes.<br>            Some passages in the Poem will be easily traced to their acknowledged sources, the Poets of Greece and Italy; one however, in the third book, relating to the Northern mythology, has been remarkably anticipated in a modern Poem.  The honourable Author may be assured that the conincidence is unintentional, as that part of this Poem was the earliest written, and previous to the appearance of his production.<br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  <b> <h3 align="CENTER">BOOK I.</h3> </b>   Land of my birth, oh Britain!  and my love,<br>  Whose air I breathe, whose earth I tread, whose tongue<br>  My song would speak, its strong and solemn tones<br>  Most proud, if I abase not.  Beauteous Isle,<br>  And plenteous!  what though in thy atmosphere [5]<br>  Float not the taintless luxury of light,<br>  The dazzling azure of the Southern skies;<br>  Around thee the rich orb of thy renown<br>  Spreads stainless, and unsullied by a cloud.<br>  Though thy hills blush not with the purple vine, [10]<br>  And softer climes excel thee in the hue<br>  And fragrance of thy summer fruits and flowers,<br>  Nor flow thy rivers over golden beds;<br>  Thou in the Soul of man, thy better wealth,<br>  Art richest: nature's noblest produce thou, [15]<br>  The immortal Mind in perfect height and strength,<br>  Bear'st with a prodigal opulence; this thy right,<br>  Thy privilege of climate and of soil,<br>  Would I assert: nor, save thy fame, invoke,<br>  Or Nymph, or Muse, that oft 'twas dream'd of old [20]<br>  By falls of waters under haunted shades,<br>  Her extacy of inspiration pour'd<br>  O'er Poet's soul, and flooded all his powers<br>  With liquid glory: so may thy renown<br>  Burn in my heart, and give to thought and word<br>  The aspiring and the radiant hue of fire. [25]<br>  <br>  Forth from the gates of Troynovant hath past<br>  King Vortigern; the Princes of the Isle<br>  Around him; on the walls, for then (though now<br>  Scorn bounds her mighty wilderness of streets,<br>  And in magnificence of multitude [30]<br>  Spread, and illimitable grandeur,) walls<br>  With jealous circuit and embattled range<br>  Girt Britain's narrow Capital; where swarm'd<br>  Eager her wondering citizens to see<br>  The Monarch.  Him the Saxon Hengist met, [35]<br>  And Horsa, with their bands in triumph led,<br>  As from a recent victory; their blue eyes<br>  Sparkled, and proud they shook their saffron hair;<br>  And in the bicker of their spears, the toss<br>  Of ponderous mallets, the quick flash of swords, [40]<br>  Th' emblazon'd White Horse on their banners waved,<br>  Was triumph.  Thus king Vortigern began:<br>  <br>  "Welcome, Deliverers!  of our kingdom's foes,<br>  Welcome, thrice-honour'd Conquerors!  never more<br>  Shall painted Caledonian o'er our realm [45]<br>  The chariots of his rapine wheel, so full<br>  The desolation, havoc so complete<br>  Hath smote and blasted in Erle Hengist's path.<br>  The mouldering ruins of our Roman wall,<br>  Leagued with the terror of the Saxon name, [50]<br>  Shall be defence more mighty, than when soared<br>  Its battlements unbroken, and above<br>  The imperial Eagle shook its wings of gold.<br>  Oh, toil'd with victory, burthen'd with renown,<br>  For ye our baths float cool and clear, our air [55]<br>  Is redolent with garland wreaths, and rich<br>  Within our royal citadel is crown'd<br>  For ye the banquet; welcome once again,<br>  Mighty to save, and potent to defend!" -- <br>  A faint acclaim, a feeble sullen din [60]<br>  Ensued, with less of gladness than fierce grief,<br>  And wrath ill stifled.  Seeming all unmoved,<br>  Elate the Monarch onward led the way;<br>  Slow follow'd Saxon Hengist's martial train,<br>  Clashing their armour loud, as they would daunt [65]<br>  All Britain with the clamour: march'd behind<br>  The island Nobles, save some restless hands<br>  Were busy with their sheathed swords, they mov'd<br>  Silent, and cold, and gloomy, as a range<br>  Of mountain pines, when cloudy lowers the storm. [70]<br>  <br>  Upon the azure bosom of the Thames<br>  Reclining, with its ponderous mass of shade,<br>  Arose the royal Citadel, the work<br>  Of the great Cæsar.  Danger he and dread<br>  Of Rome and Pompey; yet 'gainst savage foes [75]<br>  Vantage of trench and tower and massy wall<br>  Scorn'd not, so swift, so perilous, so fierce<br>  Cassivelan his painted charioteers<br>  Whirl'd to the frantic onset, standing forth<br>  Portent of freedom mid a world enslav'd. [80]<br>  <br>  They pass'd the portal arch; the sumptuous hall<br>  Flung back its gates; around the banquet board<br>  Rang'd Prince and Chieftain, where luxurious art<br>  Shower'd prodigal her dainties, poisons sweet,<br>  And baleful splendour.  Fierce the Saxon gaz'd [85]<br>  On goblet, and huge charger carved with gold,<br>  Contemptuous wonder.  But the Monarch's brow<br>  'Gan lighten, as with greedy joy he quaff'd<br>  Oblivious bliss; thus ever guilty soul<br>  Woos frenzy, and, voluptuous from despair, [90]<br>  Forgets itself to pleasure.  High aloof,<br>  Each in his azure robe, the band of Bards<br>  Mingled the wanton luxuries of sound;<br>  Gentle melodious languour, melting fall,<br>  With faint effeminate flattery the soul [95]<br>  Guiling of manhood.  Silent veil'd his harp<br>  White-hair'd Aneurin, and indignant tears<br>  Stood in the old man's eye, for wrathful shame<br>  To hear his godlike and heaven-breathing art<br>  Pampering loose revels with officious chime. [100]<br>  Then rose the glorious madness; forth he sprung,<br>  With one rude stroke along the clashing chords<br>  Won silence deep as of a summer eve<br>  After a noontide storm; his silver locks<br>  Wav'd proud, the kindling frenzy of his eye [105]<br>  Flash'd triumph, as the song of Chariots rose.<br>  The song that o'er the van of battle shower'd<br>  Pale horror, when that scourg'd Icenian Queen<br>  Through the square legions drove her car; were heard<br>  Her brazen wheels to madden, the keen scythes [110]<br>  Gride through their iron harvest; then rush'd route,<br>  Wail'd havoc; seem'd Bonduca fiercer urg'd<br>  The trampling steeds; behind her silence sank<br>  Along the dreary path of her revenge.<br>  <br>  Ceas'd the bold strain, then deep the Saxon drain'd<br>  The ruddy cup, and savage joy uncouth [116]<br>  Lit his blue gleaming eyes: nor sate unmov'd<br>  The Briton Chiefs; fierce thoughts began to rise<br>  Of ancient wars, and high ancestral fame.<br>  Sudden came floating through the hall an air [120]<br>  So strangely sweet, the o'erwrought sense scarce felt<br>  Its rich excess of pleasure; softer sounds<br>  Melt never on the enchanted midnight cool,<br>  By haunted spring, where elfin dancers trace<br>  Green circlets on the moonlight dews; nor lull [125]<br>  Becalmed mariner from rocks, where basks<br>  At summer noon the Sea-maid; he his oar<br>  Breathless suspends, and motionless his bark<br>  Sleeps on the sleeping waters.  Now the notes<br>  So gently died away, the silence seem'd [130]<br>  Melodious; merry now and light and blithe<br>  They danced on air: anon came tripping forth<br>  In frolic grace a maiden troop, their locks<br>  Flower-wreath'd, their snowy robes from clasped zone<br>  Fell careless drooping, quick their glittering feet [135]<br>  Glanc'd o'er the pavement.  Then the pomp of sound<br>  Swell'd up, and mounted; as the stately swan,<br>  Her milk-white neck embower'd in arching spray,<br>  Queens it along the waters, entered in<br>  The lofty hall a shape so fair, it lull'd [140]<br>  The music into silence, yet itself<br>  Pour'd out, prolonging the soft extacy,<br>  The trembling and the touching of sweet sound.<br>  Her grace of motion and of look, the smooth<br>  And swimming majesty of step and tread, [145]<br>  The symmetry of form and feature, set<br>  The soul afloat, even like delicious airs<br>  Of flute or harp: as though she trod from earth,<br>  And round her wore an emanating cloud<br>  Of harmony, the Lady mov'd.  Too proud [150]<br>  For less than absolute command, too soft<br>  For aught but gentle amorous thought: her hair<br>  Cluster'd, as from an orb of gold cast out<br>  A dazzling and o'erpowering radiance, save<br>  Here and there on her snowy neck repos'd<br>  In a sooth'd brilliance some thin wandering tress. [155]<br>  The azure flashing of her eye was fring'd<br>  With virgin meekness, and her tread, that seem'd<br>  Earth to disdain, as softly fell on it<br>  As the light dew-shower on a tuft of flowers.<br>  The soul within seem'd feasting on high thoughts, [160]<br>  That to the outward form and feature gave<br>  A loveliness of scorn, scorn that to feel<br>  Was bliss, was sweet indulgence.  Fast sank back<br>  Those her fair harbingers, their modest eyes,<br>  Downcast, and drooping low their slender necks [165]<br>  In graceful reverence; she, by wond'ring gaze<br>  Unmov'd, and stifled murmurs of applause,<br>  Nor yet unconscious, slowly won her way<br>  To where the King, amid the festal pomp,<br>  Sate loftiest; as she rais'd a fair-chas'd cup, [170]<br>  Something of sweet confusion overspread<br>  Her features; something tremulous broke in<br>  On her half-failing accents, as she said,<br>  "Health to the King!" -- the sparkling wine laugh'd up,<br>  As eager 'twere to touch so fair a lip. [175]<br>  <br>  A moment, and the apparition bright<br>  Had parted; as before, the sound of harps<br>  Was wantoning about the festive hall.<br>  <br>  As one just waking from a blissful dream<br>  Nor moves, nor breathes, lest breath or motion break [180]<br>  The beauteous tissue of fine form woven o'er<br>  His fancy, sate king Vortigern.  "Whence came,<br>  And whither went she?  of what race and stem<br>  Sprang this bright wonder of our earth, that leaves<br>  The rapture of her presence in our hall, [185]<br>  Though parted thence too swiftly?" -- "King (replied<br>  Erle Hengist) -- in our ancient Saxon faith,<br>  Ill bodes the joyless feast, where maiden's lips<br>  Pledge not the wassail goblet." -- "By my soul,"<br>  Cried Vortigern, "a gallant faith! and I [190]<br>  Omen so sweet discredit not; the health<br>  Those smooth lips wish'd me, well those lips might give,<br>  A fragrance and a sparkling have they left<br>  Even on the wine they touch'd." -- He said, and prest<br>  The goblet to his own.  "A father's ear, [195]<br>  King Vortigern, must love the flattering tongue<br>  That descants lavish on his daughter's praise."<br>  "Thy daughter?  Saxon!" -- "Mine, though vaunt not I<br>  Her beauty, many a German Erle and King<br>  Hath vow'd at his life's peril to proclaim [200]<br>  Her far-surpassing comeliness." -- None heard<br>  The secret converse that ensued.  Lo, rose<br>  King Vortigern, and from his brow transferr'd<br>  A coronet of radiant Eastern gems<br>  To the white hair of Hengist, and drank off [205]<br>  A brimming cup, and cried, "To Kent's high King<br>  A health, a health to Vortigern's fair bride,<br>  The golden-hair'd Rowena." -- Seiz'd at once<br>  Each Saxon the exulting strain, and struck<br>  The wine-drain'd goblet down, "Health, King of Kent!"<br>  <br>  As mid the fabled Libyan bridal stood [211]<br>  Perseus, in stern tranquillity of wrath,<br>  Half stood, half floated at his ancle plumes<br>  Out-swelling, while the bright face on his shield<br>  Look'd into stone the raging fray; so rose, [215]<br>  But with no magic arms, wearing alone<br>  Th' appalling and control of his firm look,<br>  The solemn indignation of his brow,<br>  The Briton Samor; at his rising, awe<br>  Went abroad, and the riotous hall was mute; [220]<br>  But like unruffled summer waters flow'd<br>  His speech, and courtly reverence smooth'd its tone.<br>  <br>  "Sovereign of Britain's Sovereigns!  of our crowns<br>  The highest!  in our realm of many thrones<br>  Enthron'd the loftiest!  mighty as thou art, [225]<br>  Thou dost outstep thy amplitude of sway;<br>  Thine is our isle to govern not to give;<br>  A free and sacred property hast thou<br>  In our allegiance; for a master's right<br>  Over our lives, our princedoms, and our souls, [230]<br>  King Vortigern, as well mayst thou presume<br>  To a dominion o'er our winds, to set<br>  Thy stamp and impress on our light from heaven.<br>  This Britain cannot rest beneath the shade<br>  Of Saxon empire, this our Christian soil [235]<br>  The harvest of obedience will not bear<br>  To Heathen sway; and hear me, Vortigern,<br>  The golden image that thou settest up,<br>  Like the pride-drunken Babylonian king,<br>  Though dulcimer and psaltery soothe us down [240]<br>  To the soft humour of submission tame,<br>  We will not worship." -- From the hall he past,<br>  Thus saying.  Him the Island's brave and proud<br>  Follow'd, the high and fame-enamour'd souls,<br>  Never to Britain wanting, though in hours [245]<br>  Loosest of revels soft, and wanton ease.<br>  But Vortigern, more largely pouring in<br>  The vine's delicious poison, sate, and cried,<br>  "Whom the flax binds not, must the iron gyve,<br>  Whom sceptres daunt not, must the sword control."<br>  <br>  Evening fell gentle, and the brilliant sun [251]<br>  Was going down into the waveless Thames,<br>  As bearing light and warmth to her cold Nymphs<br>  Within their crystal chambers, when the King<br>  Left the hall of banquet.  Lofty and alone, [255]<br>  Even as the Pillar great Alcides set,<br>  The limit of the world and his renown,<br>  On Calpe, round whose shaft the daylight wreathed<br>  Its last empurpling, on the battlements<br>  Stood Samor in the amethystine light, [260]<br>  And "Go to darkness, thou majestic orb!<br>  To-morrow shall the nations bask again<br>  In thy full glory." -- Thus he said, and turn'd<br>  To where the King went rapid past. -- "And thou,<br>  Thou to thy setting hastest, never more [265]<br>   Thou thy benighted splendour to renew;<br>  Late at thy noon of pride, now sunk, declined<br>  For ever from thy fair meridian, go<br>  Into thy cloudy rest!" -- The solemn tone<br>  Of his deep voice seized on the King, as frosts [270]<br>  Arrest the rapid flowing stream. -- "What means<br>  The Sovereign of the Vales, even in my halls,<br>  And on my castle battlements, to cast<br>  Bold scorn on Britain's king?  Ingrate, and blind,<br>  When I the valiant Saxon have brought in [275]<br>  To check the Caledonian, through your isle<br>  Marching by wild light of your burning towns;<br>  Ye, wedded to your sorrow and your shame,<br>  Mock at the safety my free love provides."<br>  "Ah, provident! ah, sage! ah, generous King! [280]<br>  That sets the emaciate wolf to dog the flock;<br>  The hawk to guard the dovecote." -- "Wise-lipp'd chief,<br>  I thank thee for thy phrase: doves are ye, doves<br>  That fly with piteous and most delicate speed<br>  Before the Scottish kites, that swoop your nests [285]<br>  And flesh their greedy talons in your young." --<br>  "Monarch!  the eaglet, were it smoothly nurst<br>  In the dove's downy nest, at its first flight<br>  Would shrink down dazzled from the morning sun;<br>  But with strong plumes refresh'd, anon 'twould claim<br>  Its old aspiring birthright, and unblench'd [291]<br>  Bathe in the bickering of the noontide car.<br>  Oh, we have slumber'd on soft luxury's lap,<br>  To her loose tabret; but, misjudging King!<br>  Britain is like her soil; above the turf [295]<br>  Lies velvet smooth, hard iron lurks beneath.<br>  I know the northern Pagans waste our land,<br>  And the tame mission to the Roman sent<br>  I know, 'The fierce Barbarian to the sea<br>  Drives us, the sea to the Barbarian back [300]<br>  Merciless': so ran the plaintive legend.  True!<br>  But soldiers would it cast us back; despair<br>  Hath its own valour; war makes warriors.  King!<br>  Calamities are on us, evil days<br>  O'er our isle darken, but the noble wear [305]<br>  Disaster, as an Angel wears his wings,<br>  To elevate and glorify.  Nor us<br>  Shroudeth along the enveloping gloom, the frame<br>  And fabric of our world is breaking up.<br>  Rome's dome of empire, that o'ervaulted earth [310]<br>  With its capacious shadow, rent and split,<br>  Disorders the smooth course of human things,<br>  Leaving confusion lord of this wide ball,<br>  While to and fro the Nations sway perplex'd,<br>  Like a tempestuous sea.  Oh, mid such wreck, [315]<br>  Our Britain in lone safety to uphold,<br>  On every side 'gainst gathering foes present<br>  A rampire of hard steel, or firmer far,<br>  The bulwark of a haughty spirit pour'd<br>  From the thron'd Sovereign through her sons, were pride,<br>  Were honour, might arrest Heaven's plumed hosts. [321]<br>  And in their sphere-born music win renown.<br>  So He whose sceptre glitters in thy grasp,<br>  He the Deliverer, the Defender nam'd,<br>  So Constantine had done, had the high Soul's bane, [325]<br>  Ambition, never maddened him to wear<br>  The purple, madly worn, yet nobly lost<br>  On the sad plain by Arles." -- "I knew, I knew<br>  'Twould come to this, that Constantine would end<br>  The high-wrought orat'ry.  This too I know, [330]<br>  And this I tell thee, Samor!  nor yet add<br>  Rebel!  thy secret commerce with his sons,<br>  To undermine my stately throne; the right,<br>  So babble ye in your licentious phrase,<br>  Conferr'd by our assembled British Kings [335]<br>  On Constantine for ever and his heirs." --<br>  <br>  "Alas!  how better were it to know nought,<br>  Than, like Kings, darkly.  Constantine's brave sons<br>  And Samor oft have met, have met to wail<br>  The hazard of their native land, to swear [340]<br>  Before the altar of the eternal God,<br>  Never, amid these rude and perilous times,<br>  To blow the trump of civil strife, to prop<br>  With their allegiance Britain's throne, though fill'd<br>  By one they deem usurping.  Vortigern! [345]<br>  I am upon the string that jars thy soul,<br>  And it must vibrate to its highest pitch.<br>  Oh what a royal madness, that might build<br>  Upon the strong rock of a people's love,<br>  Yet chooseth the loose quicksand of distrust, [350]<br>  And overlays the palace of his pride<br>  With a rude Saxon buttress, whose stern weight<br>  Must crush it.  Thou dost fear thy subjects arm'd,<br>  Fear, lest the old valiance in their hearts inure,<br>  And therefore fight'st their wars with foreign steel; [355]<br>  And is this he, the noble and the wise,<br>  The Vortigern, that Britain on the plain<br>  Of Arles, that fatal plain, hail'd Captain, King?<br>  Arise, be King, be Captain, be thyself!<br>  And we will stand around thy throne, and mock [360]<br>  The ruinous fashion of the times." -- "Away!<br>  My royal word is to the Saxon given."<br>  "Oh, Vortigern!  this knee hath never bow'd,<br>  Save to the King of Kings, thus low on earth<br>  I sue thee, cast the Saxon off." -- At once [365]<br>  The swift contagious grandeur set on fire<br>  The Monarch -- "I am thine, am Britain's all:<br>  Now by my throne, thus, thus I have not felt,<br>  Since first this circling gold eat in my brow,<br>  So free, so upright, and so kingly, chains [370]<br>  Fall from me, mists are curling off my soul."<br>  <br>  Like two bold Venturers, silently they stand,<br>  Launching amid the sun-light their rich bark<br>  O'er glassy waters to the summer airs:<br>  Their solemn pondering hath the lofty look [375]<br>  Of vaunting, over each high brow flames out<br>  A noble rivalry of hope and pride.<br>  <br>  The sound of wheels, lo, sliding came and smooth<br>  A car, wherein, like some fair Idol led<br>  Through the mute tumult of adoring streets, [380]<br>  Bright-hair'd Rowena pass'd the portal arch.<br>  <br>  Have ye a sense, ye gales, a conscious joy<br>  In beauty, that with such an artful touch<br>  And light ye float about her garment folds,<br>  Displaying what is exquisite display'd, [385]<br>  And thinly scattering the light veil where'er<br>  Its shadowing may enhance the grace, and swell<br>  With sweet officiousness the clustering hair<br>  Where fairest tufts its richness, and let fall<br>  Where drooping most becomes; that thus ye love [390]<br>  To lose yourselves about her, and expire<br>  Upon her shape, or snow-white robes?  She stood,<br>  Her ivory arm in a soft curve stretch'd out,<br>  As only in the obedience of her steeds<br>  Rejoicing; they their necks arch'd proud and high, [395]<br>  And by her delicate and flower-soft hands<br>  Sway'd, as enamour'd of her mastery mov'd,<br>  Lovingly on their bright-chaf'd bits repos'd,<br>  Or in gay sport upon each other fawn'd.<br>  But as the Monarch she beheld, she caught [400]<br>  The slack rein up, and with unconscious check<br>  Delay'd the willing coursers, and her head,<br>  Upon her ivory shoulder half declin'd<br>  In langour of enjoyment, rising wore<br>  Rosy confusion, and disorder fair [405]<br>  Transiently on her pride of motion broke.<br>  Or chance, or meaning wander'd to his face<br>  Her eye, with half command, entreating half;<br>  Haughty to all the world, but mild to him,<br>  Th' all admir'd admiring, and th' all-awing awed -- [410]<br>  She look'd on him, and trembled as she look'd.<br>  <br>  Alone she came, along she went not on.<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK II.</b> </h3>   Noon is ablaze in Heaven, but gloom, the gloom<br>  Of the brown forest's massy vault of shade,<br>  Is o'er the Kings of Britain; the broad oaks,<br>  As in protection of that conclave proud,<br>  Like some old temple's dome, with mingling shade [5]<br>  Meet overheard, around their rugged trunks<br>  Shew like fantastic pillars closely set<br>  By Druids in mysterious circle, wont<br>  Here, when the earth abroad was bright and clear<br>  With moonshine, to install their midnight rites [10]<br>  By blue nor earthly kindled fires, while Bards<br>  Pour'd more than music from their charmed harps.<br>  <br>  Each on his mossy seat, in arms that cast<br>  A glimmer which is hardly light, they sit<br>  Colossal, stern, and still; on every brow [15]<br>  Indignant sorrow and sad vengeance lowers.<br>  Them had the Pagan peasant deem'd his Gods,<br>  In cloudy wrath down stooping from the heavens<br>  To blast the mighty of mankind, and wreak<br>  On some old empire ruin and revenge. [20]<br>  <br>  And first majestical yet mild arose<br>  A lofty shape, nor less than monarch seem'd,<br>  Whose royal look from souls bold, brave, and free,<br>  Not stooping slavery claim'd, but upright awe<br>  And noble homage; yet uncrown'd, he wore [25]<br>  Dominion, him with stately reverence heard<br>  That armed Senate.  "Princes of the land,<br>  Lords of the old hereditary thrones<br>  Of Britain, we, the sons of Constantine,<br>  Emrys and Uther, come not here to charge [30]<br>  Inconstant counsel on your wisdom, nought<br>  Arraigning, that the sceptre to our line<br>  Solemnly given, in those disastrous days,<br>  When for the Empire of the Occident,<br>  For Gaul o'er-master'd, and submitted Spain, [35]<br>  Warr'd Constantine, and warring nobly fell,<br>  Ye placed in elder hand, our right foregone<br>  For the more precious public weal: oh, Chiefs,<br>  'Twas well and wisely done; a stripling's arm<br>  May rear the kingly standard in its pomp [40]<br>  To play with Zephyrs under cloudless skies,<br>  But when the rude storm shakes it ponderous folds<br>  'Twere hard for less than the consummate man<br>  Aloft to bear it, yet unstooping.  Well<br>  Stemm'd your new standard-bearer Vortigern [45]<br>  The o'ershadowing tempest, nor abas'd his front<br>  Your crown's old glories; till alas!  dire change!<br>  Dread fall!  the sceptre that ye fondly hoped,<br>  Would blossom, like the Hebrew Hierarch's rod,<br>  With the almond bloom of mercy and of love, [50]<br>  Liker the Egyptian magic-worker's wand<br>  Became a serpent, withering all your peace<br>  With its infection: then your virtues wrought<br>  Your sorrows, from your valour grew your shame.<br>  Your borders were o'erleap'd, your towns on fire, [55]<br>  And the land groan'd beneath fierce Rapine's wheels.<br>  Ye cried unto your King for arms, he sage<br>  In cold and jealous wisdom fear'd to arm,<br>  Whose arms might brave himself, and cast control<br>  On the fierce wanderings of his royal will. [60]<br>  Saxons must fight our wars, our hard-wrung gold<br>  Buy us ignoble safety, till the slaves<br>  Swell'd into Lords, and realms must pamper<br>  Our hirelings into Princes; Kent, fair Kent,<br>  The frontlet of our isle, where yet are seen [65]<br>  The graves great Cæsar peopled with his dead,<br>  When on his rear the Briton conqueror hung,<br>  Where first the Banner of the Cross was wav'd,<br>  Sinks to a Heathen province.  Warriors!  Kings!<br>  This must not be among baptized men, [70]<br>  This cannot be 'mong Britons.  Therefore here,<br>  Here in your presence dare we call again,<br>  Your throne our throne, and challenge in your love<br>  A Sovereign's title, by our youth we fell<br>  From that great height, but Vortigern hath fall'n [75]<br>  By his own guilt, we therefore rise again<br>  In majesty renew'd; he falls, no more<br>  To soar into the sacred royal seat."<br>  <br>  Thereat with concord loud, and stern acclaim,<br>  Gave answer that proud Senate, and denounc'd [80]<br>  Judgment irrevocable.  But with mien<br>  Somewhat appall'd, as one in high debate<br>  And solemn council unassay'd, arose<br>  Prince Uther; ere he spake his clanging mail<br>  Smote with fierce stroke, an audience to enchain, [85]<br>  Himself the battle sound enkindling, high<br>  His haughty brow and crested helm upflung,<br>  Thus rude his fiery eloquence pour'd forth.<br>  <br>  "Warriors of Britain!  me nor pomp of words<br>  Beseems, nor strife of smooth and liquid phrase, [90]<br>  In the debate of swords, the fray of steeds<br>  No combatant unskill'd.  I will not boast<br>  That I have brook'd with Emrys' patient pride<br>  A sceptre's loss; a boy, I wept to hear<br>  My father's crown was on a stranger's brow. [95]<br>  But when my arm gan grasp a sword, those tears<br>  Those soft unseemly waters, turn'd to hues<br>  Of burning indignation; every crown<br>  Shew'd, every kingly title to my ear<br>  Sounded a scorn and shame.  Even at his height [100]<br>  And plenitude of power I yearned to rise<br>  Against th' enthron'd Usurper -- now, oh Kings!<br>  Thus charter'd, thus commission'd, thus array'd,<br>  With what a noble phrenzy will we rush,<br>  Trampling the wreck of Saxon and of King; [105]<br>  Our path shall be as rapid and as bright<br>  As summer meteor, more pernicious, that<br>  Waning into the dull unkindling air,<br>  We burning, desolating as we pass.<br>  On, Britons, on, a tyrant fills your throne, [110]<br>  Nor fitter monument may tyrant find<br>  Than his throne's ruins; let the flat earth close<br>  O'er both at once; the stranger Saxon lord's<br>  Within our isle, the seas that bore him here,<br>  In his storm-braving navy, bear him back [115]<br>  Weltering and tossing in their drowning surge.<br>  <br>  Low'ring he stood, still in fierce act of speech,<br>  Yet speechless.  Sudden, then, in dread uproar<br>  Rose shout of war, with thundering clash of arms<br>  Mingled, then hurrying spears and nodding helms [120]<br>  With glittering tumult in the pale gloom flash'd;<br>  War, war each voice, each striken shield denounc'd.<br>  <br>  Amid the multitudinous din arose<br>  Solemnly the Bright City's Lord, down sunk<br>  Instant all tumult, broke abruptly off [125]<br>  Fierce voice and clash of arms: so mute and deep<br>  Settled the silence, the low sound was heard<br>  Of distant waterfall, the acorn drop<br>  From the green arch above.  Still and abash'd<br>  Sate the fierce conclave, while with mild reproof [130]<br>  Winning all hearts, the gracious Chieftain spake.<br>  <br>  "Brave sight for earth and heaven!  it doth not fail<br>  A nation's cry for freedom and for faith,<br>  Nor faint, nor deaden in the mist and gloom<br>  Of this low earth, it takes the morning's wings, [135]<br>  Passeth the crystal skies, and beats heaven's gate;<br>  There glideth through the gladdening Angel choirs,<br>  That fan it onward with their favouring plumes,<br>  To the eternal sapphire throne, and him<br>  That sits thereon, Ineffable.  Oh Kings, [140]<br>  Our council thus appealing may not wear<br>  Seeming of earthly passion, lust of sway,<br>  Or phrenetic vengeance: we must rise in wrath,<br>  But wear it as a mourner's robe of grief,<br>  Not as a garb of joy: must boldly strike, [145]<br>  But like the Roman, with reverted face,<br>  In sorrow to be so enforc'd.  Brave Chiefs,<br>  It would misseem a son of this proud isle,<br>  To trample on the fallen, though a King;<br>  It would misseem a Christian to rejoice [150]<br>  Where virtue hath play'd false, and fame's pure light<br>  Hath sicken'd to dishonourable gloom.<br>  Vortigern is our foe, no more our King,<br>  Yet King he hath been, King he had been still,<br>  Had never his high vaulting pride disdain'd [155]<br>  The smooth dominion of old use, nor striven<br>  To fix on our impatient necks the yoke<br>  Of foreign usurpation; our free land<br>  Will not endure the heathen Saxons rule,<br>  Nor him that rules by heathen Saxon power. [160]<br>  So march we forth in th' armour of our right,<br>  From our once King not falling off in hate<br>  Of fickleness, but by severe constraint<br>  Of duty to ourselves and to our God.<br>  So march we forth, and in such state may make [165]<br>  Our mother land to vaunt of us; raise up,<br>  Side by side, the fair airs to captivate<br>  To an approval of our upright deed,<br>  Our royal banner and the Cross of Christ;<br>  And move within their cirque of splendour, calm, [170]<br>  And yet resistless as the bright-man'd steeds<br>  That bear the Morn to disenthrone old Night.<br>  <br>  And now our kingly sceptre, forced aside,<br>  By stress and pressure of disorder'd times,<br>  Devious into an alien hand, reverts [175]<br>  To the old line; the heir of Constantine,<br>  Constans, the elder than this noble pair,<br>  Stands foremost on succession's golden roll.<br>  Nor know not I his gentle soul more apt,<br>  To listen to the soft flowing vesper hymn, [180]<br>  Than danger's spirit-stirring trump, yet deem,<br>  Thus once forewarn'd, 'tis dangerous to divert<br>  The stream of royal blood, that broken, pours<br>  Waters of bitterness and civil strife<br>  O'er th' harass'd land, and therefore thus hail I [185]<br>  Constans the King of Britain.  Speak I right?<br>  I pause, and wait, oh Chiefs, your high award."<br>  <br>  He ceased, nor time for voice or swift acclaim,<br>  Scowling a sullen laugh of scorn, leaped forth<br>  The mountain King, the Sovereign of the lakes [190]<br>  And dales this side the Caledonian bound;<br>  He only, when the Kings sate awe-struck, stood<br>  Elate with mocking pity in his frown;<br>  A mighty savage, he of God and man<br>  Alike contemptuous; nought of Christian lore [195]<br>  Knew he, yet scoff'd unknown, 'twas peaceful, meek,<br>  Thence worthless knowledge.  Him delighted more<br>  Helvellyn's cloud-wrapt brow to climb, and share<br>  The eagle's stormy solitude; 'mid wreck<br>  Of whirlwinds and dire lightnings huge he stood, [200]<br>  Where his own Gods he deem'd on volleying clouds<br>  Abroad were riding and black hurricane.<br>  Them in their misty pride assail'd he oft<br>  With impious threat, and laugh'd when th' echoing glens<br>  His wild defiance cast unanswered back. [205]<br>  Now with curl'd lip of scorn, and brow uplift,<br>  Lordly command, not counsel, fierce he spake.<br>  -- "Shame, coward shame! as though the fowls of heaven,<br>  When in dusk majesty and pride of wing<br>  Sails forth the monarch eagle, down should stoop [210]<br>  In homage to the daw.  Oh craven souls,<br>  When Snowdon or high Skiddaw's brow is bare,<br>  To plant the stately standard of revolt<br>  Upon a molehill.  Constans!  that to him<br>  Caswallon should bow down; aloft our crown [215]<br>  Upon the giddy banner staff, that rocks<br>  On Troynovant's tall citadel, uphang,<br>  And who the dizzy glory will rend down,<br>  Or Constans or Caswallon?  The bright throne<br>  Environ with grim ranks of steel-girt men: [220]<br>  Huge Saxons black with grisly scars of war,<br>  Who first will hew to that triumphal seat<br>  His ruinous path?  Hear, sceptred Britons, hear,<br>  A counsel worthy the deep thoughts of kings:<br>  Of valorous achievement and bold deeds [225]<br>  Be guerdon to the mightiest of our Isle,<br>  The Sov'reignty of Britain; spurn my voice,<br>  And I renounce your counsels, cast you off,<br>  And with my hardy vassals of the north<br>  I join the Saxon." -- Then fierce sounds again [230]<br>  Broke out, wan flames of brandish'd armour flash'd.<br>  In rude disorder and infuriate haste<br>  Sprang every warrior from his seat, as clouds<br>  Amid the sultry heaven, thunderous and vast,<br>  Gather their blackening disarray to burst [235]<br>  Upon some mountain turrets, so the chiefs<br>  Banded their fierce confusion to rush on,<br>  And whelm in his insulting pride the foe.<br>  <br>  He stood as one in joy, and lower'd a smile,<br>  With wolf-skin robe flung back, broad shield outstrech'd,<br>  And battle axe uplift: vaunting and huge [241]<br>  As fabled giant on embattled Heaven<br>  Glaring not less than utter overthrow,<br>  And total wreck; forthwith a youth rush'd out,<br>  His moony buckler high upheld to bar [245]<br>  The onset, and with voice, which youthful awe<br>  Temper'd to tone less resolute, address'd<br>  The haughty Chieftain.  "Father, deem not thou,<br>  Malwyn confederate in thy lawless thought,<br>  Mine is a Briton's soul, a Briton's sword, [250]<br>  But mortal man that seeks thy life, must pass <br>  O'er Malwyn's corpse."  Back Chief and King recoil'd,<br>  In breathless admiration.  Nobler pride,<br>  And human joy almost to softness smooth'd<br>  Caswallon's rugged brow.  "Well hast thou said, [255]<br>  Son of Caswallon, worthy of thy sire!<br>  On thine own track mount thou to fame, nor swerve<br>  For man, or more than man." -- Awhile the Kings<br>  Brief parley held, then stately and severe<br>  Rose Emrys, and pronounc'd their stern arrest. [260]<br>  <br>  "Caswallon of the Mountains, long our isle<br>  Hath mark'd thy wavering mood, now friend now foe;<br>  Now in the Caledonian inroad prompt<br>  To bear thy share in rapine, foremost now<br>  In our high councils.  This we further say, [265]<br>  We scorn thy war, Caswallon, hate thy peace,<br>  And deem it of our mercy that, unscath'd,<br>  We ban thee from our presence."  Nor reply<br>  Caswallon deign'd; calm strode he as in scorn<br>  Of wrath 'gainst foes so lowly.  Far was heard [270]<br>  His tread along the rocky path, the crash<br>  Of branches rent by his unstooping helm.<br>  They in blank wonder sate, nor wholly quell'd<br>  Wrath and insulted majesty, with look<br>  As he were still in presence fix'd, and stern. [275]<br>  Then spake Prince Emrys, "Not of trivial toil<br>  To shape the rude trunk of our enterprize<br>  To smooth perfection; deeply must we found,<br>  And strongly build the fabric of our hopes,<br>  And each must hold his charge.  Be, Samor, thine [280]<br>  To bear our brother Constans Britain's crown,<br>  In name of our assembled Kings.  Be mine<br>  From the Armoric shore, King Hoel's realm,<br>  (Our father's brother, Hoel) to embark<br>  The succours of his high-fam'd Chivalry. [285]<br>  Thou, Uther, to the West; each other King<br>  Unto his own, at signal of revolt<br>  To lead his armed Vassallage abroad."<br>  <br>  So saying, each departed; fell again<br>  The ancient silence on the solemn place. [290]<br>  <br>  Together from the forest pass'd the friends,<br>  Samor and Elidure; below their way<br>  Went wandering on through flowery meads, or sank<br>  Beneath green arches dim of beechen shade.<br>  Around the golden hills in summer wealth [295]<br>  Bask'd in the sunshine; on a river bank<br>  Long gleaming down its woodland course, repos'd<br>  Many a white hamlet: even fierce shrines of war<br>  Wore aspect mild of peace; towers dark of yore<br>  And rugged in the Roman war array, [300]<br>  With wanton ivy and gray moss o'ergrown,<br>  Their green crowns melted in the azure heavens.<br>  <br>  "Oh grief!  o'er yon fair meads and smiling lawns<br>  Must steeds of carnage batten, men of blood<br>  Their fell magnificence of murtherous pomp [305]<br>  Pavilion in yon placid groves of peace.<br>  The blood-thirst savages of wood and air,<br>  In meet abodes of wilderness and woe,<br>  Shroud their abhorred revels; the gaunt wolf<br>  Prowls gloomy o'er the wintry blasted heath; [310]<br>  Brood desolate on some bare mountain peak<br>  Raven and screaming vulture.  Man, fell man,<br>  Envious of bliss he scorns, 'mid haunts of peace,<br>  Spots fair and blissful, the rare stars of earth,<br>  Plays ever his foul game of spoil and death, [315]<br>  Ruthless, then vaunts himself Creation's pride,<br>  Supreme o'er all alone in deeds of blood."<br>  <br>  Thus Elidure; him Samor, from deep trance<br>  Wakening, addrest: "Soft man of peace, my prayer<br>  Would ask of heaven no theatre of strife [320]<br>  Save yon fair plain, there forth the weak would start<br>  In the tumultuous valour of despair,<br>  The timorous proudly tower in scorn of death:<br>  There, where each tree, each dell, each grassy knoll,<br>  Lovely from memory of some past delight, [325]<br>  Is kindred to the soul; his house of prayer,<br>  The altar of his bridal vow, the font<br>  Of his sweet infants baptism, kindred all,<br>  Holiest and last, his fathers peaceful graves:<br>  Oh, were all Britain, like yon beauteous plain, [330]<br>  Blissful and free, that angels there might walk<br>  Forgetful of their heavenly bowers of light,<br>  Friend of my boyhood, these all-conquering foes,<br>  Who fetter the free winds, and ride the sea<br>  Kinglike, their menacing prows would turn aloof, [335]<br>  And bitterly, in baffled lust of prey,<br>  Curse the proud happiness that mock'd their might."<br>  <br>  Lo, ere he paus'd, gay files of dazzling light<br>  Slow o'er the plain advancing, indistinct<br>  From their full brightness, gradual the long blaze [340]<br>  Broke into form, and lance and bow and helm,<br>  Standard and streamer, chariot and fair steed,<br>  Start from the mingled splendour.  On their height<br>  Unseen, the Chieftains watch'd the winding pomp.<br>  And all before the azure-vested Bards [345]<br>  From glancing instruments shook bridal glee.<br>  Then came the gorgeous chariots, rough with gold,<br>  And steeds their proud heads nodding with rich weight<br>  Of frontlet wreathed with flowers and shadowy plumes;<br>  Therein sate ladies robed in costly state, [350]<br>  Each like a Queen; the noble charioteers,<br>  Briton in garb, with purple mantle loose,<br>  O'er steel, in network bright, or scale o'er scale,<br>  Glittering, and aventayle barr'd close and firm,<br>  As yet the gaudy traitors shamed to meet [355]<br>  The cold keen glance of countryman betray'd.<br>  Dark in their iron arms, some wildly girt<br>  With Caledonian spoils, their yellow hair<br>  Down from the casque in broad luxuriant flow<br>  Spreading, and lofty banner wide display'd, [360]<br>  Whereon a milk-white courser reinless shone,<br>  Pace forth the Saxon warriors.  High o'er all,<br>  Tempestuous Horsa, chafing his hot steed,<br>  And Hengist with his wreath of amber beads, [Note 4]<br>  His hoary strength, in spite of age or toil, [365]<br>  A tower of might: with that tall grove of spears,<br>  Circled, and rampire close of serried shields,<br>  The bridegroom Monarch rode, his bright attire<br>  Peaceful, as fitting nuptial pomp, his robe<br>  Rich-floating strew'd the earth with purple shade, [370]<br>  And on his lofty brow a regal crown,<br>  Bright as a wreath of sunbeams; high his arm<br>  The ivory sceptre bore of kingly sway:<br>  Yet who his mien and bearing watch'd had seen<br>  Dim gleam of jealous steel, or lurking mail [375]<br>  Beneath those glorious trappings, for his gaze,<br>  Now jocund, chang'd anon to wandering stare,<br>  Fearful and wild, as the still air were rife<br>  With vengeful javelins showering death, his pace<br>  Hurried, yet tardy, as of one who rides [380]<br>  O'er land still tottering with an earthquake shock.<br>  <br>  And him beside, on snowy palfrey, deck'd<br>  With silver bells its pendant mane profuse,<br>  Of silver and of stainless ermelin<br>  The bright caparisons, and all her robes [385]<br>  White as of woven lily cups, the Bride<br>  Majestic rode as on a moving throne.<br>  Her sunbright hair she wav'd, and smil'd around,<br>  As though, of less than kingly Paramour<br>  Scornful, she said, Lo, Britain, through your land [390]<br>  I lead the enthralled Sovereign of your isle.<br>  Yet so surpassing fair, brief instant wish'd<br>  Those wrathful Briton Chiefs their leafy screen<br>  A thin transparent cloud: of his high charge<br>  Brief while forgetful, Samor stood entranced, [395]<br>  Fearing her form should fleet too swift away.<br>  <br>  Came it from earth or air, yon savage shape,<br>  His garb, if garb it be, of shaggy hair<br>  Close folding o'er his dusky limbs, his locks<br>  And waving matted beard like cypress boughs [400]<br>  On bleak heath swaying to the midnight storm?<br>  Came he from yon deep wood?  On the light spray<br>  No leaf is stirring.  On the winged winds<br>  Rode he?  No breeze awakes the noontide air.<br>  'Mid that arm'd throng, dismaying, undismay'd [405]<br>  With a strange eye dilated, as unus'd<br>  To common sights of earth, and voice that seem'd<br>  Rarely to hold discourse with human ears,<br>  "Joy," and again, and thrice he uttered "Joy."<br>  Cower'd Horsa on his palsied steed; aghast, [410]<br>  As toiling to despise that thing he fear'd,<br>  Sate Hengist.  "Joy to Bridegroom and to Bride!<br>  Why should not man rejoice, and earth be glad?<br>  Beyond the sphere of man, the round of earth,<br>  There's loud rejoicing, 'tis not in the heavens! [415]<br>  And many ministrant Angels shake their wings<br>  In gladness, wings that are not plum'd with light.<br>  The dead are jocund, not the dead in bliss.<br>  Your couch is blest -- by all whose blessings blast,<br>  All things unlovely gratulate your love. [420]<br>  I see the nuptial pomp, the nuptial song<br>  I hear, and full the pomp, for Hate, and Fear,<br>  And excellent Dishonour, and bright Shame,<br>  And rose-cheek'd Grief, and jovial Discontent,<br>  And that majestic herald, Infamy, [425]<br>  And that high noble, Servitude, are there,<br>  A blithesome troop, a gay and festive crew.<br>  And the Land's curses are the bridal hymn;<br>  Sweetly and shrilly doth th' accordant Isle<br>  Imprecate the glad Hymenean song. [430]<br>  So joy again, I say, to Britain's King,<br>  That taketh to his bosom Britain's fate,<br>  Her beautiful destruction to his bed.<br>  And joy to Britain's Queen, who bears her Lord<br>  So bright a dow'ry and profuse, long years [435]<br>  Of war and havoc, and fair streams of blood,<br>  And plenteous ruin, loss of crown and fame,<br>  And full perdition of the immortal soul;<br>  So thrice again I utter 'joy,' 'joy,' 'joy!'"<br>  <br>  Then upsprung spear to strike, and bicker'd bow; [440]<br>  Ere spear could strike, or shaft could fly, the path<br>  Was bare and vacant; shape nor sound remain'd;<br>  Only the voice of Vortigern moan'd out,<br>  "Merlin;" -- and on the long procession past.<br>  <br>  Down in a quiet dale, where beechen groves [445]<br>  With interchanging gold and glossy green<br>  O'ermantled the smooth slopes, that fell around<br>  Like a fair amphitheatre, beneath<br>  A brook went wand'ring through fresh meadow banks,<br>  With a cool summer dashing, here the Chiefs [450]<br>  The royal Hermit met, his gentle brow<br>  Smooth as a slumbering Angel's plumes (effaced<br>  All traces of this rude and wearing earth,<br>  All brands of fiery passions, wild desires)<br>  Wore that calm holiness the sainted dead [455]<br>  Smile on the visions of their lov'd on earth:<br>  His life was like a sleep, with heavenly sights,<br>  And harmonies, as of angelic sounds<br>  Visited ever, nor his barren heart<br>  Touch'd not the light affections, trembled not [460]<br>  His spirit with loves fervent swell, but all<br>  Most wont to bear man's soul to earth, round him<br>  As the thin morning clouds around the lark,<br>  Gather'd, to float him upward to the heavens.<br>  <br>  They at his feet down laid the kingly crown, [465]<br>  Fulfill'd their lofty mission.  He, the while,<br>  With that mild sadness he had watch'd the leaves<br>  Drip from the sere autumnal bough, survey'd<br>  Its stately glittering.  "Men of earth, why mock,<br>  With gaudy pageantry, and titled pomp, [470]<br>  The frail and transient pilgrims of this world.<br>  The fading flag-flower on yon streamlet brink,<br>  Were garland meeter for our mortal brows<br>  Than yon rich blaze of gems." "Prince," Samor spake<br>  "Sweet is it down the silent vale of life [475]<br>  To glide away, of all but Heaven forgot,<br>  Forgetting all but Heaven.  Of king-born men,<br>  Lords of mankind, high delegates of Heaven,<br>  Loftier the doom, their rare prerogative<br>  The luxury of conferring bliss.  Oh, Prince, [480]<br>  Not by the stream to slumber, not to waste<br>  Idly in joyous dreams the drowsy hours,<br>  Hath Heaven thy kingly heritage ordain'd:<br>  Set badge of Empery on thy brow: of God<br>  The noblest service is to serve mankind, [485]<br>  To save a nation all a mortal's power,<br>  To imitate the Saviour of the world."<br>  <br>  Calm answer'd Constans, "Earth's exalted fame,<br>  Grandeurs and glories gleam upon my soul<br>  Like wintery sun-light on a plain of snow. [490]<br>  With prayers, a Hermit's arms, I aid your cause,<br>  Farewell.  Why pause ye, as to question more<br>  The wisdom of my choice -- lo, yon fair orb;<br>  How spotless the fine azure where he holds<br>  His secret palace, knows not his pure light [495]<br>  A stain of dimness, till th'abode of men<br>  Pours o'er it its infectious mists." "Oh, Prince,<br>  'Tis not the glory of that peerless light,<br>  The barren glittering, the unfruitful waste<br>  Of splendour on the still inanimate skies, [500]<br>  It is the life, the motion, and the joy<br>  It breathes along this world of man, the broad<br>  Munificence of blessing that awakes,<br>  And in its rapturous gratitude springs up,<br>  To glorify its bounteous source of pride." [505]<br>  <br>  "I see thy brow at thine own words on fire;<br>  Mine, Samor, yet is calm and cold."  "Dost thou,<br>  Constans, all title, claim, and right renounce<br>  To Britain's throne?" "Even free as I renounce<br>  The everlasting enemy of man." [510]<br>  "Will thy voice mingle with the general cry,<br>  'Long live King Emrys?'" -- "Long may Emrys live,<br>  Even the eternal life beyond the grave."<br>  <br>  "Yet one word more; 'tis perilous in the storm<br>  For the tall pine, nor less, in evil days, [515]<br>  For the high born and exalted of the state.<br>  The Saxon blood-hounds are abroad for prey,<br>  Seek thou some quiet solitude remote<br>  Beyond their prowling range." -- His arm to Heaven<br>  Slowly uplifted, "Will they reach me there?" [520]<br>  Spake the meek Hermit, "there is rest secure."<br>  <br>  They parted; gentle Elidure alone,<br>  Lingering with somewhat of an envious gaze,<br>  View'd the deep quiet of that placid dell.<br>  <br>  That night were seen along the dusky wood, [525]<br>  Of more than human stature moving forms,<br>  Pale faces circled with black iron helms,<br>  Not of the Briton shape their garb or arms;<br>  Stealthy their pace and slow; the peasants thought<br>  Demons of evil that sad night had power, [530]<br>  And pray'd Heaven's grace to guard the saintly man.<br>  <br>  At morn roved forth the peasant, down the dale<br>  His dog went bounding to the Hermit's cell,<br>  For all mute creatures loved the man of God.<br>  A quick and desolate moaning nearer call'd [535]<br>  The peasant; in officious grief the dog<br>  Stood licking the cold hand that drooping hung<br>  Lifeless; the mild composure of his brow<br>  On the cross rested; praying he had died,<br>  And his cold features yet were smiling prayer. [540]<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK III.</b> </h3>   Orient the bright-hair'd Charioteer of heaven<br>  Pour'd daylight from his opal wheels, and struck<br>  From the blue pavement of the sky clear flakes<br>  Of azure light upon the Eastern sea.<br>  And as the gray mists slowly curl'd away, [5]<br>  Rose the white cliffs of Kent, like palace fair,<br>  On fane of snowy marble, to enshrine<br>  Blue Amphitrite, or the Sea-Gods old<br>   Of Pagan mariner.  Rode tall below<br>  The Saxon navy, as from midnight sleep [10]<br>  Wakening; the gray sails in the breeze of morn<br>  'Gan tremble, gleaming oars flash in the spray.<br>  The Sea Kings on the beach in parley stern<br>  Were met, nor less than nation's doom and fate<br>  Of kingdoms in their voice.  Lo, in the midst [15]<br>  Stood huge Caswallon, words of mild salute<br>  Deign'd not, but thus addrest the Ocean Lord.<br>  <br>  "Saxon!  that o'er this fair and princely isle<br>  Thou would'st win empire by the sword of war,<br>  I marvel not, arraign not -- 'tis a dream, [20]<br>  Noble as o'er the heavens to walk abroad,<br>  Companion of yon bright majestic sun.<br>  Now by my glory, Saxon, mortal peer<br>  Never Caswallon brook'd, save thee alone,<br>  Thee, rival in his race of pride and power. [25]<br>  Arm'd with myself and all th' embattled North,<br>  Not Roman Britons, sons of sires who dash'd<br>  The purple Conquerors' haughty wall to earth,<br>  And trampled their strewn ramparts; who ne'er deign'd<br>  Barter for gaudy robe and marble pile,<br>  Fierce naked freedom, and wild mountain cave,<br>  Will I, and thou with Saxon spears begirt,<br>  Bow this fair Britain to our lordly sway.<br>  Then will we two, from pale perplexed earth<br>  Seen, like twin meteors battling in high heaven, [35]<br>  On some lone eminence wage glorious strife,<br>  Sole empire meed of conquest, of defeat<br>  Utter annihilation, dark and full<br>  Solace, and lofty comfort."  Bold he paus'd,<br>  Nor Hengist with pale sign of awe or dread [40]<br>  Shamed the proud peerage, but with hardy speech<br>  Guileful, won faith by seeming scorn of guile.<br>  <br>  "Briton, to dare high deeds, and to disown,<br>  Argues a wavering valour; the firm soul<br>  Vaunts resolute its lofty dangerous scope. [45]<br>  To us our Gods o'er ocean and its shores<br>  Kingly dominion and wide sway have given;<br>  Were insult to our might and base reproach,<br>  The freedom of one sea-girt isle, to thee<br>  Honouring, not fearing, 'mid our prime we grant [50]<br>  Transcendent state, and eminence of power.<br>  Now speed we of th' immortal Powers in Heaven,<br>  Our high ominiscient Fathers, to demand<br>  If on the eternal shield of fate be graven<br>  Ruin or Conquest, ere to bold emprize [55]<br>  We gird our brazen arms." -- "Of mighty men<br>  The Gods are mighty, whom the Saxon fears,<br>  The paramount of men, 'twere rash to scorn,<br>  No calm and sunshine deities of peace." --<br>  <br>  So spake Caswallon, the mild faith of Christ [60]<br>  Scoffing with covert mockery; thus th' All Wise<br>  The imaginations of the proud on earth<br>  Silent endures, till some brief point of time<br>  Crumbles the high-built insolence of years.<br>  <br>  "Wilt thou behold our Gods?" fierce Horsa cried [65]<br>  "Then mount the bark, abroad her wings are spread,<br>  And fleet along the obedient deep she speeds.<br>  Fear not, proud Briton." -- "Fear!" Caswallon cried;<br>  All iron as he stood, o'er surf, surge, wave<br>  He bounded, hollow rang his heavy arms, [70]<br>  The bark her tall side to the troubling waves<br>  Stoop'd groaning, nor delay'd the Ocean King.<br>  <br>  "Brother, farewell!  not singly the bold wolf<br>  Scatters the mountain herd; in grim repose<br>  He rests expectant of his kindred troop, [75]<br>  Numberless from their shaggy dens they sweep,<br>  And spacious o'er the antler'd monarch's realm<br>  Spreads the wide ravage of their muster'd might."<br>  <br>  Stern Horsa bow'd assent, yet paus'd to watch<br>  The proud bark tilting o'er the azure plain. [80]<br>  Stately she rode her path of light, her sails<br>  In dalliance with the courteous winds: bold Man!<br>  Well may thy full heart bound: in earth and air<br>  The thunder-maned steed, the eagle thron'd<br>  In the pavilion of his plumes, stand forth [85]<br>  Creation's glories; but the noblest shape<br>  That walks the deep thy workmanship sublime<br>  Owneth, and starts from thee to life.  Vaunt thou,<br>  Yet humbly vaunt, all greatness is from God.<br>  <br>  What dolphin glancing in his silver sport, [90]<br>  More graceful with translucent pinion parts<br>  The liquid azure?  what Leviathan,<br>  Huge heaving on the thick Norwegian foam,<br>  More lordly than the white-wing'd bark, that wafts<br>  The Sea King o'er his empire?  the fair waves [95]<br>  Rise in their gamesome turbulence, and pay<br>  Wild homage to that royal Mariner.<br>  <br>  The motion and the murmur of the deep,<br>  The rushing of the silent, solemn sky,<br>  Each in its deep abyss and pure expanse, [100]<br>  Seeming its secret mysteries of might,<br>  Its ruling soul of everlasting change,<br>  To veil from mortal knowledge, ever pour<br>  O'er savage ev'n and rude tumultuous awe,<br>  And exultation of a pleasing dread. [105]<br>  From dizzy notions of infinity,<br>  Vague sense of ever-during sights and sounds,<br>  Inactive though the body, the free spirit,<br>  Vagrant along the illimitable void,<br>  Perils uncouth and rich uncertainties [110]<br>  Ranges in restless round, plucks treasures rare,<br>  That gem the caverns of the hoary deep,<br>  Or bathes with sea-maids in their crystal bowers,<br>  Or with gay creatures and fantastical<br>  Peoples some dreamy land; such joys of old [115]<br>  Lured the fierce Saxon from his darksome woods,<br>  To launch along the vast and barren sea.<br>  Such joys through this long voyage, wean'd brief while<br>  From thoughts of war and war-won empire wide,<br>  Haughty Caswallon, or from him assum'd [120]<br>  Fierce aspect, and a battailous character.<br>  <br>  'Twas midnight, but a rich unnatural dawn<br>  Sheets the fir'd Arctic heaven; forth springs an arch,<br>  O'erspanning with a crystal pathway pure<br>  The starry sky, as though for Gods to march, [125]<br>  With show of heavenly warfare daunting earth,<br>  To that wild revel of the northern clouds;<br>  That now with broad and bannery light distinct,<br>  Stream in their restless wavings to and fro,<br>  While the sea billows gleam them mellower back; [130]<br>  Anon like slender lances bright upstart,<br>  And clash and cross with hurtle and with flash,<br>  Tilting their airy tournament." -- "Brave signs,"<br>  Cried Hengist; "lo, our Gods their standards rear,<br>  And with glad omen of immortal strife [135]<br>  Salute our high-wing'd purpose." -- "Yea (return'd<br>  Caswallon) from mine own Helvellyn's brow,<br>  Never a brighter conflict in the skies<br>  Taught me that war was dear in Heaven: dream ye<br>  Of tamer faith in gentle Southern skies [140]<br>  Your smooth and basking deities, our North<br>  Wooes not with tender hues and sunny smiles<br>  Soft worship, but emblazons all the air<br>  With semblance of celestial strife, unveils<br>  To us of their empyreal halls the pomp, [145]<br>  The secret majesty of godlike war."<br>  <br>  Oh Lord of Lords!  incessant thus assail'd<br>  That Pagan with his frantic railings Thee,<br>  Th' Ineffable, yet worshipp'd of thy power<br>  A faint and pale effect, reflection dim [150]<br>  From thy soul-blinding glories.  On they sail'd,<br>  Till o'er the dark deep now the wintry winds<br>  Swept on their murky pinions, huge and high<br>  The liquid legions of the main arose;<br>  Like snow upon the sable pines, the foam [155]<br>  Hung hoary on their towered fronts; but slow,<br>  Like a triumphant warrior, their bold bark<br>  Wore onward, now upon the loftiest height<br>  Shaking its streamers gay defiance, now<br>  With brave devotion to the prone abyss [160]<br>  Down rushing, but the sternest Saxon cheek<br>  Put not to shame the dauntless Landsman; he<br>  In the strong passion of a new delight<br>  On the fierce tumult feasts, and almost grieves,<br>  When now beneath the haven rocks embayed, [165]<br>  The angry waves seem wearying to repose,<br>  And the slack sails slow droop their flagging folds.<br>  <br>  Their port was southward of that Strait, where bursts<br>  The Baltic, with her massy waves of ice<br>  Encumbering far and wide the Northern main. [170]<br>  <br>  South, North, and East, the rapid heralds speed,<br>  Summoning from fen or forest, moor or wild,<br>  Britain!  on thee to banquet, all who bathe<br>  In Weser, Elbe, or Rhine, their saffron locks,<br>  Hertog and Erle and King; the huntsman bold [175]<br>  Of bear, or bison, o'er the quaking moss,<br>  Or grim Vikinger, who but sues his Gods<br>  For tempests, so upon some wealthy coast<br>  Bursts unforeseen his midnight frigate fierce,<br>  And freights its greedy hold with amplest spoil. [180]<br>  <br>  And now have Hengist and Caswallon climb'd<br>  The chariot of the Oracle; no wheels<br>  Bear that strange car; like wind along the sea,<br>  It glides along the rapid rein deer's track.<br>  Beauteous those gentle rein deer arch'd their necks, [185]<br>  And cast their palmy antlers back, and spread<br>  Their broad red nostrils to the wind; they hear<br>  Old Hengist's voice, like arrows down the wind,<br>  Like shot-stars through the welkin start they forth.<br>  The car slides light, the deer bound fleet: they pass [190]<br>  Dark leagues of pine and fir, the filmy light,<br>  Shivering with every motion of the wind<br>  On their brown path lies tremulous, o'er them sails,<br>  Heard through the dismal foliage hissing shrill,<br>  And hoarser groaning of the swaying boughs, [195]<br>  The funeral descant of the ominous birds.<br>  Around them the prophetic milk white steeds, [Note 5]<br>    Their necks yet virgin of the taming curb,<br>  With all their loose long glories, arch, and pass<br>  In solemn silence, and regardless paw [200]<br>  The unechoing earth.  But that old German, set<br>  Inflexible with bolder hand to draw<br>  The veil of dusk futurity, disdains<br>  These tamer omens.  Still the car slides light,<br>  The deer bound fleet, they pause not, save to quaff [205]<br>  The narrow cruise, to share their scanty store.<br>  Like swallows o'er the glassy rivers smooth,<br>  O'er the pellucid lake, with glittering breast<br>  Yet wrinkled with its rippling waves, they skim,<br>  The dead unstirring ocean bears them on, [210]<br>  Amid the immortal ice-hills wind they now.<br>  <br>  In restless change, God's softer summer works<br>  Glitter and fade, are born and die, but these,<br>  Endiadem'd by undissolving snows,<br>  High Potentates of winter's drear domain, [215]<br>  Accumulate their everlasting bulk,<br>  Eternal and imperishable, stand<br>  Amid Creation's swift inconstant round,<br>  In majesty of silence undisturb'd,<br>  Save when from their long-menacing brows they shake<br>  The ruining Avalanche; unvisited [221]<br>  By motion, but of sailing clouds, when sleets<br>  From their unwasting granary barb their darts,<br>  And the grim North-wind loads his rimy wings.<br>  Nor trace of man, save many a fathom deep, [225]<br>  Haply dark signs of some tall people strange,<br>  That walk'd the infant earth, may shroud profound<br>  Their legends inaccessible.  They soar<br>  In headlong precipice, or pyramid<br>  Linking the earth and heaven, to which the piles [230]<br>  Where those Egyptian despots rot sublime,<br>  Or even that frantic Babylonian tower,<br>  Were frivolous domes for laughter and for scorn.<br>  <br>  Nor wants soft interchange of vale, where smiles<br>  White mimicry of foliage and thin flower. [235]<br>  Feathery and fanlike spreads the leafy ice,<br>  With dropping cup, and roving tendril loose,<br>  As though the glassy dews o'er flower and herb<br>  Their silken moisture had congeal'd, and yet<br>  Within that slender veil their knots profuse [240]<br>  Blossom'd and blush'd with tender life, the couch<br>  Less various where the fabled Zephyr fans<br>  With his mild wings his Flora's bloomy locks;<br>  But colourless and cold, these flowering vales<br>  Seem meeter for decrepit Winter's head [245]<br>  To lie in numb repose.  The car slides light,<br>  The deer bound fleet, the long gray wilderness<br>  Hath something of a roseate glimmering dim,<br>  And widens still its pale expanse: when lo,<br>  A light of azure, wavering to display [250]<br>  No sights, no shapes of darkness and of fear.<br>  Tremblingly flash'd the inconstant meteor light,<br>  Shewing thin forms, like virgins of this earth,<br>  Save that all signs of human joy or grief,<br>  The flush of passion, smile or tear had seem'd [255]<br>  On the fix'd brightness of each dazzling cheek,<br>  Strange and unnatural: statues not unlike<br>  By nature, in fantastic mood congeal'd<br>  From purest snow, the fair of earth to shame,<br>  Surpassing beauteous: breath of mortal life [260]<br>  Heaved not their bosoms, and no rosy blood<br>  Tinged their full veins, yet mov'd they, and their steps<br>  Were harmony.  But three of that bright troop,<br>  The loveliest and the wildest, stood aloof,<br>  Enwrapt by what in human form were like [265]<br>  Impulse divine, of their fine nature seem'd<br>  The eternal instinct.  Them no less survey'd<br>  Caswallon with the knitted brow of scorn,<br>  Bitter he spake -- "No marvel Saxon souls<br>  Revel in war's delights, so stern, so fierce [270]<br>  Their deities."  Severe with wrath supprest,<br>  As one ill-brooking that irreverent mirth<br>  Scoff'd the wild lore, himself ne'er dar'd to doubt,<br>  Answer'd the Son of Woden. "These, proud Chief,<br>  So snowy, soft, and airy gentle, these [275]<br>  Are ministers of destiny and death,<br>  The viewless Riders of the battle field:<br>  When sounds the rushing of their sable steeds,<br>  Down sink the summon'd mighty, and expand<br>  Valhalla's cloudy portals; to their thrones [280]<br>  They the triumphant strangers lead, and pour<br>  Lavish the eternal beverage of the Gods.<br>  Mark thou yon bright-hair'd three?  and would thy soul<br>  Grasp the famed deeds of ancient time, or know<br>  The master spirits of our present world. [285]<br>  Lo Gudur, she whose deep mysterious soul<br>  Treasureth the past, and Rosta, who beholds<br>  All acts and agents of this living earth;<br>  She too is there before whose spacious sight<br>  The years that have not been start up and live, [290]<br>  Who reads within the soul of man unborn<br>  The unimagin'd purpose, of the sage<br>  Skulda the sagest.  Ask and thou shalt know."<br>  -- "I am not King of Britain, have not been,<br>  Hateful the present and the past, my soul [295]<br>  Thirsteth for what shall be." -- The Hengist spake<br>  In tone of mix'd authority and prayer,<br>  "Queen of the Future, Valkyr, hear and speak,<br>  Speak to the Son of Woden." -- All the troop<br>  Instant the thin bright air absorb'd, alone [300]<br>  Stood Skulda with her white hair waving wide,<br>  As trembling on the verge of palpable being,<br>  Ready to languish too in light away.<br>  <br>  "O'er Britain's isle doth Woden to his sons<br>  Give empire?" She, but in no human tone, [305]<br>  E'er from the soul's emotion harsh or soft,<br>  One glittering rich unvarying tone replied,<br>  "To thine, but not to thee." -- And, "I am thine,"<br>  Caswallon shouted loud, and sternly shook<br>  His visionary sceptre.  "Whence the foe [310]<br>  Fatal to Hengist, and to Hengist's sway?"<br>  "Not from the Mountain, Saxon, from the Vale."<br>  Heard, heeded not the Mountain Chief that strain<br>  Dire and ill-boding, or if heard, disdain'd [314]<br>  Adverse what prosperous seem'd a voice from Heaven.<br>  <br>  "By what rich rite," he cried, "may Briton Chief<br>  Win favour from high Woden?" -- "Not the blood<br>  Of steed or stag; a flower of earth must fade.<br>  Blest o'er all virgins of the earth, the chaste,<br>  The beautiful, by Heaven ordain'd to lead [320]<br>  The souls of valiant men to the pale hall<br>  Of the Immortal; air her path, and Heaven<br>  Her dwelling, with the fair and brave of earth<br>  Her sole communion?" -- "By my future throne,<br>  Proud office for the daughter of a King! [325]<br>  A royal damsel, mine own blood, shall join<br>  Your cloudy mysteries." -- A hue like joy<br>  Overspread all her face and form, while slow<br>  Into the air she brighten'd, indistinct<br>  Even now, and now invisible.  Sad seem'd [330]<br>  In gloomy converse with his own dark mind<br>  Old Hengist, nor despair'd that bold of soul,<br>  In pride of human wisdom to revoke<br>  The irrevocable, what himself deem'd fate,<br>  By force or fraud t' o'ermaster or elude. [335]<br>  <br>  O glorious eminence of virtuous fame,<br>  Glorious from peril!  Warriors of the Vales,<br>  Fate-signal'd Samor, vaunt not thou the love<br>  Of a blind people, or weak prince: thy boast<br>  The sworn unerring hate of Britain's foe. [340]<br>  <br>  So pass'd they forth, one in wild joy elate,<br>  Already in his high disdainful thought<br>  Wielding supremacy; each of fix'd fate<br>  Nought heeding, but what fed his fierce desires.<br>  <br>  The car slides light, the deer bound fleet, nor sun [345]<br>  Nor star in all the hazy heavens.  Snow, snow,<br>  Above, around, beneath.  Unblinded yet,<br>  Drive on the kingly charioteers, and shake<br>  The showery plumage from their locks; fast fades<br>  The long pale plain, the giant ice-hills sink, [350]<br>  Lakes, rivers, seas are patient of their speed,<br>  Huge, dim, and dusk the forest pines rush back,<br>  Now pant the brown deer by the ocean bay.<br>  <br>  How desolate are now thy unplough'd waves,<br>  Dark Baltic!  wandering Elbe, thy icy breast [355]<br>  How silent of thy hunters.  Sleep thou calm<br>  Amid thy wanton vineyards, Gaul!  no more<br>  The blue-eyed Plunderers, bridging thy broad Rhine,<br>  Waste thy inebriate harvests clustering pride.<br>  Sing songs of joy, soft Italy!  o'er thee [360]<br>  But Alaric and Attila drive on<br>  Their chariot wheels of conquest, this their peer<br>  In majesty of havoc, in renown<br>  Of devastation, this, the fiercer third<br>  Of human Furies, scap'st thou, therefore sing, [365]<br>  Soft Italy; for lo, at Hengist's call,<br>  Vast Germany dispeoples her wide realm,<br>  Deserts to silence and the beasts of game<br>  Her long and soundless forests.  Seems the North<br>  The forge of Nations, in one fleet t' exhaust [370]<br>  Her iron wealth of warriors; helmed high<br>  The Suevian with his towery knotted locks, [Note 6]<br>  Frisian and Scandinavian, Cimbrian rich<br>  In ancient vauntage of his sires, who clomb<br>  The Alpine snows, and shook free Rome with dread. [375]<br>  And others nameless, numberless, sweep forth<br>  Their bands; but three almost in nations came:<br>  The Jute, the Anglian, and the Saxon, each<br>  Leaving earth bare for many a lonesome league,<br>  His wives, his children, and his Gods embarks, [380]<br>  On the fierce quest of peril and of power.<br>  <br>  Then forth arose each Chieftain to salute<br>  The polestar of their baleful galaxy,<br>  Prime Architect of ruin: him who sway'd<br>  Their hot marauding, desultory strife [385]<br>  To cool and steady warfare, of their limbs<br>  The domineering soul.  As each past on<br>  Shook up the Scald his harsh-strung shell, and cast<br>  The war tones of each nation to the winds;<br>  And Hengist with imperious flattery met [390]<br>  Each tall and titled Leader: "Art thou here,<br>  Bold Frisian Hermengard!  a broader isle<br>  And fairer than thy azure Rhine laves round,<br>  Spreads for thee her green vallies.  How brook'st thou,<br>  Strong Scandinavian Lodbrog, thou the Chief [395]<br>  Of the renown'd Vikinger, while the waves<br>  So nobly riot with the wintry storms,<br>  The tame and steadfast land?  Now freely leap,<br>  Arngrim, along thy Suevian forests brown<br>  The bear and foam-tusk'd wild boar; let them leap, [400]<br>  A braver game is up on Britain's shore.<br>  O Cedric, gray in glory, young in power,<br>  The Drave ran purple with thy boyish deeds,<br>  A darker, redder dye, o'er silver Thames<br>  Shall spread before thy ancient battle axe. [405]<br>  Ho, Offa, the rich-flowing mead hath worn<br>  Your Jutland cups, beneath the British helms<br>  Capacious goblets smooth and fair await<br>  Offa's carousals.  Heir of Cimbric fame, [Note 7]<br>  Frotho, how these, of late the Roman's slaves, [410]<br>  Will the race daunt, who set our Thor afront<br>  The Roman's Capitolian Jove.  And thou,<br>  My gold-hair'd brother, are the British maids,<br>  Or British warriors, Abisa, the first<br>  In the fierce yearnings of thy boyish soul? [415]<br>  And lo the mighty Anglian; oh, unfold<br>  Ocean more wide, more wealthy realms, too brief,<br>  Too narrow for Argantyr's fame, the round<br>  Of this the choice, the Sovereign of thine isles.<br>  <br>  Thereat a sound of clattering shields arose, [420]<br>  As all the rocks around with one harsh rift<br>  Had rent asunder: "Fair must be the land,<br>  And brave the conquest, plenteous the renown,<br>  Where Hengist leads strong Woden's sceptred sons!"<br>  <br>  But inly laugh'd Caswallon, as he long'd [325]<br>  With each or all to match his Briton strength;<br>  On the prophetic Valkyr thought, and glanced<br>  Proud pity on the legends of their praise.<br>  <br>  Advanced Argantyr, his bold grasp apart,<br>  As peer his peer, led Hengist.  "Thou and I, [430]<br>  Saxon, must have our compact; dark I know<br>  Thy paths of strife, while my frank valour loves<br>  The broad bright sunshine; thou by sleight and art<br>  Min'st thy slow conquest; I with naked sword<br>  Affront my peril, till its menacing height [435]<br>  Bow to the dust before me; for bold war,<br>  For noonday battling, tender I mine arm,<br>  But no allegiance own to subtle craft;<br>  To peace Argantyr doth revolt when thou<br>  Array'st stern war in the smooth garb of guile." [440]<br>  "The weak, Argantyr, and the friendless, need<br>  Such politic skill; I take thee at thy word.<br>  Who skulks a fox when he dare prowl a wolf?<br>  Power charters force, where strong Argantyr stands<br>  Is power. -- And now aboard, brave Chiefs, aboard, [445]<br>  Or the soft spring o'ertakes our tardy keels,<br>  And with her slothful breezes smooths the skies."<br>  <br>  Wonderous that ocean armament; in shoals<br>  Ride boat and bark, innumerous as the waves<br>  That show white slender streaks of foam between [450]<br>  Their tawny sides, save here and there towers up<br>  Some statelier admiral in lordly height<br>  O'er the frail comm'nalty, whose limber ribs<br>  Are the light wicker, cased with sturdy hides<br>  Their level bottoms smooth. [Note 8]  Oh, that frail Man, [455]<br>  Loose-woven frame of dissolube stuff,<br>  Uncharter'd from the boisterous license rude<br>  Of pitiless winds and fierce unfetter'd waves,<br>  To that unshackled libertine, wild Chance,<br>  Amenable, unguaranteed from burst [460]<br>  And inroad of invading surge, that he,<br>  With such thin barrier between life and death,<br>  Should sit and skim along the ocean waste,<br>  Careless as maiden in a flowery field;<br>  Valour or phrenzy is it?  They their toil [465]<br>  Ply nimbly, and with gallant oar chastise<br>  The insurgent billows, their despotic sails<br>  Lords o'er the wild democracy of air.<br>  <br>  Less vast, and mann'd with tamer, feebler spirits,<br>  In later days, against our Virgin Queen, [470]<br>  The Spaniard's mad Armada; but the flag<br>  Of Howard, and the Almighty's stormy hand,<br>  Belied their braggard baptism, so they won<br>  Brave conquest!  graves in ocean's barren caves,<br>  Or on the whirlpool-girded Orcades. [475]<br>  <br>  But onward rides that Pagan fleet: young Spring<br>  Hath scarcely tipt the leafless woods with green;<br>  Tyne's jetty tide is blanch'd with German oars.<br>  <br>  Now wither with that dark-brow'd priest set forth<br>  Old Hengist and the Briton Mountain Lord? [480]<br>  Is it, fell Hengist, that Caswallon's name<br>  Paragon thine in British hate, close link'd<br>  By fellowship in nameless rites accurst,<br>  Be hence more deeply, execrably thine?<br>  Or, from weak credence in such impious Gods, [485]<br>  Urgest thou that fell sacrifice?  Oh, where<br>  The spotless Virgin doom'd (so wild the creed)<br>  The Valkyr's airy troop to join, and glide<br>  Immortal through Valhalla's cloudy halls?<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK IV.</b> </h3>   Sunk was the sun, and up the eastern heaven,<br>  Like maiden on a lonely pilgrimage,<br>  Moved the meek Star of Eve; the wandering air<br>  Breathed odours; wood, and waveless lake, like man,<br>  Slept, weary of the garish babbling day. [5]<br>  <br>  Dove of the wilderness, thy snowy wing<br>  In slumber droops not; Lilian, thou alone,<br>  'Mid the deep quiet, wakest.  Dost thou rove,<br>  Idolatress of yon majestic moon,<br>  That like a crystal-throned queen in Heaven, [10]<br>  Seems with her present diety to hush<br>  To beauteous adoration all the earth?<br>  Might seem the solemn silent mountain tops<br>  Stand up and worship, the translucent streams<br>  Down th' hill sides glittering cherish the pure light [15]<br>  Beneath the shadowy foliage o'er them flung<br>  At intervals; the lake, so silver white,<br>  Glistens, all indistinct the snowy swans<br>  Bask in the radiance cool: doth Lilian muse<br>  To that apparent Queen her vesper hymn? [20]<br>  <br>  Nursling of solitude, her infant couch<br>  Never did mother watch, within the grave<br>  She slept unwaking; scornful turn'd aloof<br>  Caswallon, of those pure instinctive joys<br>  By father's felt, when playful infant grace, [25]<br>  Touch'd with a feminine softness, round the heart<br>  Winds its light maze of undefin'd delight,<br>  Contempuous; he with haughty joy beheld<br>  His boy, fair Malwyn, him in bossy shield<br>  Rock'd proudly, him upbore to mountain steep, [30]<br>  Fierce and undaunted, for their dangerous nest<br>  To battle with the eagle's clamorous brood.<br>  <br>  But she the while from human tenderness<br>  Estranged, and gentler feelings that light up<br>  The cheek of youth with rosy joyous smile, [35]<br>  Like a forgotten lute, play'd on alone<br>  By chance-caressing airs, amid the wild<br>  Beauteously pale, and sadly playful grew,<br>  A lonely child, by not one human heart<br>  Belov'd, and loving none; nor strange, if learnt [40]<br>  Her native fond affections to embrace<br>  Things senseless and inanimate: she lov'd<br>  All flow'rets that with rich embroidery fair<br>  Enamel the green earth, the odorous thyme,<br>  Wild rose, and roving eglantine, nor spar'd [45]<br>  To mourn their fading forms with childish tears.<br>  Gray birch and aspen light she lov'd, that droop<br>  Fringing the crystal stream; the sportive breeze<br>  That wanton'd with her brown and glossy locks,<br>  The sunbeam chequering the fresh bank.  Ere dawn [50]<br>  Wandering, and wandering still at dewy eve,<br>  By Glenderamakin's flower-empurpled marge,<br>  Derwent's blue lake, or Greta's wildering glen.<br>  <br>  Rare sound to her was human voice, scarce heard,<br>  Save of her aged nurse, or shepherd maid [55]<br>  Soothing the child with simple tale or song.<br>  Hence, all she knew of earthly hopes and fears,<br>  Life's sins and sorrows; better known the voice<br>  Belov'd of lark from misty morning cloud<br>  Blithe carolling, and wild melodious notes [60]<br>  Heard mingling in the summer wood, or plaint,<br>  By moonlight, of the lone night-warbling bird.<br>  Nor they of love unconscious, all around<br>  Fearless, familiar they their descants sweet<br>  Tun'd emulous.
