Her knew all living shapes [65]<br>  That tenant wood or rock, dun roe or deer,<br>  Sunning his dappled side at noontide crouch'd,<br>  Courting her fond caress, nor fled her gaze<br>  The brooding dove, but murmur'd sounds of joy.<br>  <br>  One summer noon, the silvery birchen shade [70]<br>  Pendant above from dripping crag her brow<br>  Veil'd from the fiery sunbeam, gems of spray<br>  Gleam'd cool around with watery rainbow-light,<br>  From a pure streamlet down its rocky bed<br>  Dashing sweet music; she on mossy couch [75]<br>  Sate listening the blithe thrush, whose airy notes<br>  In amorous contention Echo caught<br>  Responsive.  Sudden droop'd its flagging wing<br>  The timorous bird of song, and fluttering sought<br>  Soft refuge in the maiden's snowy breast. [80]<br>  She o'er the nestling prisoner folding light<br>  Her careless vest, stood gazing, where, awhile<br>  Dark in the sun-cloud's white, came fiercely down<br>  A swooping falcon: at her sight it check'd,<br>  Its keen eye bright with joy, th' admiring bird [85]<br>  Fearfully beauteous floated in the air,<br>  Its silver wings, and glossy plumage gray,<br>  Glanc'd in the sun light.  Up the maiden gaz'd,<br>  Smiling a pale and terrified delight,<br>  And seem'd for that lov'd warbler in her breast [90]<br>  Beseeching mercy.  'Mid the green wood sank<br>  Th' obedient bird; she, joyous at his flight,<br>  Her bosom half reveal'd, with gentle hand<br>  Caressing smooth'd her captive's ruffled plumes.<br>  Anon around a frighted thankful look [95]<br>  Glancing, what seem'd a human shape she saw,<br>  Or more than human; stately on his arm<br>  The falcon sate, and proudly flapp'd his wings.<br>  She turn'd to fly, yet fled not, turn'd to gaze,<br>  Yet dared not raise her downcast eye; she felt [100]<br>  Her warm cheek, why she knew not, blush, her hand<br>  Unconscious closer drew her bosom's fold.<br>  With accent mild the Stranger brief delay<br>  Entreated; she, albeit his gentle words<br>  Fell indistinct on her alarmed ear, [105]<br>  Listening delay'd, and still at fall of eve<br>  Delay'd, e'en then with dim reverted eye,<br>  Slow lingering on her winding homeward path.<br>  <br>  No more in pomp of war, or vaulting steed,<br>  Joyeth the Son of Vortigern, nor feast [110]<br>  With jocund harpings, and rich-jewell'd dames,<br>  Outshining in their pride the starry heavens.<br>  <br>  As fair the spring-flower's bloom, as graceful droops<br>  The wild ash spray, as sweet the mountain bee<br>  Murmurs, melodious breathes the twilight grove, [115]<br>  Unheard of her, unheeded, who erewhile<br>  Visited, constant as the morning dew,<br>  Those playmates and sweet sisters of her soul.<br>  In one sole image sees the enamour'd maid<br>  Concentrated all qualities of love, [120]<br>  All beauty, grace, and majesty.  The step<br>  Of tall stag prancing stately down the glen,<br>  The keen bright fierceness of the eagle's glance,<br>  And airy gentleness of timorous roe,<br>  And, more than all, a voice more soothing soft [125]<br>  Than wild-bird's carol, or the murmuring brook,<br>  With eloquence endued and melting words<br>  So wond'rous; though unheard since eve, the sounds<br>  Come mingling with her midnight sleep, and make<br>  The damask of her slumbering cheek grow warm. [130]<br>  <br>  And she is now beneath the moonlight rock,<br>  Chiding the rippling waters that efface<br>  That image on its azure breast indistinct,<br>  Garb, form, and feature, Vortimer, though mute,<br>  As prodigal of fondness, his bright face [135]<br>  Looks up to her with glance of tenderer love,<br>  Than wild-dove to its mate at earliest spring.<br>  <br>  Oft hath that moonlight wax'd and wan'd, since last<br>  He parted, all of him that could depart;<br>  Save that no distance could remove the words, [140]<br>  The look, the touch, that lives within her still,<br>  The promise of return sworn on her lips.<br>  <br>  And hark it comes, his steed along the glen;<br>  She o'er the lucid mirror stooping low,<br>  'Gins prank her dark-brown tresses, bashful smiles [145]<br>  Of virgin vanity flit o'er her cheek,<br>  Tinging its settled paleness.  Now 'tis near,<br>  But ne'er did Vortimer with iron hoof<br>  Bruise the green flowery sward that Lilian loves.<br>  A gentle frown of winning fond reproach [150]<br>  Arch'd her dark eyelash, as her head she turn'd,<br>  Ah!  not on Vortimer.  Her father stood<br>  Before her, stern and dark, his trembling child<br>  Cheer'd nor fond word, nor greeting kiss; his arm<br>  Clasp'd round her, on his steed again he sprung. [155]<br>  And on through moon-light and through shade he spurr'd<br>  Gleam'd like a meteor's track his flinty road,<br>  Like some rude hunter with a snow-white fawn,<br>  His midnight prey.  Anon, the mountain path<br>  'Gan upward wind, the fiery courser paus'd [160]<br>  Breathless, and faintly raising her thin form;<br>  "Oh, whither bear ye me?" with panting voice,<br>  Murmur'd.  Caswallon spake unmov'd, "to death."<br>  <br>  "Death, Father, death is comfortless and cold?<br>  Aye me!  when maiden dies, the smiling morn, [165]<br>  The wild birds singing on the twinkling spray,<br>  Wake her no more; the summer wind breathes soft,<br>  Waving the fresh grass o'er her narrow bed,<br>  Gladdening to all but her.  Senseless and cold<br>  She lies; while all she lov'd, unheard, unseen, [170]<br>  Mourn round her."  There broke off her faltering voice.<br>  Dimly, with farewell glance, she rov'd around,<br>  Never before so beautiful the lake<br>  Like a new sky, distinct with stars, the groves,<br>  Green banks and shadowy dells, her haunts of bliss, [175]<br>  Smil'd, ne'er before so lovely, their last smile;<br>  The fountains seem'd to wail, the twilight mists,<br>  On the wet leaves were weeping all for her,<br>  Had not her own tears blinded her; there too<br>  She surely had beheld a youthful form, [180]<br>  Wandering the solitary glen.  But loud<br>  The courser neigh'd, down bursting, wood and rock<br>  Fly backward, the wide plain its weary length<br>  Vainly outspreads; and now 'tis midnight deep.<br>  Ends at a narrow glen was pal'd with rude black rocks,<br>  There slowly roll'd a brook its glassy depth;<br>  Now in the moon-beams white, now dark in gloom.<br>  <br>  She liv'd, she breath'd, she felt, to her denied<br>  That sole sad happiness the wretched know, [190]<br>  Ev'n from excess of feeling, not to feel.<br>  Behold her gentle, delicate, and frail,<br>  Where all around, through rifted rock and wood,<br>  Grim features glare, huge helmed forms obscure<br>  People the living gloom, with dreary light [195]<br>  Glimmering, as of the moon from iron arms<br>  Coldly reflected, lovely stands she there,<br>  Like a blest Angel 'mid th' accurst of Hell.<br>  A voice is heard. -- "Lo, mighty Monarch, here<br>  The stream of sacrifice; to man alone [200]<br>  Fits the proud privilege of bloody death<br>  By shaft or mortal steel; to Hela's realm,<br>  Unblooded, woundless, must the maid descend;<br>  So in the bright Valhalla shall she crown<br>  For Woden and his Peers the cup of bliss. [205]<br>  Her white arms round her father's rugged neck<br>  Winding with desperate fondness, she 'gan pour,<br>  As to some dear, familiar, long-lov'd heart,<br>  Most eloquent her inarticulate prayers.<br>  Is the dew gleaming on his cheek?  or weeps [210]<br>  The savage and the stern, yet still her sire?<br>  But some rude arm of one, whose dreadful face<br>  She dared not gaze on, seiz'd her.  Gloomy stood,<br>  Folding his wolf-skin mantle to conceal<br>  The shuddering of his huge and mailed form, [215]<br>  Caswallon.  Then again the voice came forth,<br>  "Fast wanes the night, the Gods brook no delay,<br>  Monarch of Britain, speed."  He, at that name<br>  Shaking all human from his soul, flung back<br>  The foldings of his robe, and stood elate, [220]<br>  As haughty of some glorious deed, nor knew<br>  Barbarian blind as proud, who feels no more<br>  The mercies and affections of his kind,<br>  Casts off the image of God, a man of ill,<br>  With all his nature's earth, without its heaven. [225]<br>  <br>  A sound is in the silent night abroad,<br>  A sound of broken waters; rings of light<br>  Float o'er the dark stream, widening to the shore. [NOTE 9]<br>  And lo, her re-appearing form, as soft<br>  As fountain Nymph by weary hunter seen, [230]<br>  In the lone twilight glen; the moonlight gleam<br>  Falls tenderly on her beseeching face,<br>  Like th' halo of expiring Saint, she seems<br>  Lingering to lie upon the water top,<br>  As to enjoy once more that light belov'd; [235]<br>  And tremulously mov'd her soundless lips<br>  As syllabling the name of Vortimer;<br>  Then deep she sank, and quiet the cold stream,<br>  Unconscious of its guilt, went eddying on,<br>  And look'd up lovely to the gazing moon. [240]<br>  <br>  What deepest thoughts, young Vortimer, have place<br>  Within thy secret breast?  thou slowly rid'st<br>  By Eamont's alder brink, thy silver arms<br>  Through the brown copse with moonshine glittering dim.<br>  Is't that late fight by Thanet, when the fire [245]<br>  From thine and Horsa's steel, frequent and red,<br>  Burnt the pale sea-spray?  or thy stately charge,<br>  With show of British war, to curb and check<br>  The threatening Caledonian? or what bathes<br>  Youth's cheek in bitterest and most gall-like tears; [250]<br>  Thy father's shame, the curse that, unredeem'd<br>  By thy young valour, his once kingly name<br>  Brands with the deep-sear'd characters of hate?<br>  <br>  Or is 't that gentle Maid by Derwent lake,<br>  Her flower-prankst tresses and her pale sweet smile?<br>  How pleasant, after war and journeying fleet [256]<br>  To Britain's Northern realm, from Kent's white cliffs,<br>  Once more to see her early gliding foot<br>  Skimming the morning dews, to hear her voice,<br>  As artless, as melodious, melt on air, [260]<br>  Among the wood-birds matins, to surprise<br>  Thine own dear name upon her bashful lips!<br>  <br>  What floateth down the stream a deep dead white<br>  Amid the glittering moonshine, where the stream<br>  Runs black beneath the thicket boughs, still white, [265]<br>  Still slowly drifting, like a dying swan,<br>  In snowy beauty, on its watery bier?<br>  Oh, were but Lilian here! perchance its neck<br>  May struggle up, to the still waves to chaunt<br>  Its own soft requiem, the most gentle breath, [270]<br>  Most fancifully, delicately sweet,<br>  That ever soothes the midnight's dewy calm.<br>  <br>  Near, and more near, it takes a human shape;<br>  Some luckless maiden; haply her lov'd youth<br>  Awaits her at the well known place, upbraids [275]<br>  Her broken faith, as fond as Vortimer,<br>  As full of love.  'Tis closer now; he leaps<br>  From his high steed, he draws it to the shore.<br>  Scarce time for fancy or for fear, the moon<br>  Quench'd her broad light behind a rushing cloud, [280]<br>  And utter darkness settled round.  He sate<br>  In solitude, with that cold lifeless thing;<br>  He dared not leave it, for a hideous thought<br>  Was in his brain. -- "Why is it like to thee,<br>  My Lilian! be it any one but thou -- [285]<br>  Hopelessly cold, irrevocably cold:<br>  It cannot be, and yet 'twas like: her height,<br>  Her slender waist like Lilian's, and her hair<br>  As dainty soft, and trick'd with flowers; 'tis she,<br>  And I will kiss her, pardon if I err, [290]<br>  If stranger lips -- round, smooth like thine; but oh!<br>  So coldly passive! when we parted, thine<br>  Thwarted me with a struggling bashfulness,<br>  And, won at length, with meek surrender swell'd.<br>  Wild and delirious fancy! many a maid [295]<br>  Hath full round lips, to trick the hair with flowers<br>  'Tis common vanity.  If dead, even dead,<br>  So chilly senseless Lilian could not be<br>  To Vortimer's embrace.  Oh, but for light,<br>  Though dim and scanty as a glow-worm's fire, [300]<br>  To make me surely, hopelessly undone!<br>  Aught but this racking ignorance.  Dawn forth,<br>  Thou tortoise-footed sluggard, Morn!  one beam,<br>  Thou pitiless cold Moon!" -- Morn dawn'd not yet,<br>  And pale and thick remain'd the moonless sky. [305]<br>  Darkness around, the dead within his arms,<br>  He sate, even like a poison'd man, that waits,<br>  Yet haunted by a miserable hope,<br>  The palpable cold sickness in his veins,<br>  And yearns to live or die, scarce cares he which, [310]<br>  So one were certain.  But when slow the dawn<br>   Unveil'd its filmy light, he turn'd away<br>  From that which might be Lilian's face, and pray'd<br>  Even for the hateful, dun, uncertain gloom,<br>  As now by habit the slow-creeping grief, [315]<br>  Winding like ivy round and round his heart,<br>  Were rapture, and not lightly to be lost.<br>  It seem'd unconsciously his hand held up,<br>  Unconsciously declin'd his heavy eye,<br>  Where slowly brighten'd on that lifeless face [320]<br>  The intrusive beauty; one tress lay across,<br>  O'erspreading yet a thin and shadowy doubt;<br>  Move it he dare not, but the officious wind<br>  At length dispers'd it.  As the thought, the fear<br>  Were new, were sudden; like the lightning flash [325]<br>  That sears the infant in its mother's arms,<br>  Smote on him the dire certainty.  He clasp'd<br>  Her dampy dead cheek to his. -- "Thus, meet we thus,<br>  Lilian, my Lilian, silent, strange, and cold?<br>  I do not bid thee fondly gaze, nor ask [330]<br>  Long garrulous welcoming, -- but speak, but move!<br>  Lilian; ne'er thought I, I should live to loathe<br>  Thy gentle presence. -- Most ungrateful girl,<br>  And I for thee forsook my warrior trust,<br>  Was truant to my country's cause for thee. [335]<br>  By the green Tees my murmuring camp upbraids<br>  My soft unwarlike absence -- aye, unbraid!<br>  Henceforth finds Fortune no where on this soul<br>  To fasten misery on; I laugh at Fate,<br>  For I am past its wavering malice now. [340]<br>  Thinks she with hollow gauds of fame, and clang<br>  Of cymbal praise, to lure me forth, a bland<br>  And courteous parasite in her fond train?<br>  No; hang thou there, my helm, my broad-barr'd shield<br>  Rust on yon bank, my sword, one duty more, [345]<br>  To shape the smooth turf for my Lilian's grave;<br>  Thy bridal bed, sweet Maid, it should have been,<br>  Where thou and Vortimer had met.  Thy grave<br>  Shall be my field of fame, my wreath of pride<br>  The flowers the courteous spring shall lavish there;<br>  And I'll have glory -- in my depth of woe -- [351]<br>  A wild and strange delight -- in my despair --<br>  Not yet, the cold earth must not part us yet,<br>  One glimmer more from thine eye's dark-fring'd blue,<br>  One throb, one tremor, though it be the last [355]<br>  In thy soft limbs -- dead, sightless, icy dead!" -- <br>  <br>  O'er his lost Love, thus that sad Prince, undream'd<br>  The hell-born secret of her fate, arraign'd<br>  Blind Chance for keen-ey'd Man's earth-sullying sins.<br>  <br>  But southward far the savage fleet bore on. [360]<br>  On Flamborough-head the morning sun look'd dusk<br>  Through their dim sails; where Scarborough's naked foot<br>  Spurns back, and saith, "no further," to the waves,<br>  From cleft and cave the sullen sea birds sprang,<br>  Wheeling in air with dizzy flight, and shriek'd [365]<br>  Their dreary fears abroad.  The Shepherd, wont<br>  O'er level Lindesay view the watery plain,<br>  Blue trembling to the soft horizon's line,<br>  Sees, like a baleful portent from the heavens,<br>  That sable train of gloom warp slowly past. [370]<br>  Th' Icenian coast (that sceptered woman's realm,<br>  Bonduca, who from her fair body slaked<br>  The stain of Roman lust in Roman blood,)<br>  Looks haggard, with distracted faces wan,<br>  Hoar age, fair youth, the woman and the child, [375]<br>  From beech or steep cliff, gazing now to Heaven,<br>  Now on that ocean army's watery march.<br>  <br>  Oh Nelson!  if the unborn soul distinct<br>  Amid the loose infinity of space,<br>  Be visited by apparitions dim [380]<br>  Of this earth's fleeting Present, and inhale<br>  Faint foretaste of its mortal passions, thou,<br>  When, with usurping prow, that foreign fleet<br>  Daunted thy Britain, thou didst surely yearn<br>  To unordained maturity to force [385]<br>  Thy unripe being, to foreseize from Fate<br>  Thy slow existence.  Oh, the days must dawn,<br>  When Saxon and when Briton, melted off<br>  All feud, all hate, all discord, of their strength<br>  And valour blent th' abstract and essence rich, [390]<br>  One sword, one name, one glory, and one God,<br>  From their bright armoury of Captains, thee<br>  Their chosen thunderbolt shall usher forth,<br>  From the leagued Nations' frantic grasp to wrest<br>  Britain's allotted sceptre of the sea. [395]<br>  <br>  A brighter and more British battlement,<br>  Than tender forms of women, the pale dread<br>  Of infants and decrepit eld, from Thames<br>  To Thanet crown the pale-brow'd cliffs of Kent.<br>  As when from Aulis that immortal fleet [400]<br>  Swept the Ægean, all the hollow beach,<br>  And every Phyrgian promontory glow'd<br>  With brazen battle, here the Morning's Son,<br>  Swarth Memnon, here the invulnerable strength<br>  Of Cycnus, here the beardless Troilus, [405]<br>  Unwounded by soft Cresseide's arrowy eyes;<br>  Here Hector, seeking through the watery route<br>  The tall Thessalian prow, with fatal thirst<br>  Furious even then, the silver-footed Queen<br>  To orphan of her heaven-soul'd boy.  So broad, [410]<br>  So brave in splendour tower'd the rampart bold<br>  Of British Warriors on that pallid shore.<br>   On Thanet are the Sea King Brethren met.<br>  Their greeting in that fiercely sportive strain<br>  That, elevate with imminent success, [415]<br>  Scoffs at past ill. -- "On Thanet's marge well met,<br>  Erle Horsa; now meseems our spacious realm<br>  Is somewhat waste and shrunken, since we last<br>  View'd its fair confines, for such noble guests<br>  And numerous as attend our royal march, [420]<br>  Our kingdom's harbours shew too close, our land<br>  Narrow and brief for such free spirits' range.<br>  Ill husbandry!  our fertile province wide<br>  To barter for this spare and meagre isle.<br>  Horsa, for anchorage and breathing space [425]<br>  Our weary mariners must e'en go sue<br>  Their gentle Briton neighbours; haply they,<br>  Knowing our native courtesy, may cede<br>  From their abundance some fair leagues of earth.<br>  <br>  "Ingrate and blind (cried Horsa), they forswear [430]<br>  Our mild dominion; to their King's behest<br>  Rebellious, they proclaim the British earth<br>  The undivided, indivisible right<br>  Of their old British sires, nor may't descend<br>  Sever'd and mutilate to their British sons. [435]<br>  They shook not off the Roman's gentle sway,<br>  To slave it to Barbarians.  Specious terms,<br>  And with such cogent arguments enforc'd,<br>  We were fain shroud us in this narrow isle<br>  From such hot disputants; a desperate spirit [440]<br>  Was that old Caesar, who first planted here<br>  The tree of conquest." -- "Holds the King his faith?"<br>  "Oh, thy fair daughter hath a soft-link'd chain<br>  For the old royal Lion; he obeys,<br>  Like a slim greyhound in a silken leash, [445]<br>  Her eye-won empire.  But there walks abroad<br>  A youngling of the brood; no blood but mine<br>  Might flesh the ravine of his dainty jaws.<br>  This Vortimer, this bright-ey'd, beardless boy,<br>  Aye, front to front I met him, but their bands [450]<br>  Rent us asunder, and my crest-lopp'd helm,<br>  My scatter'd blood, past unaveng'd.  Now earth<br>  Swallow me in my wrath, heaven's bolt sear up<br>  My constant heart, if I forget thee, Boy,<br>  Nor shear the gay sprouts of thy budding fame!" [455]<br>  "A child their mightiest!" -- "Scornful Hengist, no;<br>  A manlier spirit rideth the fierce storm,<br>  One in whom bravery and counsel vie<br>  For excellence: wild battle wears the shape<br>  His will ordains; and if the rebel swerve, [460]<br>  He forceth it with his strong sword t' obey<br>  His high behest, and take the fate he gives."<br>  "His name -- his name!" -- "The Chieftain of the Vales,<br>  So sounds his title." -- Then a bitter groan,<br>  'Twere hard to tell from what bad passion, hate [465]<br>  Or dread, or hideous hope, from Hengist's breast<br>  Burst forth; with his mail'd hand he clasp'd his head,<br>     <br>  As though to mould the discord of his thoughts<br>  To one strong mass: then, as the birth were ripe,<br>  A light and laughing carelessness relax'd [470]<br>  Those knitted furrows, seem'd his eager soul<br>  Clasp'd the dim future with a wanton joy.<br>  <br>  But on the mainland, in sad council, meet<br>  The Baronage of Britain, timorous hearts<br>  In hollow unsubstantial valour trick'd, [475]<br>  While those who dare shew fear, fear undisguis'd.<br>  Their first fierce rush of courage pass'd, like flame<br>  The mountain heath devouring, with fleet blaze,<br>  But transitory; they of generous thoughts,<br>  Of appetites whose sole rich draught is fame, [480]<br>  Wanting the steadfast fuel, the strong wind<br>  Wanting of love devotional, heart-deep<br>  To their own native land, that passion proud<br>  That is all passions, that hath breath to fan<br>  To a broad light beyond the noon-day Sun [485]<br>  The waning embers of faint zeal; they hence<br>  Powerful but now with gallant charge to sweep<br>  From Kent's fair valleys Horsa's Saxon train,<br>  Downcast in mien and mind, with prospect sad<br>  Now count that countless navy's gathering sails. [490]<br>  <br>  Not now the rapture and the restlessness,<br>  The riding and the racing, burst and shock,<br>  And sudden triumph, or as sudden death;<br>  Now long, long wasting of the limbs and life,<br>  The circumspect cold strife, drear march, long watch,<br>  Forepining day, and vigilant sleepless night, [496]<br>  Eternal and interminable war,<br>  Before them spreads its comfortless wide tract.<br>  Gone all soft joys, all courtly luxuries gone:<br>  The languor of the bath, the harp, the song [500]<br>  By twilight in the Lady's sleepless porch,<br>  The loitering in the sunny colonnade,<br>  The circus and the theatre, the feast<br>  Usurping the mild midnight's solemn hours;<br>  From holier hearts, the chapel and the prayer, [505]<br>  The matins, and melodious vesper hymn,<br>  The bridal with its gay and jocund route,<br>  The baptism with its revel, gone - all gone.<br>  The burial on cold battle field, unhymn'd,<br>  Unmourn'd, untomb'd; nor taper, tear, nor rite: [510]<br>  Gentle commercing between God and man<br>  Broke off, save hasty prayer ere battle morn,<br>  Cold orison upon the midnight watch.<br>  <br>  Sole pillar of the quaking temple, firm,<br>  Inflexible, on the foundation deep [515]<br>  Of his broad spirit, Samor bears the weight<br>  Of imminent danger, and his magic voice<br>  With shame, with praise, with soothing, and with scorn,<br>  Scatters the languid mist, that wreathes their souls,<br>  And from their blanch'd cheecks drives the white dismay.<br>  <br>  What ho! a trumpet from the Thanet shore, [521]<br>  Truce for the Saxon's embassage; his hand<br>  Outholding the white wand of peace, comes on<br>  Old Cerdic, and before the assemblage proud<br>  Speaks frank and bold that gray Plenipotent [525]<br>  <br>  "Britons, most strange 'twill sound, while our vast fleet<br>  Affronts your pale cliffs with fierce shew of war,<br>  Yet would we peace with Britain.  Deem not this,<br>  In the blown arrogance of brief success,<br>  The hard-wrung cowering of faint fear; look round [530]<br>  Your own brief camp, then gaze abroad, our sails<br>  Outnumber your thin helms, and that pale fear<br>  Is not familiar with our German souls.<br>  This know ye further, what we Saxons dare,<br>  That dare we nobly, openly.  Far south [535]<br>  A rich and wanton land it champaign green<br>  Spreads to the sun, there all the basking hill<br>  Glow with the red wine, there the fresh air floats<br>  So fragrant, that 'tis pleasure but to breathe,<br>  Aye, one blue summer, in the cloudless skies; [540]<br>  And our old Bards have legends, how of yore<br>  From that soft land bright eagles, fledged with gold,<br>  Danube or Rhine o'erflew, their Caesars fired<br>  Our holy groves with insolent flames, and girt<br>  Our fierce free foresters with slavish chains, [545]<br>  That scarce bold Herman rent their massive links.<br>  Not to despoil a mild and gentle isle,<br>  For full fierce vengeance on Imperial Rome<br>  Pours forth embattled Germany. Then hear,<br>  Brave Islanders! our Saxon terms of peace: [550]<br>  For this fair province, our's by royal boon<br>  Of your King, Vortigern, give plenteous gold,<br>  And with it take the gift, that deepest wrings<br>  Our German souls to part with, our revenge.<br>  With most unwonted patience will we bear [555]<br>  Erle Horsa's camp with fierce assault o'er-borne,<br>  And British wolves full-gorged with Saxon gore.<br>  Then not as foes, but friends, we disembark<br>  Our sea-worn crews, ourselves, the Chiefs of war,<br>  In solemn festival to your high Lords, [560]<br>  Pledge on the compact our unwavering faith.<br>  But if ye still with lavish thirst pursue<br>  War's crimson goblets, freely let them flow.<br>  If the fierce pastime of the fire and sword<br>  Be jocund to ye, ho, let slip the game. [565]<br>  Your city walls are not as airy high,<br>  But our fleet flames may climb their dizzy towers,<br>  And revel on their pinnacles of pride;<br>  Your breastplate not so adamantine proof,<br>  But our keen falchions to your hearts may find [570]<br>  A direful passage.  And not we alone,<br>  Caswallon, at our call, o'er the wide North<br>  Wakes the hoarse music of his rushing cars;<br>  Then choose your bride, oh Britons, lo, each courts<br>  Your arms with rival beauties, Peace and War. [575]<br>  <br>  Thus half in courtesy, defiant half,<br>  To wait their answer he withdrew.  Ere died<br>  His voice, ere from a single lip assent<br>  Had parted, Samor rose, and cried aloud --<br>  <br>  "Britons! oh Britons! hinds fear fawning wolves, [580]<br>  The peasant flies the snake that smoothly coils<br>  Round his numb foot its gay enamell'd rings;<br>  I dread a peaceful Saxon.  'Tis too rare,<br>  Prodigious, and unnatural, like a star<br>  Seen in the noon day.  Was't for this, for this [585]<br>  Round Vortigern's tame soul that proud-ey'd Queen<br>  Wound her voluptuous trammels?  did the meek,<br>  The hermit Constans, bleed for this?  Oh, Peace<br>  Is like the rain from heaven, the clouds must burst<br>  Ere earth smile lovely with its lucid dews. [590]<br>  Peace must be won by war, swords, and swords alone<br>  Work the strong treaty.  Shall our slaves, that sold<br>  Their blood, their lives unto us for base hire,<br>  On our fair provinces set now their price?<br>  Nor feast, nor metal give we, but cold steel! [595]<br>  Give gold!  as wisely might the miser lead<br>  The robber to his treasury, and then cry,<br>  "Go hence, and plunder;" 'twere to tempt, to bribe<br>  The undream'd perjury, and spread a lure,<br>  To bring the parted spoiler swiftly back. [600]<br>  Outnumber us!  and are we sunk so low<br>  To count our valour by our helmet crests? --<br>  Oh, every soul that loves his native land,<br>  It is a legion; where the fire shall sear<br>  The hydra heads of Liberty?  Our earth [605]<br>  Shall burst to bearing of as boon a crop<br>  Of sworded soldiers, as of bladed grass,<br>  And all our hills branch out in groves of steel.<br>  So thought our fathers, so they bravely strove<br>  For the bleak freedom of their steamy moors, [610]<br>  Their black oaks' fruitage coarse, and rites uncouth<br>  Of Druid, by the beal-fire's lurid flame.<br>  But we, less drossy beings, filter'd off<br>  Our natures rude and gross, create anew<br>  Souls of fine wants, and delicate desires, [615]<br>  Rich in the fair civilities of life,<br>  Endued with sensitiveness keen and clear<br>  Of earth's best pleasures, shall we tamely yield<br>  Our beauteous Britain, our own pleasant isle,<br>  To dreary-soul'd Barbarians?  'Tis not now [620]<br>  Merely to 'scape the heaven-branded name of slaves,<br>  For license to breathe where we choose, and wield<br>  At our own wayward will unfetter'd limbs.<br>  Oh, if we fail, free Christians must sink down<br>  To Heathen slaves, our gilded palace roofs [625]<br>  Shout the loose riot of new Lords, our wives<br>  Be like base plunder, vilely bought and sold;<br>  Worse shame!  worse sin!  the murky Heathen groves<br>  O'er our fall'n Churches their pale gloom advance;<br>  Our holy air go hot and reeking up [630]<br>  With impious incense to blood-beverag'd Gods;<br>  The deep damnation of a Pagan creed<br>  Rot in our children's souls!  Then be our peace<br>  Not hasty, as of timorous souls that snatch<br>  At every feeble reed, but stoop we to it [635]<br>  As with a conqueror's pride, with steel-glov'd hand<br>  Seal our stern treaty.  So if they depart, <br>  And with their spread sails hunt their mad emprize;<br>  But while one prow dash menace on our shore,<br>  Our earth be patient of one armed hoof, [640]<br>  Tame treaty, temporizing truce, avaunt!<br>  The foreign banner that usurps our winds,<br>  Be it a foe, strange steel that both divert<br>  One ray of sunlight from our shores, be that<br>  The scope and centre of all British swords. [645]<br>  So build we up our peace on the strong rock<br>  Of brave defiance, cement it with scorn,<br>  Set bright-arm'd Valour in its jealous porch,<br>  Bold warden; from our own intrinsic strength,<br>  Not from the mercy of our foes, be free." -- [650]<br>  <br>  Oh the soul's fire, of that swift element<br>  Th' intensest, broadest spreads the nimblest mounts,<br>  With flaky fierce contagion; it hath caught<br>  In that Baronial conclave, it hath blazed.<br>  But then rose Elidure, with bashful mien, [655]<br>  Into himself half shrinking, from his lips<br>  The dewy words dropt, delicate and round,<br>  And crept into the chambers of the soul,<br>  Like the bee's liquid honey: -- "And thou too,<br>  Enamour'd of this gaudy murderer, War! [660]<br>  Samor, in hunger's meagre hour who scorns<br>  A fair-skinn'd fruit, because its inward pulp<br>  May be or black or hollow?  this bland Peace<br>  May be a rich-rob'd evil; war, stern war,<br>  Wears manifest its hideousness, and bares [665]<br>  Deformities the Sun shrinks to behold.<br>  Because 'tis in the wanton roll of chance<br>  That he may die, who desperately leaps<br>  Into the pit, with mad untimely arms<br>  To clasp annihilation?  Were no path [670]<br>  But through the grim and haunted wilds of strife,<br>  To the mild shrine of peace, maids would not wear<br>  Their bridal chaplets with more joy, than I<br>  Th' oppressive morion: then th' old vaunt were wise,<br>  To live in freedom, or for freedom die. [675]<br>  Then would I too dissemble, with vain boast,<br>  Our island's weakness; wear an iron front,<br>  Though all within were silken, soft, and smooth.<br>  For what are we, slight sunshine birds, thin-plum'd,<br>  For dalliance with the mild, luxurious airs, [680]<br>  To grapple with these vultures, whose broad vans,<br>  Strung with their icy tempests, but with wind<br>  Of their forth rushing down would swoop us?  Then, <br>  Then, Samor, eminent in strength and power,<br>  It were most proud for thee alone to break [685]<br>  The hot assault, with single arm't arrest<br>  The driving ruin -- ruin, ah! too sure.<br>  Oh, t'were most proud; to us sad comfort; sunk,<br>  Amerc'd of all our fair, smooth sliding hours,<br>  Our rich abodes the wandering war-flame's feast. [690]<br>  Samor, our fathers fear'd not death; cast off<br>  Most careless their coarse lives; with nought to lose,<br>  They fear'd no loss; our breathing is too rich,<br>  Too precious this our sensitive warm mould,<br>  Its joyances, affections, hopes, desires, [695]<br>  For such light venture.  Oh, then, be we not<br>  Most wretched from the fear of wretchedness?<br>  If war must be, in God's name let war be;<br>  But, oh, with clinging hand, with lingering love,<br>  Clasp we our mistress, Peace.  Gold! what is gold? [700]<br>  My fair and wealthy palace set to sale,<br>  Cast me a beggar to the elements' scorn;<br>  But leave me peace, oh, leave my country peace,<br>  And I will call it mercy, bounty, love!" --<br>  <br>  So spake he, with vain shew of public zeal [705]<br>  Blazoning his weak intent; and so prevail'd<br>  His loose and languid eloquence.  Each rent<br>  The golden frontlet from his helm, cast down<br>  His breastplate's golden scales, in contest free<br>  Prodigal rivals at rich price to buy [710]<br>  That baleful merchandize, their country's shame.<br>  <br>  Oh, where the royal Brethren now?  the pride<br>  Serene of Emrys?  where thy Dragon crest,<br>  Prince Uther?  for thy voice, young Vortimer!<br>  Seal, Samor, thy prophetic lips; in vain [715]<br>  The trumpet of thy warning shouts abroad.<br>  Will the winds hear thee?  will the rocks obey?<br>  Or hearts than wind more light, than rocks more cold?<br>  <br>  Gray Cerdic hath their faint award; they part<br>  Jocund, and light of hope; but Samor grasp'd [720]<br>  The hand of Elidure: -- "My childhood's friend,<br>  I sue thee by all joys we two have shared,<br>  Our interchange of souls, communion free<br>  Of every thought and motion of our hearts,<br>  Our infant pastimes, and our graver joys, [725]<br>  Go not thou to this feast." -- "Doth Samor go?"<br>  "Britain must have no danger, gentle friend,<br>  That Samor shares not; thou art noted well<br>  To hate the riotous and brawling feast.<br>  With thy fond bride, thy Evelene, await [730]<br>  Silent the knowledge whether thou or I<br>  Have err'd in this day's council." -- "No, best friend,<br>  Samor must have no danger Elidure<br>  Shares not; oh, why this cold and gloomy dread?<br>  In the deep centre of our isle be held [735]<br>  This dreaded banquet.  Samor, ne'er thought I,<br>  While my mild blood ran contrast, thine would flag,<br>  And curdle with the pallid frost of fear."<br>  <br>  'Tis famed, that then, albeit amid the rush<br>  Of clamorous joy unmark'd, in drearier days [740]<br>  Remember'd, signs on earth, and signs in heaven,<br>  With loud and solemn interdict arraign'd<br>  That hasty treaty; maniacs kindled up<br>  With horrible intelligence that pits<br>  Of their deep hollow eyes, and meaning strange [745]<br>  Gave order to their wandering utterance: stream'd<br>  Amid the dusky woods broad sheeted flames;<br>  The blue fires on the fen at noon-day danc'd<br>  Their wavering morrice, and the bold ey'd wolves<br>  Howl'd on the sun.  Life, ominous and uncouth, [750]<br>  Seiz'd upon ancient and forgotten things;<br>  The Cromlechs rock'd, the Druids circles wept<br>  Cold ruddy dews; as of that neighbouring feast<br>  Conscious, the tall Stone Henge did shrilly shriek<br>  As with a whirlwind, though no cloud was mov'd [755]<br>  In the still skies.  A wailing, as of harps,<br>  Sad with no mortal sorrow, sail'd abroad<br>  Through the black oaks of Mona.  Old deep graves<br>  Were restless, and arm'd bones of buried men<br>  Lay clattering in their stony cells.  'Twas faith, [760]<br>  White women upon sable steeds were seen<br>  In fleet career 'neath the rank air; the earth<br>  Gave up no echo to their noiseless feet,<br>  And on them look'd the Moon with leprous light<br>  Prodigious, haply like those slender shapes [765]<br>  In the ice desert by Caswallon seen.<br>  From Mona to the snowy Dover cliffs,<br>  From Skiddaw to St. Michael's vision'd mount,<br>  Unknown from heaven, or earth, or nether pit,<br>  Unknown or from the living or the dead,<br>  From being of this world, or nature higher,<br>  Pass'd one long shriek, whereat old Merlin leap'd<br>  From his hoar haunt by Snowdon, and in dusk<br>  And dreary descant mutter'd all abroad<br>  What the thin air grew cold and dim to hear. [775]<br>  <br>  'Tis said, rude portents in the Church of God,<br>  With insolent noises, brake the holy calm.<br>  The gray owl hooted at the noontide chaunt,<br>  The young owl clamour'd at the matin song,<br>  The pies and ravens, from the steeple top, [780]<br>  To the priest's Benedicite moan'd back<br>  A sullen hoarse Amen, and obscene bats<br>  Around the altar candlesticks did flap<br>  Their leathern wings.  Yea, from his stricken hand<br>  The white-stol'd Bishop to the earth let fall [785]<br>  The consecrated chalice; th' holy wine<br>  (Ineffable!) flow'd on the pavement stone.<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK V.</b> </h3>   Swan of the Ocean, on thy throne of waves<br>  Exultant dost thou sit, thy mantling plumes<br>  Ruffled with joy, thy pride of neck elate,<br>  To hail fair Peace, like Angel visitant,<br>  Descending, amid joy of earth and heaven, [5]<br>  To bless thy fair abode.  The laughing skies<br>  Look bright, oh, Britain!  on thy hour of bliss.<br>  In sunshine fair the blithe and bounteous May<br>  O'er hill and vale goes dancing; blooming flowers<br>  Under her wanton feet their dewy bells [10]<br>  Shake joyous; clouds of fragrance round her float.<br>  City to city cries, and town to town<br>  Wafting glad tidings: wide their flower-hung gates<br>  Throw back the churches, resonant with pomp<br>  Of priests and people, to the Lord their prayers [15]<br>  Pouring, the richest incense of pure hearts.<br>  With garland and with song the maids go forth,<br>  And mingle with the iron ranks of war<br>  Their forms of melting softness; gentle gales<br>  Blow music o'er the festal land, from harp [20]<br>  And merry rebeck, till the floating air<br>  Seem harmony: still all fierce sounds of war;<br>  No breath within the clarion's brazen throat;<br>  Soft slumber in the war-steed's drooping mane.<br>  <br>  Not in the palace proud, or gorgeous hall, [25]<br>  The banqueting of Peace; on Ambri plain<br>  Glitter the white pavilions, to the sun<br>  Their snowy pomp unfolding; there the land<br>  Pours its rejoicing multitudes to gaze,<br>  Briton and Saxon, in majestic league, [30]<br>  Mingling their streaming banners blazon'd waves.<br>  Blithe as a virgin bridal, rich and proud<br>  As gorgeous triumph for fair kingdom won,<br>  Flows forth the festal train; with arms elate<br>  The mothers bear their infants to behold [35]<br>  That Hengist, whose harsh name erewhile their cheeks<br>  Blanch'd to cold paleness; they their little hands<br>  Clap, smiling, half delighted, half in dread.<br>  Upon that hated head, from virgin hands,<br>  Rain showers of bloom; beneath those hated feet [40]<br>  Is strewn a flowery pavement; harp and voice<br>  Hymn blessings on the Saxon, late denounc'd<br>  Th' implacable, inexorable foe.<br>  <br>  Lordly they pass'd and lofty; other land<br>  Save Britain, of such mighty despots proud, [45]<br>  Had made a boast of slavery; giant men<br>  In soul as body. Not the Goth more dread,<br>  Tall Alaric, who through imperial Rome<br>  March'd conqueror, nor that later Orient chief,<br>  Turban'd Mohammed, who o'er fall'n Byzance [50]<br>  His moony ensign planted: they, unarm'd,<br>  Yet terrible, went haughty on, of power<br>  A world to vanquish, not one narrow isle.<br>  <br>  The hollow vault of heaven is rent with shouts,<br>  Wild din and hurry of tumultuous joy [55]<br>  Waves the wide throng, for lo, in perfect strength,<br>  Consummate height of manhood, but the glow,<br>  The purple grace of youth, th' ambrosial hue<br>  Of life's fresh morning, on his glossy hair,<br>  His smooth and flushing features, Samor comes. [60]<br>  His name is on the lisping infant's lips,<br>  Floats on the maiden's song; him warrior men<br>  Hail with proud crest elate; him present, deem<br>  Peace timorous mercy on the invading foe.<br>  Around the Kings of Britain, some her shame, [65]<br>  Downy and silken with luxurious ease,<br>  Others more hardy, in whose valiant looks<br>  Were freedom and command: of princely stem<br>  Alone were absent the forsaken King<br>  And his sad Son, and those twin royal youths, [70]<br>  Emrys and Uther; nor the Mountain Lord,<br>  With that young eaglet of his race; deign share<br>  The gaudy luxuries of peace; save these,<br>  All Britain's valiance, princedom, and renown<br>  March'd jubilant, with symphony and song. [75]<br>  <br>  Noon; from his high empyreal throne the Sun<br>  Floods with broad light the living plain; more rich<br>  Ne'er blaz'd his summer couch, when sea and sky,<br>  In royal pomp of cloudy purple and gold,<br>  Curtain his western chambers, breathing men [80]<br>  Gorgeous and numberless as those bright waves<br>  Flash, in their motion, the quick light; aloof<br>  The banqueters, like Gods at nectar feast,<br>  Sit sumptuous and pavilion'd; all glad tones<br>  From trembling string, or ravishing breath or voice, [85]<br>  In clouds of harmony melt up to Heaven;<br>  O'erwhelming splendour all of sight and sound,<br>  One rich oppression of eye, ear, and mind.<br>  <br>  Midnight, in darkness heavy, thick, and chill;<br>  In silence rigid, deep and breathless, stands [90]<br>  On the wide plain one lonely Man.  Wan light,<br>  From dim decaying firebrand in his grasp,<br>  Feebly, with gleam inconstant, shews his mien<br>  Hopeless, too haughty to despair: His eye,<br>  As jealous of dark foe, goes wandering round: [95]<br>  Yet seems he one more fear'd than fearing; rent<br>  His robes' rich splendour; and his ponderous arm,<br>  With its wild weapon wearily declin'd,<br>  Bears token of rude strife -- though rude, though fierce,<br>  By thy brow's pride, thou sad and stately Man! [100]<br>  No faint inglorious craven hast thou shrunk,<br>  In dread of death, or avarice base of blood.<br>  <br>  At that dead hour, in Caesar's city gates [NOTE 10]<br>  The Briton wives and mothers sate; at eve<br>  They, from the plain, had homeward turn'd, to rock [105]<br>  Their infants' rosy sleep, or trim the couch<br>  For him belov'd and loving; some, from joy<br>  Sleepless, sate watching the gray shadows fall,<br>  In luxury of impatience; slumbering some,<br>  From weariness of pleasure, in light dreams [110]<br>  Liv'd o'er again the morning's jocund hours.<br>  <br>  That hour, one horn with long and solemn blast<br>  Went wailing up the heavens; less shrill, less drear,<br>  Blew through the fatal Roncesvalles pass,<br>  In after times, Roland's deep bugle, heard [115]<br>  Dolorous, so poets feign, on Paris' wall.<br>  The air seem'd shivering where the knell pass'd on,<br>  As with a cold wind shudder'd the thick trees.<br>  <br>  But those fond women hail that brazen sound,<br>  Joy's harbinger, sweet signal of return; [120]<br>  As the fond maid her lover's moonlight lute,<br>  They drink in its dire harshness, busy round<br>  Gazing, if aught neglected, careless aught<br>  Belie the welcome, or to wakening child<br>  Smile the glad tidings, or along the walls [125]<br>  People the dim air with the forms they love.<br>  Oh, fond of fancy!  credulous of hope!<br>  Ye hear but pleasure in that horn; but see,<br>  In the dim tumult of yon moving lights,<br>  Swift homeward hurrying.  Now the slow delay [130]<br>  Is but a lengthen'd rapture: steps are heard,<br>  And figures indistinct are in the gloom<br>  Advancing; yet no festal pomp proclaim'd<br>  By music's merry breath, but mute and slow,<br>  As from dark funeral: haply wearied all [135]<br>  With the long revel day.  But ye 'gin trace<br>  Some well-known gesture, dear familiar step,<br>  Each boastful of her lover's speedier pace.<br>  Saxon the first, how wearily slow they pass!<br>  Still are they Saxon, Saxon still, the last [140]<br>  Saxon; in wonder they, nor yet in fear,<br>  Question the dark air with their searching eyes,<br>  Incredulous arraign the deepening gloom,<br>  That with an envious melancholy shroud<br>  Palls the long-look'd for, late-returning.  Them, [145]<br>  Ah, deeper darkness covers; to their homes<br>  Never more to return!  Lo, all at once<br>  The bloody knives, borne boastful, their red light<br>  Flash murtherous; known is all ere aught is fear'd.<br>  And yet are there unfaded on their brows [150]<br>  The garlands that ye fondly wove, the air<br>  Not silent of your blessings.  From these walls,<br>  At morn, three hundred breathing valiant men<br>  Went proudly forth -- in solitary life<br>  Moves o'er the plain that one majestic shape, [155]<br>  Like Spirit of Vengeance o'er some ghastly land<br>  That scoff'd erewhile, in high portentous guilt,<br>  The slumbering of God's wrath, now blasted lies,<br>  Infecting with the ashes of its wreck<br>  The late chastising heavens.  So lone, so dark, [160]<br>  But pale with human sorrows at his heart,<br>  The King of that Bright City in the Vales,<br>  Walks the waste gloom, around him the cold winds<br>  Speak voices from the dead, and oft he turns,<br>  Brandishing defiance on the air, and smites [165]<br>  Some seeming Saxon with his smouldering brand.<br>  <br>  Now rests he in that old mysterious ring,<br>  The dateless and the numberless Stonehenge,<br>  That is, and hath been, whence or how, none knows.<br>  But even the Master Druid with slow dread [170]<br>  Its dangerous precincts trod, though noontide bright<br>  Revell'd in the rich heavens, and holiest harps<br>  Purified the calm air: rose like the wreck<br>  Of some old world the shadowy temple huge,<br>  Shapeless magnificence!  here souls profane [175]<br>  Deem'd rites so potent held as made the oaks<br>  Stand still and motionless 'mid the wild storm,<br>  And with a light, nor of the stars nor moon,<br>  Sheeted the midnight heavens: deem'd some, more sage,<br>  Th' Invisible his cloudy presence here [180]<br>  Embodied, and with wisdom heavenly and high<br>  Full feasted the tranced soul; all the dire place<br>  Fled, fearing more, unknowing what they fear'd.<br>  <br>  Amid those stony giants that uptower<br>  In massy darkness, or in the wind's rush [185]<br>  Seem swaying on their dizzy balance, stands,<br>  If virtue of aught earthly may feel awe,<br>  Awe-struck the Christian; now his calmer soul<br>  Had time for grief, for memory; o'er him flows<br>  Deep-lulling quiet; here the light and gay [190]<br>  Had felt a motion on their lips like prayer,<br>  Nor marvel then that holy thoughts oppress'd<br>  With a full extacy the Christian soul.<br>  <br>  "Merciful!  by whose will mine arm hath pav'd<br>  With the strewn corpses of my murtherous foes [195]<br>  A dismal passage, while around me Death<br>  Mow'd Britain with his secret scythe!  oh God,<br>  I thank thee, if I die, a warrior's death<br>  May be my brave distinction: if this life<br>  Be worthy thy upholding, though all lost, [200]<br>  The friendships and the prides, that made its course<br>  Blissful and bright, I thank thee for my life:<br>  Thank thee, that yet on British earth shall breathe<br>  A Briton, resolute on that last crag,<br>  That knows not the rude Saxon's tread, to rise [205]<br>  Erect in stately freedom, and o'er-brood<br>  The dim and desert beacon of revenge.<br>  Or deign'st thou this low frame of dust to choose<br>  Thy minister of wrath, I not with prayer<br>  Vain and presumptuous, summon from the clouds [210]<br>  Thy thunders, nor invoke prodigious Death<br>  To smite my foes.  Hopes perishable man,<br>  At his wild bidding, thou the laws wilt burst,<br>  Wherewith thou fetterest thy Omnipotence?<br>  Harden to stern endurance these frail limbs, [215]<br>  With adamantine patience sheathe my soul,<br>  That nor pale shrinking of the coward flesh,<br>  Nor inward palsying swerve from its brave scope<br>  Th' aspiring spirit; grant thou this sole prayer,<br>  And I thus lone, thus desolate proclaim, [220]<br>  Single, yet dauntless, to yon Saxon host<br>  Stubborn defiance, haughty to bear up<br>  The wreck of Britain with unstooping neck."<br>  <br>  Now over all the orient sky, the Morn<br>  Spread rosy in her youth of light, as fair, [225]<br>  As bright her rising on this plain of death,<br>  As yesterday, when festal multitudes<br>  Greeted her dawn; so vain the boast of man,<br>  That earth, and air, and sky, their mimic hues<br>  Borrow from his frantic woes and joys. [230]<br>  <br>  And o'er the plain began his lonely way<br>  The Warrior, on his brow the unheeded wind<br>  Fannd freshness, and the wandering lark unheard,<br>  Quiver'd her blithe song, like an airy voice,<br>  Bathing in light.  Anon a dale beneath [235]<br>  Open'd, and slow withdrew the misty veil<br>  That o'er her hamlets roofs and bowery trees<br>  Ting'd with a liquid azure the thin air.<br>  Along the winding path he roves, that none,<br>  Save feet habituate to its maze, could thread, [240]<br>  Heedless that here to Elidure's green home<br>  He came, unweeting visitant.  Within,<br>  Breathless, as though she listen'd in her sleep,<br>  Close to the door, as jealous lest some ear<br>  Earlier than her own should catch the sound [245]<br>  Of Elidure's returning tread, or voice<br>  Anticipate the welcome of her own,<br>  Reclin'd the bride, soft Evelene.  The step<br>  Up from the pillowing hand her flushing cheek<br>  Waken'd, or ere the threshold he o'erpast, [250]<br>  The form yet indistinct to her quick sight,<br>  Murmur'd her fond upbraiding.  "Truant Lord,<br>  Art thou too chang'd, thou too of midnight feast<br>  Enamour'd?  time hath been the rosy cup,<br>  Thou Saxon in thy revels, had look'd pale [255]<br>  To Evelen's cheek." -- 'Tis wretched solace, yet<br>  'Tis solace in the drear extreme of grief,<br>  To find one human heart whose deeper woe<br>  Makes weakness of our wailing.  Though alone<br>  Of the fray's dizzy tumult lay distinct [260]<br>  Elidure's image on the Wanderer's soul,<br>  His image as beneath the Saxon steel<br>  Dying, he struggled back to life from joy<br>  His stern friend to behold with fiery brand<br>  Piercing his path of flight, less bitter seem'd [265]<br>  His cup of woe, when from his sprang that bride,<br>  Nor knew him; knew him but no Elidure.<br>  Then sued for tidings, and with all her soul<br>  Listen'd, but could not hear, mistrusting all<br>  While yet but fearing, but when all assured, [270]<br>  Mistrusting even her fears, even then to hope<br>  Clinging with desperate energy of soul.<br>  Her Samor left in that dead night of mind,<br>  When madness were a comfort, all wild whirl,<br>  All dizzy hurry of rack'd sense were rich [275]<br>  Were rapturous to that blank and dismal void,<br>  When one incessant miserable thought<br>  Blends with the life, the being of the spirit.<br>  <br>  Him scared no Saxon clarion, the drear blast<br>  Winding of fleet pursuit; came o'er his soul [280]<br>  His own, his wedded Emerie, her babes<br>  Hushing, while greedily with ear and soul<br>  She drinks each sound the busy babbling fame<br>  Spreads on the wandering winds; the fleetest steed<br>  Of Elidure bestriding, still he moves [285]<br>  A tardy laggard to his soul's desire.<br>  Sedulous each throng'd haunt of man avoids<br>  His jealous speed, and still from town and tower<br>  Came blithely forth the jubilant hymns of peace;<br>  Still unextinguish'd their glad brilliance, wan'd [290]<br>  In morn's gray mists the yellow festal fires.<br>  <br>  Day pass'd, day sank, 'tis now the dewy eve,<br>  Beneath him, in the soft and silent light,<br>  Spread the fair Valleys, mead and flowery lawn<br>  With their calm verdure interspers'd allay [295]<br>  The forest's ponderous blackness, or retire<br>  Under the chequering umbrage of dim groves,<br>  Whose shadows almost slumber: far beyond<br>  Huge mountains, brightening in their secret glens,<br>  Their cold peaks bathe in the rich setting sun, [300]<br>  Sweeps through the midst broad Severn, deep and dark,<br>  His monarchy of waters, its full flow<br>  Still widening, as he scorn'd to bear the main<br>  Less tribute than a sea; or inland roll'd<br>  Ambitious ocean, of his tide to claim [305]<br>  The wealthy vassalage.  High on its marge<br>  Shone the Bright City, in her Roman pomp,<br>  Of bath, and theatre, and basilic,<br>  Smooth swelling dome, and spiring obelisk,<br>  Glittering like those more soft and sunny towns [310]<br>  That bask beneath the azure southern skies<br>  In marble majesty.  Silent she stands<br>  In the rich quiet of the golden light.<br>  The banner on her walls its cumbrous folds<br>  Droops motionless.  But Samor turn'd aloof, [315]<br>  Where lordly his fair dwelling's long arcade<br>  On its white shafts the tremulous glittering light<br>  Cherish'd, and starry with the river dews<br>  Its mantle of gay flowers, the odorous lawn<br>  Down sloped, as in the limpid stream to bathe. [320]<br>  <br>  No watch-dog, with glad bark and fawning joy,<br>  His Lord saluted.  Samor mark'd it not.<br>  No menial caught the slack rein from his hand.<br>  He heeded not.  No swift familiar step<br>  Forth started at his coming; face of joy [325]<br>  Brightened not -- vacant all; yet heeds he not.<br>  No infants, in their giddy, tottering speed,<br>  Clung round his knees.  So early at their rest,<br>  Thought the fond father.  Emeric's chamber door<br>  Stands open; he but paused his name to hear [330]<br>  Low mingled with her murmur'd orisons:<br>  All hush'd as in a tomb; perchance she sleeps,<br>  At his long absence heartsick.  He the folds<br>  Gently withdrawing of his nuptial bed,<br>  As with the amorous violence of his lips [335]<br>  To wake her to delicious fear, bends down.<br>  Cold, cold as marble, the forsaken bed<br>  Received the fervent pressure.  Back he sprung,<br>  And strange, like one that moveth in his sleep,<br>  Stood with loose arms and leaden listless gaze. [340]<br>  Unconscious, to the city walls, far seen<br>  From that high chamber, rove his eyes: behold<br>  Against the Sun's last light a wandering breeze<br>  Swells up the heavy banner; in the gleam<br>  The White Horse of the Saxon shakes his mane. [345]<br>  <br>  Then felt he the blank silence, then perceiv'd<br>  The tumult, and rude disarray that marr'd<br>  The face of his fair dwelling.  Forth he rush'd,<br>  As eager that his soul at one wild draught<br>  Might glut itself with perfect woe, all ill [350]<br>  Exhausted, laugh drain'd destiny to scorn.<br>  Cradle and infants couch with frantic hand<br>  Hurrying he explores, the sad chill void<br>  Almost delights.  Now on the river brink<br>  He watches yon huge forms that pace the walls, [355]<br>  Saxon their long black lances, Saxon helms<br>  Nod o'er their lofty brows terrific gloom.<br>  <br>  Lo!  at his feet, beneath a primrose bed,<br>  Half veil'd, and branching alder that o'er-droop'd<br>  Its dark green canopy, a slumbering child -- [360]<br>  If slumber might be call'd, that but o'erspread<br>  A wan disquiet o'er the wither'd cheek,<br>  Chok'd the thin breath that through the pallid lip<br>  Scarce struggled, clos'd not the soft sunken eye.<br>  Well Samor knew her, of his love first pledge, [365]<br>  First, playfullest, and gentlest: he but late<br>  Luxurious in the fulness of his woe,<br>  Clings to this 'lorn hope, like a drowning man,<br>  Not yet, not yet in this rude world alone.<br>  Lavish of fond officious zeal, he bathes [370]<br>  With water from the stream her marble brow,<br>  Chafes her; and with his own warm breath recalls<br>  The wandering life, that like a waning lamp<br>  Glimmer'd anon, then faded: but when slow<br>  Unfix'd her cold unmeaning eye regain'd [375]<br>  Brief consciousnesss, powerless her languid arm<br>  Down fell again, half lifted in his hair<br>  To wreathe as it was wont, with effort faint<br>  Strove her hard features for a woful smile:<br>  And the vague murmurs of her lips 'gan fall [380]<br>  Intelligible to his ear alone.<br>  "And thou art come -- too late -- yet thou art come," --<br>  He soothing her with hope, he knew most false,<br>  Slow modell'd from her broken faltering voice<br>  One sad continuous story. -- "'Twas at eve [385]<br>  We went to rest, I never slept so soft;<br>  Our mother lull'd us with assurance sweet<br>  Of thy returning. -- By and by I woke,<br>  But the bright morning was not shining fair,<br>  Nor the birds singing as they us'd.  I saw, [390]<br>  By a dim and dusky light, huge iron men<br>  With hair like fire, and their fierce voices spake<br>  Strange language: of my prayers I thought, and strove<br>  My eyes to close, still those grim-visag'd men<br>  Stood in the wavering darkness by the light [395]<br>  Of their blue weapons -- then they went away.<br>  I crept out to my mother's couch; she lay<br>  Asleep, but not as I have seen her sleep,<br>  When I have stol'n at morn to look on her,<br>  And thou hast laid me by her quiet side. [400]<br>  She shiver'd in her sleeping, and her skin<br>  Was chilly to the touch, yet, oh to sleep,<br>  Even as she did, I long'd; for they came back,<br>  Those shapes in all their darkness, all their light,<br>  Before their rugged faces I felt cold [405]<br>  As in the snow time; my eyes could not see,<br>  Oh, but I heard a dizzy sound, like shrieks<br>  Of many voices all at once.  I thought<br>  Rude hands were busy on my mother's couch,<br>  As though to bear her thence -- yet woke she not. [410]<br>  Oh Father, I have never look'd on death,<br>  But she was dead, I felt that she was dead.<br>  I could not breathe, yet from my thirsty throat<br>  My voice was bursting, but down o'er me fell<br>  The foldings of the couch -- long, long it seem'd, [415]<br>  Ere from that cumbrous weight I struggled forth,<br>  Then all was silent, all except the dash<br>  Of distant oars; I cried aloud, and heard<br>  But my own voice, I search'd, yet found I none;<br>  Not one in all these wide and lofty halls, [420]<br>  My mother, my sweet brothers gone, all gone.<br>  Almost I wish'd those fierce men might return<br>  To bear me too in their dread arms away.<br>  Hither I wander'd, for the river's sound<br>  Was joyous to the silence that came cold [425]<br>  Over my bosom, since the Sun hath shone,<br>  Yet it seem'd dark -- but oh, 'tis darker now,<br>  Darker, my Father, all within cold, cold.<br>  The soft warmth of thy lips no more can reach<br>  This shuddering in my breast -- yet kiss me still." -- [430]<br>  <br>  Vain, all in vain, that languid neck no more<br>  Rises to meet his fondness, that pale hand<br>  Drops from his shoulder, that wooed voice hath spent<br>  Its last of sweetness: wanted this alone<br>  That could enhance his agony, baffled hope. [435]<br>  <br>  Quiet and cool the deep tide at his feet<br>  Rolls with a tranquil murmur; one lone gleam<br>  Still lingering from the sunken Sun, beneath<br>  The moving surface, lightens its cold depth.<br>  How pleasant in its secret caves to quench [440]<br>  The soul, the body's fever; to cast off<br>  This restless, trembling consciousness, that clings<br>  Enamour'd to its anguish, sedulous<br>  To nurse its own disquiet: not to feel,<br>  Though cast by wandering waves on Emeric's grave;<br>  Though Saxon barks triumphant bound above, [446]<br>  To feel not, and have freedom though in death.<br>  For why this barren wilderness of earth<br>  Still haunt, man's pity, and the arch fiend's scoff?<br>  Why to the wearying wretchedness of life [450]<br>  Cling with a coward fondness? -- but a step<br>  To quiet -- to forgetfulness, a step.<br>  <br>  But alien to proud Samor those bad thoughts<br>  Startled his nature, burnt his soul with shame,<br>  That such unholy musings dare intrude [455]<br>  On its sad sanctity; upright he sprung;<br>  Oh, not in vain a Christian, with clench'd hand<br>  And inward rack convulsive of chok'd pain,<br>  Forc'd calmness to his brow: his hollow voice<br>  Wrought to a mournful fortitude. -- "Oh thou, [460]<br>  Glorious in thy prosperity of crime,<br>  Hengist, and thou that barter'st thy old fame<br>  For sweet lascivious chambering, hast unking'd<br>  Thy stately soul within the wreathing arms<br>  Of that fair Saxon, in loose dalliance soft [465]<br>  To steep the inebriate sense, on Samor's state<br>  Look, and be pale with envy; he dares stand<br>  Lofty beneath yon starry throne of God,<br>  And bless him, that his fate is scant and poor<br>  In joys like your's, by all your pomp, your bliss, [470]<br>  Made lovesick of his misery; still he feels<br>  The haughty solace of disdain; still soothes<br>  The madness of his grief by pitying you.<br>  Nor yet, oh impotent of cruelty,<br>  I am not utterly from this dark world [475]<br>  Estrang'd and outcast: gone, for ever gone,<br>  Those exquisite mild luxuries of the heart,<br>  That summer sunshine of the soul, sweet love,<br>  That makes life what we deem of heaven; remain<br>  Hardier delights, severer joys.  Oh reft [480]<br>  Of all thy brave, thy princely, of my faith,<br>  Thou hast a deeper need -- be thou my bride,<br>  Oh Britain, to thy wreck I proudly wed<br>  The sadness of my widowhood, and bid<br>  Pale bridesmaids to our nuptials, holy Wrath [485]<br>  And iron-handed Vengeance; and invoke<br>  Death, that dark minstrel from fast-slaughter'd mounds<br>  Of Saxons, to awake our bridal hymn,<br>  And spread for torchlight on our spousal eve<br>  Wild gratulation of their funeral fires. [490]<br>  <br>  "And thou, of stainless denizen of heaven,<br>  Soft soul of my lost Emeric, endure<br>  Though jealous my new bride from thee bereave<br>  The rude tumultuous day, the midnight hour<br>  I consecrate thee; then slide thou down, [495]<br>  Like moonlight on the darkness' raven wing,<br>  And oh, if human passion, human love,<br>  Stain the pure essence of immortal spirits,<br>  Leave heaven in heaven, earth's frailer loveliness<br>  Resuming, chaste mild fondness, timorous warmth, [500]<br>  Visit my desert fancy.  Him by day,<br>  Savage and merciless, with soul of steel,<br>  And pale brow cloudy with a nation's cares,<br>  Shall midnight find an amorous dreamer fond,<br>  A dotard on a dim unreal shade." [505]<br>  <br>  Now o'er what was the rosy, playful, warm,<br>  Now pale, now changeless, icy cold, the maid<br>  Whose blue eyes danc'd with rapture, whose light step<br>  Was consort to the air-roving winds (half seal'd<br>  That lustreless wan azure; stiff and damp [510]<br>  Those sprightly limbs) oft pausing as yet loath<br>  To part from what he shudder'd to behold,<br>  Heaps Samor the light earth; ere o'er her face<br>  He plac'd the primrose knot, once stoop'd his lips,<br>  And started to find cold what he knew dead. [515]<br>  <br>  Now closed that mournful office, nearing fast<br>  Is heard a dash of oars, and at his side<br>  Forth leap'd an armed Saxon, with rais'd arm<br>  Menacing; but Samor down with scornful strength<br>  The grim intruder dash'd to earth, and fix'd [520]<br>  His stern heel on his neck, and stood in act<br>  The life to trample from the grasping trunk.<br>  Sudden withdrawn his angry tread, he spake,<br>  "Thee first of Saxon race, thee last, this arm<br>  Spares, not of milky mercy, but as meet [525]<br>  To minister my purpose; go unscath'd,<br>  And tell to Hengist, tell thy Lord, who robs<br>  The Lion's den, should chain the Lion first;<br>  Add, Samor is abroad," -- Then to the boat<br>  He sprang, and pass'd to Severn's western shore. [530]<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK VI.</b> </h3>   A voice, o'er all the waste and prostate isle<br>  Wandereth a valiant voice; the hill, the dale,<br>  Forest and mountain, heath and ocean shore<br>  Treasure its mystic murmurs; all the winds<br>  From the bleak moody East to that soft gale [5]<br>  That wantons with the summer's dewy flowers,<br>  Familiar its dark burthen waft abroad.<br>  <br>  Is it an utterance of the earth? a sound<br>  From the green barrows of the ancient dead?<br>  Doth fierce Cassivelan's cold sleep disdain [10]<br>  That less than Cæsar with a master's step<br>  Walk his free Britain?  Doth thy restless grave,<br>  Bonduca, to the slavish air burst ope,<br>  And thou, amid the laggard cars of war,<br>  Cry, "Harness and away!"  But far and wide, [15]<br>  As when from marish dank, or quaking fen,<br>  Venomous and vast the clouds uproll, and spread<br>  Pale pestilence along the withering land,<br>  So sweeps o'er all the isle his wasting bands<br>  The conqueror Saxon; he, far worse, far worse [20]<br>  His drear contagion, that the body's strength<br>  Wastes, and with feverish pallor overlays<br>  The heaven-shap'd features; this the nobler soul,<br>  With slavery's base sickliness attaints,<br>  Making man's life more hideous than his death. [25]<br>  Thames rolls a Saxon tide; in vain delays<br>  Deep Severn on Plinlimmon's summits rude<br>  His narrow freedom, tame anon endures<br>  Saxon dominion: high with arms uplift,<br>  As he had march'd o'er necks of prostrate kings, [30]<br>  Caswallon on the southern shore of Trent<br>  Drives onward, he nought deeming won, while aught<br>  Remains unwon.  But still that wonderous voice,<br>  Like vulture in the grisly wake of war,<br>  Hovers, and flings on air his descant strange, [35]<br>  "Vengeance and Vigilance!" -- in van, in rear,<br>  Around, above, beneath, the clouds of Heaven<br>  Enshroud it in their misty folds; earth speaks<br>  From all her caves, "Vengeance and Vigilance!"<br>  Aye, at that sound the Briton crest assumes [40]<br>  High courage and heroic shame, he wears<br>  With such bold mien his slavery, he might seem<br>  Lord over fortune, and with calm disdain<br>  He locks his fetters, like proud battle arms.<br>  Without a foe o'er this wide land of foes [45]<br>  Marcheth the Saxon.  City, tower, and fort<br>  On their harsh hinge roll back their summon'd gates,<br>  With such a sullen and reluctant jar,<br>  Submission seems defiance.  Though to fear<br>  Impassive, scarce the Victor dare unfurl [50]<br>  Banner of conquest on the jealous air.<br>  Less perilous were frantic strife, were wrath<br>  Desperate of life, and blind to death, wild hate<br>  Of being struck all heedless so it strike,<br>  Than this high haughty misery, that fierce woe [55]<br>  Baffles by brave endurance, and confronts<br>  With cold and stern contentedness all ill,<br>  Outrage, and insult, ravage, rape, and wreck,<br>  That dog barbaric Conquerors march of war.<br>  'Tis like the sultry silence, ushering forth [60]<br>  The thunder's cloudy chariot, rather like<br>  The murky smothering of volcanic fire<br>  Within its rocky prison; forth anon<br>  Bursts the red captive, to the lurid heaven<br>  Upleaps, and with its surging dome of smoke [65]<br>  Shuts from the pale world the meridian Sun.<br>  But in their camp, in fierce divan and full,<br>  The lordly robbers sate, assemblage proud,<br>  Ethling, and Erle, and King, for council met,<br>  For council and carousal; so they deem'd [70] [NOTE 11]<br>  The drunken sense would hardier daring grasp,<br>  And the bold revel of the blood, the soul<br>  Flush to more noble valiance, strong desire<br>  In fierce embrace to meet that mistress dark,<br>  Danger: Hoarse din of merriment, the air [75]<br>  Smote with meet music blending loud and deep.<br>  <br>  But Horsa lighting with disdainful mirth<br>  His broad bright eye, 'gan scoff with rugged jest.<br>  "Ill have we done, though for one sumptuous feast<br>  Be our's this spacious isle, ill have we done; -- [80]<br>  That in our prodigal and heedless waste<br>  Of those tall high-born Britons spared we none<br>  To tilt at with our thirsty spears, and scare<br>  The frost and slumber from our sluggish hearts.<br>  Now hang we forth our banners to disport [85]<br>  In the smooth breeze, our armours steeled clasps<br>  To summons soft of Lady's tender hands<br>  Surrender; or go joust the hardy oaks<br>  For pastime.  Oh, along these velvet plains<br>  To prance 'mid timorous hinds with their pale souls [90]<br>  In their white faces, heralds crouching low,<br>  With looks beseeching, voices meek, clasp'd hands;<br>  'Tis tame and wearisome as at dead noon<br>  To rock upon the flat and lazy sea."<br>  <br>  "This too," cried hoary Cerdic; "this bright sword<br>  Loathes its long Christian fast, yet not despairs [96]<br>  Erewhile to glut with banquet rich and full<br>  Its ravening blade; for trust me, fiery Erle,<br>  Many a fierce steed hath brook'd the brazen curb,<br>  That chaf'd anon, from his high seat to dust [100]<br>  Hath shaken his pale rider; Erle, I read<br>  In yon bow'd foreheads sterner characters<br>  Than abject, tame allegiance, homage base:<br>  There the firm purpose, meditation deep,<br>  And study of revenge; the wand of peace [105]<br>  Is in their hands, but in their souls they grasp<br>  The battle-axe and spear." -- A bitter laugh<br>  Came with the fierce reply, "Shall Horsa watch<br>  The shiftings in the visage of a slave;<br>  I issue forth my mandate, and 'tis done, [110]<br>  Whether with cloudy or with sunshine brow<br>  I know not and regard not." -- Cerdic's voice,<br>  Ruffled to somewhat of prophetic tone:<br>  <br>  "Not, Horsa, to the stones, the deaf dull stones,<br>  Nor the cold current of the senseless winds [115]<br>  Speaks that wild orator, the Man, whose paths<br>  Are hidden as the ways of fate, unknown<br>  Who knoweth all, who seeth all unseen,<br>  Nor like the lightning shaft his presence dread<br>  Divulgeth, but to shatter, but to slay. [120]<br>  Whose breath beneath the soft dove's snowy down<br>  A soul might breathe of valour to outsoar<br>  The falcon's pitch of pride: I tell thee, Erle,<br>  This soft effeminate Britain, to our sway<br>  Gentle and pliant as a willow wand, [125]<br>  Will that dark Man uprear a ponderous Mace<br>  To crush our infant empire." -- "Man! hath man<br>  Curdled the blood of Offa, made his soul<br>  Patient of that pale trembling motion, fear,<br>  And Offa live, live shameless of his shame, [130]<br>  Amid his peers with unblench'd front to say,<br>  These knees have quail'd, these stubborn joints have felt<br>  The aspin's coward fluttering, and the Sun<br>  That saw his flight, hath seen not his revenge.<br>  Cerdic, the name of perishable man [135]<br>  Thou dost belie, so titling beings dim;<br>  Viewless and formless denizens of air,<br>  That sport and dally with the human shape<br>  Making of mortals of their mortal peers,<br>  Dark things of doubt and danger.  We had sworn, [140]<br>  Gurmund and Sigvart, Ælla, Attilar,<br>  And other six, than whom no German arm<br>  Sways heavier the long lance, nor German foot<br>  Treads firmer battle's crimson paths, I speak,<br>  Fiery-soul'd Horsa, to thy front; to thine, [145]<br>  High-sceptred Hengist! mortal steel we swore<br>  Should choke that full-voic'd Wanderer's clamorous breath.<br>  Sage oath!  as to adjure our souls, and vow<br>  Th' irregular mad ocean our word "Peace"<br>  Should hearken, and sleek smooth his cresting waves.<br>  But gaily went we forth with brand and bow, [151]<br>  Like hunters to the chase, scoffing our prey.<br>  'Now if he meet us in his mortal shape,<br>  Let him melt back into his native air;<br>  Then shall he scape' -- high o'er our path a rock [155]<br>  Hung beetling, from its summit came a voice,<br>  'Behold him!' -- with the voice a fragment vast,<br>  An earthquake had been weak to hurl it forth;<br>  Two stately necks to the low earth sank down,<br>  And o'er them that huge mass lay stern and still, [160]<br>  Like an old giant's monument.  But we<br>  Leap'd onward, Ælla met the dark unknown,<br>  Heavy with ruin hung his arm in air,<br>  But in his valiant heart a javelin stood,<br>  Drinking the crimson life.  Still on we swept, [165]<br>  Many a wild league o'er moor and marish swamp,<br>  Forest and wold, and still our pathway lay<br>  O'er the warm corpses of our foremost peers.<br>  Sole, sad survivors of our host, we came,<br>  Sigvart and Offa; on the giddy brink [170]<br>  Of precipice abrupt the Conqueror paus'd,<br>  As weary with his prowess, our defeat,<br>  To mock us with the calmness of his rest.<br>  "Now come what will," cried Sigvart, "come what may,<br>  Or thou, or I, or both." -- Then on he sprung, [175]<br>  Yet not the more relax'd that shape of gloom<br>  Its stern contemptuous quiet, wav'd his arm<br>  With motion less of strife than proud command,<br>  And then of Sigvart's fall the deep abyss<br>  Sent up a hollow sound.  I fled, proud Peers, [180]<br>  I say again, I fled, and, or disdain'd<br>  That being dark a lone and single foe:<br>  Or by the shielding of our mightier Gods<br>  I 'scap'd -- "I too (cried Hermingard), I too<br>  Of that mysterious Wanderer have known [185]<br>  The might and savage mercy.  I had stray'd<br>  Into a fabric fair, of Christian Gods,<br>  A fane it seem'd, rich-crested pillars rang'd<br>  On either side, above the hollow roof<br>  Aye lessening, seem'd to melt into the air [190]<br>  On which it floated. -- High uprear'd there shone<br>  An altar, bright with chalice, lamp, and cup<br>  All of the flaming gold.  I rush'd to seize,<br>  An arm was on my neck, that dash'd me down<br>  Like a soft infant; then a vengeful voice [195]<br>  Struck on my dizzy hearing. -- "But thy blood<br>  Would dye this holy pavement with foul stain,<br>  Heathen, thy soul and mortal shape were rent<br>  Asunder." -- As I fled, I turn'd -- reclin'd<br>  Low by that altar on his knees, all quench'd [200]<br>  Fierce wrath and fiery menace, drooping all<br>  Stern pride of mastery, triumph, and high scorn<br>  That wild Unknown, calm, not with weariness;<br>  Gentle, but not with sleep.  Majestic light<br>  Beam'd on the quiet of his heavenward brow, [205]<br>  Yet human tears stood glittering in his eyes.<br>  My thoughts were vengeance, but the cold clear air<br>  Went creeping up my veins, an awful frost<br>  Drank up the languid current of my blood,<br>  And unrevenged I fled that tranquil Man." [210]<br>  <br>  Upsprang young Abisa, and beauteous scorn<br>  Curl'd his smooth cheek --"in tumult or in calm;<br>  But have he blood within his beating veins,<br>  Mine is a steel of such a searching thirst,<br>  'Twill drain its crimson source." "Thou! wanton Boy,"<br>  The pale laugh wrinkling on his swelling lip. [216]<br>  "Thou! thou! (cried Offa) with thy mother's milk<br>  Yet white within thy beardless cheek." -- "Proud Jute,<br>  The stem of Woden is a mounting tree,<br>  Its saplings soar to meet the golden Sun, [220]<br>  While tamer shrubs creep with base trail on earth.<br>  Hengist, my King, my Brother!  by our Sire<br>  I swear, that ne'er again metheglin cup<br>  Shall sparkle on these lips, till I have met<br>  This mystic deity of Offa's fear." [225]<br>  <br>  Then on the Monarch turn'd all eyes; he sate<br>  In darkness, or by chance, or art the lamps<br>  Stream'd bright and yellow down the festal board<br>  But fell no ray within his folded robe.<br>  Yet wore not Hengist on his brow his soul, [230]<br>  High spake he from its cold and stately calm,<br>  Law to the lawless, to the dauntless dread;<br>  But his were rarer qualities of power,<br>  Dominion o'er himself; deep, deep within<br>  Dwelt all the stormy passions; by no eye [235]<br>  Pierc'd in its dark abiding lay the spirit<br>  With all its shames and grandeurs, loves and hates,<br>  And all its greedy family of lusts.<br>  Though now there seem'd beneath his royal crown<br>  A faint uncertain paleness, as of fear [240]<br>  Not wholly quell'd, and on his cheek and lip<br>  Hover'd a quivering motion, ere he spake,<br>  But cool his speech. -- "Presumptuous youth, thy oath<br>  Though wild, is holy -- Woden guard thee well.<br>  Yet art thou sole in madness?  time hath been [245]<br>  When the brave phrenzy of rash daring spread<br>  A broad contagious flame through all our camp;<br>  Till not a sword but sham'd its sluggish sheath,<br>  Needed not Saxon king, as now, to gild<br>  Fair danger ere it please'd, as now proclaim [250]<br>  Rich guerdon to the warrior, that aspires<br>  To rival Woden's blood, and be the peer<br>  Of Abisa in peril and renown.<br>  More lofty duties fetter thee and me,<br>  High Horsa" -- (for the fiery warrior's hand [255]<br>  Had started to his sword's familiar hilt)<br>  Rob we not of their fame the valiant Erles."<br>  <br>  No seat was vacant, not a voice came forth,<br>  As he were single in his shame sate each,<br>  Nor dared on his compeers to look, in fear [260]<br>  Soul might be there more dauntless than his own.<br>  Blank silence all!  but loud that silence spake.<br>  Not vainly, Samor, worn thy title proud,<br>  Avenger!  by thy country's Conquerors thou<br>  Magnificently deified; so soar'd [265]<br>  Thy mortal virtue o'er their tamer Gods.<br>  Not that the vassal elements thy sway<br>  Hearken'd, nor beings of the middle air<br>  Stoop'd on their glistening wings to work thy will.<br>  Avenger! but for thee, the Almighty wrought [270]<br>  Most marv'lous, most mirac'lous; in thy soul,<br>  That nobler field, high wonders manifold<br>  Laboured to light and lustre: for what thought<br>  Unwing'd by inbreath'd Godhead e'er might dream<br>  Of glory to be born from this broad night [275]<br>  Of desolation and deep darkness, strive<br>  For faint, impalpable, and airy good,<br>  Through the thick clouds of evil and of woe,<br>  Strong, stately constant, like an eagle set<br>  To drink the last light of the parting sun? [280]<br>  What heart of earthly clay, that ne'er imbib'd<br>  Holier and purer ether, might endure<br>  Danger, dismay, despair, all ills, that wring<br>  Within, and rack and rankle?  not alone<br>  Fierce wrong and insult of triumphant foe, [285]<br>  But worse, far worse, from those our friends misdeem'd,<br>  Pity of calm, cold cowards, or rude scorn<br>  From sleek and smiling slaves; or scoff and mock<br>  At thy hard sufferings from those ingrate hearts<br>  For whom thou suffer'st; these the woes that wait [290]<br>  That nobly desperate, who with stedfast hand<br>  The statue of his country's fame, down dash'd<br>  And trampled by barbarian feet, ingrain'd<br>  With the coarse dust and black, before the world<br>  Would rear again to sov'reignty and state. [295]<br>  But thou didst strive and suffer, thou didst hope,<br>  And therefore in thy dark and silent deeds<br>  Beam'd manifest God's Spirit; till in thee<br>  Even the base body that e'er clogs and clouds<br>  The nobler energies, its state infirm [300]<br>  Shook off, and by communion close assum'd<br>  The soul's immortal essence, or the soul<br>  A climate and peculiar atmosphere<br>  Spread round its weaker instrument of power.<br>  Hence human accidents of heat and cold, [305]<br>  Famine and thirst, wasting and weariness,<br>  Fell light and thin upon thy tranquil frame,<br>  Like flakes of snow upon th' unbroken lake;<br>  Thus didst thou pass most fearless, and most fear'd; [310]<br>  By virtue, and thy foeman's dread, array'd<br>  In attributes of strong divinity;<br>  Danger became thy safety, thy renown<br>  Grew from thy utter desperate wretchedness.<br>  <br>  But now the more enjoy'd that Saxon youth<br>  His solitude of glory; forth he springs [315]<br>  Hasty, lest valorous repentance fire<br>  Some rival Erle of half his peril yet<br>  To wrong him.
