[100]<br>  Evil is not, where man no evil wills,<br>  And good is not, where will not man and God."<br>  <br>  "Chief wise as brave, as to our feeble sight<br>  Yon pebble's slight circumference, the Past,<br>  The Present, and the Future of this world [105]<br>  Are to the All-seeing vision; oft doth Heaven<br>  In sign and symbol duskily reveal<br>  The unborn future; oft Fate's chariot wheels<br>  Are harbinger'd by voices that proclaim<br>  The fashion of their coming; gifted Seers [110]<br>  Feel on their lips articulate the deeds<br>  Of later days, and dim oracular sights<br>  Crowd the weak eyes, till pall'd attention faint<br>  To dizziness." -- "Oh, Merlin, time hath been<br>  When in the guilty cities the Lord's voice [115]<br>  Hath spoken by his Prophets, hath made quail<br>  By apparitions ominous and dire<br>  Strong empires on their unassailed height.<br>  But oh, for us of this devoted isle,<br>  Drench'd with the vials of Almighty wrath, [120]<br>  To gaze up, and beseech the clouds to rain<br>  Bright miracles on this poor speck of earth."<br>  <br>  "Shame choke thy speech, despondent slanderer! thee<br>  Avenger! this from thee! Away! my lips<br>  Burn with the fire of heaven, my heart flows o'er [125]<br>  With gladness and with glory.  Peerless Isle,<br>  How dost thou sit amid thy blue domain<br>  Of ocean, like a sceptred Queen!  The bonds<br>  Like flax have wither'd from thy comely limbs.<br>  Thou, the strong freedom of thy untam'd locks [130]<br>  Shaking abroad, adornest God's fair world.<br>  Thou noblest Eden of man's fallen state,<br>  Apart and sever'd from the common earth,<br>  Even like a precious jewel, deep and far<br>  In the abyss of time thy dawn of pride<br>  Still with a fuller and more constant blaze<br>  Grows to its broad meridian, and Time's rolls<br>  Are silent of thy setting.  Oh, how fair<br>  The steps of freemen in thy vales of peace;<br>  Thy broad towns teem with wealth, thy yellow fields<br>  Laugh in their full fertility; thy bays [141]<br>  Whiten and glisten with thy myriad barks.<br>  The Angels love thee, and the airs of heaven<br>  Are gladden'd by thy holy hymns, while Faith<br>  Sits on thy altars, like a nestling dove, [145]<br>  In unattainted snowyness of plume."<br>  <br>  "Now, by my soul, thou strange and solemn Man.<br>  Mistrust thee more I dare not; be't a dream<br>  Or revelation of immortal truth,<br>  Of Britain's fame I cannot choose but hear [150]<br>  With a child's transport." -- Then the Prophet shook<br>  The dark profusion of his swelling hair<br>  With a stern triumph; then his aged eye<br>  Grew restless with delight: his thin white hand<br>  Closing around the Baron's arm, lay there [155]<br>  Like a hard glove of steel.  He led him on,<br>  Till now the black and shaggy pass spread out<br>  To a green quiet valley, after named<br>  The Bed of Gelert, that too-faithful hound<br>  Slain fondly by his erring Lord: the stream [160]<br>  Here curl'd more wanton, lightly wafting down<br>  The last thin golden leaves the alders dropt,<br>  Like fairy barges skimming the blue waves.<br>  That stream o'erpass'd, rightward their silent way<br>  Lay to the foot of Snowdon.  Pause was none, [165]<br>  They front the steep ascent, and upward wind<br>  A long, sheer, toilsome path, their footfalls struck<br>  Upon the black bare stillness, audible<br>  As in thick forest the lone woodman's axe.<br>  'Twas strange, yet slack'd not that old reverend Man<br>  His upward step, as though the mountain air [171]<br>  Were his peculiar element, still his breath<br>  Respir'd unlabouring, lively bounded on<br>  His limbs, late slow and tremulous.  Three long hours,<br>  Now front to front upon that topmost peak,<br>  Erwydfa, sit they motionless, alone:<br>  As when two vultures on some broken tower,<br>  That beetles o'er a dismal battle field,<br>  In dark and greedy patience ruminate<br>  Their evening feast, a stillness as of sleep [180]<br>  Heaves in their ruffled plumes, their deep bright eyes<br>  Half clos'd in languid rest; so undisturb'd,<br>  So lofty, sate the Avenger and the Seer.<br>  The atmosphere, that palls our restless world,<br>  Lay coiling in its murky folds below: [185]<br>  So in some regal theatre, when droops<br>  The unfolding curtain, and within it shrouds<br>  The high disastrous passions, crimes, and woes<br>  Erewhile that fretted on its pomp of scene;<br>  Thus Earth, with all its solemn tragedies, [190]<br>  Heroic vauntings, sumptuous imagings,<br>  Set in its veil of darkness from their sight.<br>  The filmless, the pellucid heaven above<br>  One broad pure sheet of sunlight. -- "Gifted Man,<br>  (Cried Samor,) wherefore to this desolate [195]<br>  Untrodden!" -- "Ha! untrodden! know ye not,<br>  Where course humanity defiles not, there<br>  The snowy-footed Angels lightly skim<br>  Their taintless soil, the fragrance of their plumes<br>  Fans the pure air where chokes no breath of sin [200]<br>  The limpid current?  Desolate!  the motes<br>  That flicker in the sun are few and rare<br>  To the immortal faces that smile down<br>  Exquisite transport on the ravish'd sense.<br>  Here, from their kindred elements, emanate [205]<br>  The festive creatures of the heavenly fields,<br>  Glories, and Mercies, and Beatitudes;<br>  Some dropping on the silent summer dews,<br>  Some trembling on the rainbow's violet verge,<br>  Some rarely charioteering on the wings [210]<br>  Of the mild winds, in moonlight some.  Why shakes<br>  The Man of Vengeance?  wherefore of mine hand<br>  This passionate wringing?" -- "Tell me, truely tell;<br>  The name of Emeric, from some mild-lipp'd tone<br>  Hath it e'er trembled on thine ear?  Old Man, [215]<br>  Is't sin to say her presence might adorn<br>  That gentle company?" -- "To souls like thine,<br>  Warrior, Heaven grants sweet intercourse and free<br>  With its beatified." -- "Ah, now thou rak'st<br>  The ashes of a buried grief: gone all, [220]<br>  My gentle visitations broken off,<br>  My delicate discoursings silent, ceas'd!<br>  Oh, I talk idly, Prophet, speak thou on."<br>  <br>  "Aye, Warrior, and of mild and soft no more;<br>  Grandeurs there are, to which the gates of heaven [225]<br>  Set wide their burnish'd portals: midnight feels<br>  Cherubic splendours ranging her dun gloom,<br>  The tempests are ennobled by the state<br>  Of high seraphic motion.  I have seen,<br>  I, Merlin, have beheld.  It stood in light, [230]<br>  It spake in sounds for earth's gross winds too pure.<br>  Between the midnight and the morn 'twas here<br>  I lay, I know not if I slept of woke,<br>  Yet mine eyes saw.  Long, long this heart had yearn'd,<br>  'Mid those rich passings and majestic shows [235]<br>  For shape distinct, and palpable clear sound.<br>  It burst at length, yea, front to front it stood,<br>  The Immortal Presence.  I clench'd up the dust<br>  In the agony and rapture of my fear,<br>  And my soul wept with terror and deep joy. [240]<br>  It stood upon the winds, an Angel plumed,<br>  And mail'd and crown'd; his plumes cast forth a tinge<br>  Like blood on th' air around: his arms, in shape<br>  Etherial panopoly complete, in hue<br>  The moonlight on the dark Llanberis lake, [245]<br>  A bright blue rippling glitter; for the crown,<br>  Palm leaves of orient light his brown enwreath'd,<br>  That bloom'd in fair divinity of wrath,<br>  And beautiful relentlessness austere.<br>  Knowledge was in my heart, and on my lips; [250]<br>  I felt him, who he was. -- "Archangel!  hail,<br>  Destroyer!  art not thou God's Delegate,<br>  To break the glassy glories of this world?<br>  The gem-knosp'd diadem, the ivory ball,<br>  Sceptre and sword, imperial mantle broad, [255]<br>  The Lord of Nations, Thundershaft of war,<br>  Are glorious on the pale submissive earth:<br>  Thou com'st, and lo, for throne, for sword, for king,<br>  Bare ashes and thin dust.  Thou art, that aye<br>  The rich tower'd cities smoulder'st to pale heaps [260]<br>  Of lazy moss-stones, and aye after thee<br>  Hoots Desolation like a dank-wing'd owl<br>  Upon the marble palaces of Kings.<br>  Thou wert, when old Assyrian Niniveh<br>  Sank to a pool of waters, waste and foul; [265]<br>  Thou, when the Median's brow the massy tiar<br>  Let fall, and when the Grecian's brazen throne<br>  Sever'd and split to the four winds; and now<br>  Consummatest thy work of wreck and scorn,<br>  Even on Rome's Caesars, making the earth sick [270]<br>  Of its own hollowness.  Archangel!  Hail,<br>  Vicegerent of destruction, Cupbearer,<br>  That pour'st the bitter liquor of Heaven's wrath,<br>  A lamentable homage pay I thee,<br>  And sue thee tell if Britain's days are full, [275]<br>  Her lips for thy sad beverage ripe.  Thereat<br>  Earthward his sunny spear its lurid point<br>  Declin'd, and lo, a White Horse, through the land<br>  Ranging in stately speed; our city gates<br>  Shrunk open at his coming, our fair fields [280]<br>  Wither'd before him, so his fiery breath<br>  Flar'd broad amazement through the gasping land.<br>  Triumph was in the trampling of his feet,<br>  And the strong joy of mockery, for he trod<br>  On broken principalities; his mane [285]<br>  Familiar Conquest, as a rushing wind,<br>  Fann'd in loose brilliant streamings." -- "False-lipp'd Seer,<br>  Thou spak'st of gladness, and thy ominous tone<br>  Is darkness and dismay." -- "Hark, Warrior, hark:<br>  That wanton mane was trail'd down to the dust, [290]<br>  That fiery trampling falter'd to dull dread,<br>  That pale victorious steed Thee, Thee I saw,<br>  Visible as thou stand'st, with mastering arm<br>  Drag down, and on his strong and baffled neck<br>  Full trod thy iron-sandal'd heel.  The sight [295]<br>  Was wine unto my soul, and I laugh'd out,<br>  And mock'd the ruinous Seraph in the clouds.<br>  <br>  "Yet stood he in the quiet of his wrath,<br>  Angelic Expectation, that awaits<br>  Calmly till God accomplish God's high will, [300]<br>  Full on his brow.  Then stoop'd the spear again,<br>  And lo, Seven Steeds, like that pale One, bestrode<br>  The patient Isle, and they that on them rode<br>  Wore diadem and regal pall; then rose<br>  To war against those royal riders fierce, [305]<br>  From a round table, Knights in sunlike arms,<br>  Shields bossy with rich impress quaint, and fair<br>  Their coursers, as the fire-hoof'd steeds of Morn.<br>  To white-arm'd Ladies in a stately court<br>  Bards hymn'd the deeds of that fine chivalry, [310]<br>  And their crown'd Captain's title smote mine ear,<br>  <br>  Arthur of Bretagne." -- Years went rolling on,<br>  Cloudy, discordant, and temptestuous years,<br>  For the sword reap'd the harvest of the land,<br>  And battle was the may-game of her sons. [315]<br>  And lo, a Raven o'er the Eastern sea<br>  Swoop'd desolation on the Isle; her wings<br>  Blasted wheree'er they wav'd, the earth wept blood<br>  In her foul talons gripe.  But he that rode<br>  On the White Steed, the Sovereign of the Land, [320]<br>  (Patience, Avenger, patience!) fair was he,<br>  That Sovereign, as the virgin's spring-tide dream,<br>  Holy as new anointed Christian Priest,<br>  Valiant as warrior burnish'd for the fight,<br>  Fond and extatic, as love-dreaming Bard, [325]<br>  Solemn and wise, as old Philosopher,<br>  Stately, as kingborn lion in the wood;<br>  As he his fine face heavenward turn'd in prayer,<br>  The Angels bent down from their throning clouds,<br>  To wonder at that admirable King, [330]<br>  Sky-wandering voices peal'd in transport out --<br>  'Alfred!' the baffled Raven cower'd aloof,<br>  The isle look'd up to heaven in peace and joy.<br>  <br>  "Still stood he there, betwixt me and the sun,<br>  Th' Archangel; not in sleep, nor senselessness [335]<br>  Absorb'd, but terrible inaction spread<br>  Over his innate menace.  Oh, I strove,<br>  Yet dared not hope the dregs of wrath were drain'd,<br>  The mission of dismay fulfill'd and done;<br>  Yet had those wings of fatal hue droop'd down [340]<br>  In folded motionlessness, wreathy light<br>  Had crept and wound around the dusky spear,<br>  Silvering its perilous darkness.  Dropt at once<br>  That tender light away; at once those wings<br>  Started asunder, and spread wide and red [345]<br>  The rain of desolation, thicker roll'd<br>  The pedestal of clouds whereon he stood,<br>  As to bear up the effort of his wrath.<br>  Again the Eastern Raven snuff'd our air,<br>  The frantic White Horse laved his hoofs in blood, [350]<br>  Till from the Southern Continent sprung forth<br>  A Leopard, on the ocean shore he ramp'd.<br>  Woe to the White Horse, to the Raven woe,<br>  Woe for the title of the Leopard Lord,<br>  The Conqueror!  and a Bell I heard, that sway'd [355]<br>  Along the isle, and froze it into peace<br>  With its majestic tyranny of sound.<br>  <br>  "But he, upon the air, th' Archangel, he,<br>  The summons of whose eye from climes remote<br>  Beckon'd those grisly ministers of wrath, [360]<br>  Northward he look'd, no northern ruin came.<br>  To th' East, there all was still.  The South, nor shape<br>  Nor sound.  The West, calm stretch'd th' unruffled sea.<br>  Ha! thought I, earth hath now no ruin more,<br>  The race of havock in extinct for us, [665]<br>    Angel of wreck away!  thy task is o'er;<br>  Majestic Mischief, from our isle away!<br>  <br>  He went not; as an earthquake's second shock,<br>  With dreary longing watch'd I what might come;<br>  Moments were years; and lo, the Island's sons [370]<br>  Nor Briton they, nor Saxon, nor the stock<br>  Of those new comers, but from each had flow'd<br>  All qualities of honour and renown,<br>  The foul dishonest dregs had fum'd away,<br>  And the rich quintessence, unmix'd, unsoil'd, [375]<br>  An harmony of energies sublime,<br>  Knit in that high-brow'd people.  Courtesy,<br>  Death-scorning valour, Fame's immortal thirst,<br>  And honour inbreath'd like the life of life.<br>  <br>  Then rose that strong Archangel, and he smote [380]<br>  The bosom of the land; at once leap'd up<br>  That mighty people.  Here a Snow-white Rose,<br>  And there a Red, with a fatal blossoming,<br>  And deadly fragrance, maddening all the land.<br>  I heard, I saw -- ah, impious sights and sounds! [385]<br>  Two war-cries in one tongue, two banner-rolls<br>  Wov'n in one loom, two lances from one forge,<br>  Two children from one womb in conflict met;<br>  'Gainst brother brother's blood cried out to heaven,<br>  And he that rent the vizor of his foe, [390]<br>  Look'd through the shatter'd bars, and saw his son.<br>  Ha Britain! in thine entrails dost thou flesh<br>  Thy ravine!  thy Baronial castles blaze<br>  With firebrands from their hospitable hearths.<br>  <br>  "Mercy, I cried aloud, thou Merciless! [395]<br>  Destroy no more, Destroyer!  Prone I fell,<br>  And hid mine aching eyes deep in the dust;<br>  So from my rocking memory to shut out<br>  Those wars unnatural.  Pass'd a sound at length<br>  As of a Wild Boar hunted to his death; [400]<br>  I raised mine head, still there the Archangel stood;<br>  Another pause, another gleam of hope;<br>  But in that quiet interval me-seem'd<br>  Trumpetings as of victory from the sea,<br>  Flow'd o'er the Isle, and glories beam'd abroad [405]<br>  From a triumphal throne, where sate elate<br>  A Virgin: all around her Poets harps<br>  Strew'd flowers of amaranth blooming; and methought<br>  Was joy and solemn welcoming in heaven<br>  Of a pure incense, that from all the Isle [410]<br>  Soar'd to the unapproached throne of God.<br>  <br>  "Then saw I through the Isle, a River broad<br>  And full, and they that drank thereof look'd up<br>  Like children dropt forth from a nobler world,<br>  So powerful that proud water work'd within, [415]<br>  Freshening the body and the soul: and each<br>  Beauty array'd and a frank simple strength.<br>  The river's name was Freedom: her fair tide<br>  So pleasant thrall'd mine eye, I saw not rise<br>  Th' Archangel's spear: th' earth's reeling work me then,<br>  For lo, upon a throne, a gallant Prince, [421]<br>  That with misguided sceptre strove to check<br>  That powerful stream: whereat the rebel tide<br>  Swell'd up with indignation, and aloof<br>  Stood gathering its high-cresting waves; down came [425]<br>  The deluge, that fair throne, and all its strong<br>  Nobility of pillars, with a crash<br>  Came to the earth, while they that drank rush'd out<br>  Inebriate with excess of that fierce stream,<br>  And cast a bloody sacrifice, that head [430]<br>  Endiadem'd with royalty, to glut<br>  The tide implacable.  'Tis sad to hear,<br>  Aye Samor, what was it to see!  Brave Chief,<br>  Cold winter leads the pleasant summer on,<br>  The night must darken ere the morning dawn; [435]<br>  The summer came, the morning dawn'd, I saw<br>  The arch'd heavens open o'er the angelic shape,<br>  And upward like a cloud he mingled in<br>  To the sky's cloudiness.  I cried aloud<br>  'For ever!' the close settling in the heavens [440]<br>  Seem'd to reply 'For ever.'  Not with him<br>  Pass'd off my vision fair.  Another throne<br>  Stood by the venturous margin of that stream;<br>  Then merriment, and loose-harp'd wantonness<br>  Smooth'd the late ruffled air; immodest tones, [445]<br>  To which fair forms in dancing motion swam:<br>  They paus'd, then dark around that throne it seem'd,<br>  Whereat those holy hymns that scarce had ceas'd<br>  To float up in their airy-winged course,<br>  In faintness 'gan to tremble and break off; [450]<br>  That stream again upgather'd its wak'd wrath,<br>  And foamy menace.  When behold, a fleet<br>  Came tilting o'er the ocean waves, and cast<br>  A Lady and a Warrior on the shore,<br>  And kingly crowns around their brows august [455]<br>  Out blossom'd; on the throne they took their seat,<br>  Soar'd gladness on the wings of those pure hymns,<br>  And the majestic stream in sunlight flow<br>  And full rejoicing murmur, all its waves<br>  Wafted around the high and steady throne. [460]<br>  <br>  Now listen with thy soul, not with thine ears,<br>  Briton!  beside that stream a Tree sprang out,<br>  With ever-mounting height, and amplitude<br>  Aye-spreading; deep in earth its gnarled roots<br>  Struck down, as though to strengthen this frail world:<br>  Its crown amid the clouds seem'd soaring up [466]<br>  For calm above earth's tossing and rude stir,<br>  And its broad branching spread so wide, its shade<br>  Lay upon distant realms; one golden bright,<br>  Close by the cradle of the infant sun, [470]<br>  And others in new western worlds remote;<br>  And from that mystic river, Freedom, flow'd<br>  A moisture like the sap of life, that fed<br>  And fertilized the spacious Tree: the gales<br>  Of ocean with a gorgeous freshness flush'd [475]<br>  The beauty of its foliage.  Blossoms rare<br>  Were on it; holy deeds, that in the airs<br>  Of heaven delicious smelt, and fruits on earth<br>  Shower'd from it, making its sad visage smile,<br>  For life and hope and bliss was in their taste. [480]<br>  Amid the state of boughs twin Eagles hung<br>  Their eyries, Victory and Renown, and swung<br>  In rapturous sport with the tumultuous winds,<br>  But birds obscene, Dishonour, Shame, Dismay,<br>  Scar'd by the light of the bright leaves, aloof [485]<br>  Far wheel'd their sullen flight, nor dar'd to stoop.<br>  I saw the Nations graft their wasted trunks<br>  From those broad boughs of beauty and of strength,<br>  And dip their drain'd urns in that sacred stream.<br>  But in the deep peculiar shade there stood [490]<br>  A Throne, an Altar, and a Senate-house.<br>  Upon the throne a King sate, triple-crown'd<br>  As by three kingdoms; voices eloquent<br>  In harmony of discord fulmin'd forth<br>  From that wise Senate: in swift intercourse [495]<br>  To and fro from heaven's crystal battlements<br>  To that pure altar Angels stoop'd their flight.<br>  And through the sunny boughs Philosophers<br>  Held commerce with the skies, and drew from thence<br>  The stars to suffer their sage scrutiny; [500]<br>  And Poets sent up through the bowery vault<br>  Such lavish harmonies, the charm'd air seem'd<br>  Forgetful of its twinkling motion dim.<br>  <br>  "Oh, admirable Tree!  thou shalt not fall<br>  By foreign axe, or slow decay within! [505]<br>  The tempests strengthen thee, the summer airs<br>  Corrupt not, but adorn.  Until that tide,<br>  Freedom, the Inexhaustible, exhaust,<br>  Lives thy coeval Immortality."<br>  <br>  The Prophet ceas'd: still Samor on his face, [510]<br>  That in solemnity of firm appeal<br>  Look'd heavenward, with a passionate belief<br>  Gaz'd, and a glad abandonment.  "Ha, Seer,<br>  But now when thou begann'st 'twas noon of day,<br>  And now deep night.  Yea, Merlin, and by night [515]<br>  The Tamer of the White Steed must go forge<br>  His iron curb."  Forth like a cataract<br>  He burst, and bounded down the mountain side.<br>  "Yet once again, tumultuous world, I plunge<br>  Amid thy mad abyss; thou proud and fierce, [520]<br>  I come to break and tame thee!  see ye not,<br>  Wise Hengist!  stong Caswallon!  how the sand<br>  Is under your high towering thrones, the worm<br>  Is in your showy palms." -- And then a pause<br>  Of tumult and proud trembling in his soul, [525]<br>  And, "False it was not, but a gleam vouchsaf'd<br>  From the eternal orb of truth, the sense<br>  That inbred and ingrain'd with my soul's life,<br>  Hath made of Britain to this leaping heart<br>  A sound not merely of deep love, but pride [530]<br>  Intense, and inborn majesty.  I feel,<br>  And from my earliest consciousness have felt,<br>  That in the wide hereafter, where old Fate<br>  Broods o'er the unravelling web of human things,<br>  Wov'n by the Almighty, spreads thy tissue broad [535]<br>  In light, among the dark and mazy threads;<br>  Vicissitude or mutability<br>  Quench not its desolate lustre, on it winds<br>  Unbroken, unattainted, unobscur'd." --<br>  <br>  So pass'd he, who had seen, him then had deem'd, [540]<br>  By the proud steedlike tossing of his crest,<br>  His motion like the uncheck'd August sun<br>  Travelling the cloudless vacancy of air,<br>  A monarch for his summer pastime gone<br>  Into the shady grove, with courtier train, [545]<br>  And plumed steed, and laden sumpter mule,<br>  Cool canopy, and velvet carpeting.<br>  But he beneath the sleety winter sky,<br>  Even his hard arms bit into by the keen<br>  And searching airs, houseless, by hazard found [550]<br>  His coarse irregular fare, his drink, the ice<br>  Toilsomely broken from the stiff black pool.<br>  The furr'd wold in the mossy oaken trunk<br>  Lapp'd himself from the beating snow, but on<br>  Went Samor with unshivering naked foot; [555]<br>  The tempest from the mountain side tore down<br>  The pine, like a scath'd trophy casting it<br>  To moulder in the vale, but Samor's brow<br>  Fronted the rude sky; the free torrent felt<br>  The ice its rushing turbulence o'ergrow, [560]<br>  Translucent in its cold captivity<br>  It hung, but Samor burst the invading frost<br>  From the untamed waters of his soul, and flow'd<br>  Fetterless on his deep unfathom'd course.<br>  <br>  And thou, wild Deva, how hast thou foregone [565]<br>  Thy summer music, and thy sunny play<br>  Of eddies whitening 'mid thy channel stones;<br>  Bard-belov'd river, on whose green-fring'd brink<br>  The fine imagining Grecian sure had feign'd<br>  'Twixt thy smooth Naiads and the Sylvans rude [470]<br>  Of thy gray woods stol'n amorous intercourse;<br>  With such a slow reluctance thou delay'st<br>  Under the dipping branches, that flap up<br>  With every shifting motion of the wind,<br>  Thy limpid moisture, and with serpent coil [575]<br>  Dost seem as thou would'st mingle with thyself<br>  To wander o'er again the same lov'd course.<br>  Now lies thy ice-bound bosom mute and flat<br>  As marble pavement, thy o'ershadowing woods<br>  One bare, brown leaflessness, that faintly drop [580]<br>  At intervals the heavy icicles,<br>  Like tears upon a monumental stone.<br>  But though thy merry waters and brisk leaves<br>  Are silent, with their close-couch'd birds of song,<br>  Even in this blank dead season music loves [585]<br>  Thy banks, and sounds harmonious must be heard<br>  Even o'er thy frozen waters.  'Twas an hymn<br>  From a low chapel by the river side,<br>  Came struggling through the thick and hazy air,<br>  And made a gushing as of tears flow o'er [590]<br>  The Wanderer's soul; the form winds could not bow<br>  Nor crazing tempests, those soft sounds amate;<br>  Those dews of music melt into the frame<br>  Of adamant, proof against the parching frost.<br>  <br>  Under the porch he glided in, and knelt [595]<br>  Unnotic'd in the throng: whose motion sway'd<br>  The beasts of ravine, he before his God<br>  Wore nought distinctive, save of those bruis'd reeds,<br>  Was he the sorest bruis'd, and deepest seem'd<br>  The full devotion settling round his heart. [600]<br>  More musical than the music on that soul,<br>  So long inured to things tumultuous, sights<br>  Rugged and strange, and hurrying and distract,<br>  Came the sensation of a face belov'd.<br>  The calm of that old reverend brow, the glow [605]<br>  Of its thin silver locks, was like a flash<br>  Of sunlight in the pauses of a storm.<br>  Now hath the white stol'd Bishop lifted up<br>  His arms, his parting benison descends<br>  Like summer rain upon his flock.  Whose ear, [610]<br>  Oh, holy Germain, felt thy gentle tones<br>  As Samor's?  ah, when last thy saintly brow<br>  For him look'd heavenward, and less tremulous then<br>  Thy voice on him breath'd blessing, 'twas in times<br>  Far brighter, at that jocund bridal hour [615]<br>  When Emeric, rosy between shame and joy,<br>  Stood with him by the altar side: -- "Thus live<br>  In love till life's departure;" -- Such thy prayer,<br>  Ah, words how vain! sweet blessings unenjoy'd!<br>  <br>  The throng hath parted; in the House of God [620]<br>  Still knelt the armed man; with pressure strong<br>  He clasp'd old Germain's hand -- "Good Bishop, thou<br>  Art skill'd in balancing our earthly sins<br>  I was a man, whose high ambitious head<br>  Was among God's bright stars; I deem'd of earth, [625]<br>  As of a place whose dust my feet shook off<br>  With an heaven-gifted scorn, so far, so high<br>  Seem'd I above its tainting elevate.<br>  At midnight, on my slumber came the sin,<br>  I will not say how exquisite and fair, [630]<br>  Mine eyelids sprung apart to drink it in,<br>  My soul leap'd up to clasp it, and the folds<br>  Of passion, like a fiery robe, wrapt in<br>  My nature; I had fall'n, but bounteous Heaven<br>  Of its most blest permitted one t'extend [635]<br>  A snow-white arm of rescue." -- "The hot tears<br>  Corrode and fret the warrior's brazen helm;<br>  I will not ask thee if thine outward eyes,<br>  Hath thy soul wept?" -- "Aye, Bishop, tears of blood;<br>  Sorrow and shame weigh'd down my nerveless arm [640]<br>  And clipp'd th' aspiring plumage of my soul;<br>  From out mine own heart scorn hiss'd at me." -- "Well,<br>  Strong Man of arms, hast fought the inward fight,<br>  And God remit thy sins, as I remit." --<br>  <br>  "Then take thou to thine arms thy ancient friend." [645]<br>  So saying, uprose Samor, like a star<br>  Out of the ocean, shining his bright face<br>  With the pure dews of penitence.  But he,<br>  The old man, fell upon his neck and wept,<br>  As though th' endearing name, my Son, were voic'd [650]<br>  By nature, not by saintly use, a sound<br>  Not of the lips, but th' overflowing heart.<br>  <br>  Their's was a broken conference, drear thoughts<br>  Of anguish, desolation, and despair,<br>  So moulded up with recollections sweet, [655]<br>  They made the sunken visage smile through tears;<br>  A few fair roses shed on a brown heath,<br>  A little honey in deep cups of gall:<br>  Light bridal airs broke in upon by sounds<br>  Funereal, shouts of triumph languishing [660]<br>  To the faint shriek of agony, direness forc'd<br>  Into the fresh bowers of delight, and death,<br>  Th' unjoyous, in the laughing feast of joy.<br>  <br>  'Tis th' one poor luxury of the wretched have<br>  To speak of wretchedness -- yet brief their speech, [665]<br>  "Vengeance and vigilance," the stern adieu<br>  Even in that hoary Bishop's ear, he went.<br>  <br>  But by the Bishop's side, just there where knelt<br>  Th' Avenger, a new form: 'twas man in garb,<br>  But the thin fringing of the humid eye, [670]<br>  The delicate wanderings of the rosy veins,<br>  The round full alabaster of the skin,<br>  The briefness of the modest sliding step,<br>  Something of womanly composure smooth,<br>  Even in the close and girt habiliments, [675]<br>  Belied the stern appearance. -- "Priest, with him<br>  But now who parted, is my soul allied<br>  In secret, close society; his faith<br>  Must be my faith, his God my God." -- "Fair youth,<br>  I question not by what imperious tie [680]<br>  Of admiration or strong love, thou 'rt led;<br>  For as the Heavens with silent power intense<br>  Draw upward the light mists and fogs of earth,<br>  And steeping them in glory, hang them forth<br>  Fresh, renovate, and radiant; virtue holds [685]<br>  The like attractive influence, to her trains<br>  Souls light and clayey-tinctur'd, till they catch<br>  The fair contagion of her beauty, beam<br>  With her imparted light.  Hear, heathen youth,<br>  Hear and believe." -- As when beneath the nave [690]<br>  Tall arching, the Cathedral organ 'gins<br>  Its prelude, lingering exquisite<br>  Within retir'd the bashful sweetness dwells,<br>  Anon like sunlight, or the floodgate rush<br>  Of waters, bursts it forth, clear, solemn, full; [695]<br>  It breaks upon the mazy fretted roof,<br>  It coils up round the clustering pillars tall,<br>  It leaps into the cell-like chapels, strikes<br>  Beneath the pavement sepulchres, at once<br>  The living temple is instinct, ablaze [700]<br>  With the uncontrolled exuberance of sound.<br>  <br>  Even so with smoothing gentleness began<br>  The mitred Preacher, winning audience close:<br>  Till rising up, the rapid argument<br>  Soar'd to the Empyrean, linking earth [705]<br>  With heaven by golden chains of eloquence;<br>  Till the mind, all its faculties and powers<br>  Lay floating, self-surrender'd in the deep<br>  Of admiration.  Wondrous 'twas to see,<br>  With the transitions of the Holy Creed, [710]<br>  The workings of that regular bright face:<br>  Now ashy blank, now glittering bright, now dew'd<br>  With fast sad tears, now with a weeping smile,<br>  Now heavy with droop'd eyelids, open now<br>  With forehead arch'd in rapture; 'till at last [715]<br>  Ensued a gasping listening without breath.<br>  But as the voice severe wound up the strain,<br>  And from the heavenly history to enforce<br>  The everlasting moral, 'gan extort<br>  From the noviciate in the jealous faith [720]<br>  Passionless purity, and life sincere<br>  From all the soft indulgences of sin;<br>  Forbidden in the secret heart to shrine<br>  A dear unlawful image, to reserve<br>  A sad and narrow sanctuary for desire: [725]<br>  Then stood in speechlessness, yet suppliant,<br>  With snowy arms outstrech'd, and quivering loose,<br>  The veiling mantle thrown in anguish back,<br>  Confest the Woman; starting from their band,<br>  Like golden waters o'er a marble bed, [730]<br>  Flow'd out her long locks o'er her half-bare neck.<br>  <br>  "To tell me that in such cold solemn tones,<br>  All, all unwelcome, bitter as it is,<br>  I must believe, for its oppressive truth<br>  Loads on my soul, and he believes it all. [735]<br>  To tell it me here, here, where all around<br>  Linger his vestiges, where the warm air<br>  Yet hath the motion of his breath, the sound<br>  Of his departing footsteps beating yet<br>  Upon my heart.  Long sought!  and found in vain! [740]<br>  In sunshine have I sought thee and in shade,<br>  O'er mountain have I track'd thee, and through vale,<br>  The clouds have wrapp'd thee, but I lost thee not,<br>  The torrents drown'd thy track, but not from me,<br>  I dared not meet thee, but I sought thee still; [745]<br>  To me forbid, alone to me, what all<br>  The coarse and common things of nature may,<br>  The airs of heaven may touch thee, I may not,<br>  All human eyes behold thee -- all but mine;<br>  And thou, the senseless, enviable dust [750]<br>  May'st cherish the round traces of his limbs,<br>  His fresh fair image must away from me.<br>  Oh, that I were the dust whereon thou tread'st,<br>  Even though I felt thee not!" -- And is this she,<br>  The Virgin of the festal hall, who won [755]<br>  A kingdom for a smile, nor deign'd regard<br>  Its winning, and who stoop'd to be a Queen?<br>  And is this she, whose coming on the earth<br>  Was like the Morn in her impearled car,<br>  Loftiest or loveliest which, 'twere bold to say? [760]<br>  She whose enamouring scorn feel luxury-like<br>  On her beholders, who seem'd glad to shrink<br>  Beneath the wreath'd contempt of her full lip?<br>  This she, the Lady of the summer bark,<br>  To whom the sunshine and the airs, and all [765]<br>  Th' inconstant waters play'd the courtier smooth,<br>  That cast a human feeling of delight<br>  At her bewitching presence o'er the blind<br>  Unconscious forms of nature?  Is this she!<br>  Those rich lips, for a monarch's banquet meet, [770]<br>  Visiting the dust with frantic kiss, thus low,<br>  Thus desolate, thus fallen, of her fall<br>  Careless, so deep in shame, yet unasham'd!<br>  <br>  But thou, Heaven reconcil'd, on earth the seal'd,<br>  The anointed by the prophet's gladdening oils, [775]<br>  God's instrument, hath midnight now resum'd<br>  Its spirit-wafting function?  Emeric, she<br>  On earth so mild, in her had anger seem'd<br>  Unnatural as a war-song on a lute,<br>  As blood upon the pinion of a dove. [780]<br>  In heaven hath she her heavenly qualities<br>  Unlearnt?  is she the angel now in all<br>  But its best part, forgiveness?  Can it be<br>  Th' ungentle North, the bleak and snowy air<br>  Estrange her now?  those elements of earth [785]<br>  But tyrannize beneath the moon, the stars<br>  And spirits in their nature privileg'd<br>  From heat and cold, from fevering and from frost,<br>  Their pure and constant temperament maintain,<br>  Glide through the storm serene, and rosy warm [790]<br>  Rove the frore winter air.  Are sounds abroad,<br>  That Samor from his mossy pillow, stretch'd<br>  Under the oak, uplifts his head, and then<br>  Like one bliss overcome, subsides again<br>  Half asleep, half sense he lies, his nuptial hymn, [795]<br>  Articulate each gay and dancing word,<br>  Distinct each delicate and dwelling fall,<br>  Is somewhere in the air about him, looks<br>  Are on him of a bashful eye, too fond<br>  To turn away, too timorous to fix [800]<br>  And rest unwavering.  All the marriage rite<br>  Is acting now anew; the sunlight falls<br>  Upon the gold clasp'd book of prayer, as then<br>  It fell, and Germain speaks as Germain spake;<br>  And Emeric, on her cheek the tear is there, [805]<br>  Where then it hung in lucid trembling bright;<br>  The very fluttering of her yielded hand,<br>  When gliding up her finger small, the ring<br>  Made her his own for ever, throbs again<br>  Upon his sensitive touch.  He dares not move [810]<br>  Lest he should break the lovely bubble frail,<br>  His tranc'd eyes stir not, lest they rove away<br>  From that delicious sight, his open hand<br>  Lies pulseless, lest the slightest change disturb<br>  That exquisite sensation, so he lies,<br>  Knowing all false, yet feeling all as true.<br>  <br>  And it was false, yet why?  that is indeed,<br>  Which is to sense and sight.  Ah, well beseems<br>  Us, the strong insects of an April morn,<br>  Steady and constant as the thistle's down [820]<br>  When winds are on it, lasting as the flake<br>  Of spring snow on the warm and grassy ground,<br>  Well beseems us, ourselves, our forms, our lives,<br>  The earth we tread on, and the air we breathe,<br>  The light and glassy peopling of a dream, [825]<br>  T' arraign our visions for their perishing,<br>  And on their unreality to rail,<br>  Ungrateful to the illusion, that deceives<br>  To rapture, and unwise, to cast away<br>  Sweet flowers because they are not amaranth. [830]<br>  <br>  Thou, Samor, nor ungrateful nor unwise,<br>  That, scaping from this cold and dark below,<br>  Dost spread thee out for thy peculiar joy<br>  A land of fair imaginings, with shapes,<br>  And sounds, and motions, and sweet stillnesses, [835]<br>  Dost give up all the moon beholds to woe<br>  And tumult, but in some far quiet sphere<br>  Findest thyself a pure companionship<br>  With spirits thou dids't love, and who lov'd thee<br>  While passionate and earthly sense was theirs. [840]<br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK IX.</b> </h3>   Who tracks the ship along the sea of storms?<br>  Who through the dark haste of the wintry clouds<br>  Pierceth to where the planet is retired<br>  And constant motion the blue arch of heaven<br>  Traverseth?  Sometimes on the mountain top [5]<br>  Of some huge wave the reappearing bark<br>  Takes its high stand, with pennon fluttering far<br>  And cautious half furl'd, yet eminent<br>  As of th' assaulting element in disdain.<br>  Sometimes amid' the darkness falling off, [10]<br>  And scattering from its crystal sphere away,<br>  Bursts out the argent orb refresh'd, and shews<br>  Its lamp unquenchable.  Thou voyager<br>  'Mid the rude waves of desolation, Star<br>  Of Britain's gloomy night, so bafflest thou [15]<br>  My swift poetic vision!  now the waves<br>  Ride o'er thee, now the clouds devour thee up,<br>  And thou art lost to sight, and dare I say<br>  Lost to thy immortality of song.<br>  Thee to anon I see emerging proud [20]<br>  From the dusk billows of calamity,<br>  That swol'n and haughty from the recent wreck<br>  Of thy compatriot navy, thee assail<br>  With their accumulated weight of surge,<br>  Thou top'st some high-brow'd wave, and shaking off [25]<br>  On either side of their fury, brandishest<br>  Thy solitary banner.  Thee I see,<br>  Within th' embosoming midnight of the land,<br>  On gliding with smooth motion undisturb'd,<br>  And through the glimpses of the breaking gloom, [30]<br>  Sometimes a solemn beauty sheddest forth<br>  Of the distemper'd face of human things.<br>  <br>  Full in the centre of Caer Ebranc stood [NOTE 13]<br>  A temple, by the August Severus rear'd<br>  To Mavors the Implacable; what time [35]<br>  That Cæsar stoop'd his eagles on the wreck<br>  Of British freedom, when the mountaineer,<br>  The King of Morven, if old songs be sooth,<br>  Fingal, from Carun's bloody flashing waves<br>  Shook the fled Roman on his new-built wall; [40]<br>  And Ossian woke up on his hill of dreams,<br>  And spread the glory of his song abroad,<br>  To halo round his sceptred Hero's head.<br>  <br>  But not the less his work of pride pursued<br>  Th' imperial Roman; up the pillars rose, [45]<br>  Slow lengthening out their long unbroken lines,<br>  In delicate solidity advanc'd,<br>  And stately grace toward the sky, till met<br>  By the light massiveness of roof, that sloped<br>  Down on their flowery capitals.  Nor knew [50]<br>  That man of purple and of diadem,<br>  The Universal Architect at work,<br>  Framing for him a narrow building dark,<br>  The grave's lone building.  Th' emperor and his bones<br>  Into the blank of things forgot and past [55]<br>  Had moulder'd, but this proud and 'during pile,<br>  By wild weeds overgrown, by yellow hues<br>  Of age deep tinted, still a triumph wrought<br>  O'er time, and Christian disregard, and stood<br>  As though to mock its Maker's perishing. [60]<br>  <br>  Upon the eastern pediment stood out<br>  A fierce relief, where the tumultuous stone<br>  Was nobly touch'd into a fit device<br>  For th' immortal Homicide within: it showed<br>  His coming on the earth; the God had burst [65]<br>  The gates of Janus, that fell shattering back<br>  Behind him, from the wall the rearing steeds<br>  Sprung forth, and with their stony hoofs the air<br>  Insulted.  Them Bellona urg'd, abroad<br>  Her snaking locks from her bare wrinked brow [70]<br>  Went scattering; forward th' haggard charioteer<br>  Lean'd, following to the coursers reeking flanks<br>  The furrowing scourge with all herself, and hung<br>  Over their backs half fury, and half joy,<br>  As though to listen to their bruising hoofs, [75]<br>  That trampled the thick massacre.  Erect<br>  Behind, with shield drawn in and forward spear,<br>  The con'd helm finely shap'd to th' arching brow,<br>  The God stood up within the car, that seem'd<br>  To rush whenever the fleet wind swept by. [80]<br>  His brow was glory, and his arm was power,<br>  And a smooth immortality of youth,<br>  Like freshness from Elysium newly left,<br>  Th' embalming of celestial airs inhal'd,<br>  Touch'd with a beauty to be shudder'd at [85]<br>  His massy shape, a lightning-like fierce grace,<br>  That makes itself admir'd, whilst it destroys.<br>  <br>  There on a throne, fronting the morning sun,<br>  Caswallon sate, his sceptre a bright sword<br>  Unsheath'd; with savage art had he broke up [90]<br>  His helmet to the likeness of a crown,<br>  Thereon uncouthly set and clustering bright<br>  Rich jewels glitter'd; to his people rang'd<br>  Upon the steps of marble sloping down,<br>  Barbaric justice minist'ring he sate, [95]<br>  Expounding th' absolute law of his own will,<br>  And from the abject at his feet receiv'd<br>  Homage that seem'd like worship: not alone<br>  From his wild people, but from lips baptiz'd,<br>  Came titles that might make the patient Heavens [100]<br>  Burst to the utterance of a laughing scorn;<br>  Might wake up from the bosom of the grave,<br>  A bitter and compassionate contempt,<br>  To hear the inheritance of her dull worms,<br>  Nam'd in his dauntless and unblushing style, [105]<br>  "Unconqu'rable!  Omnipotent!  Supreme!"<br>  <br>  But all along the ranging column files,<br>  And all abroad the turgid laudings spread,<br>  Unconqu'rable!  Omnipotent! Supreme!" --<br>  <br>  Yet he, the Stranger, whom Prince Malwyn leads, [110]<br>  He bows not, those hymn'd flatteries seem to jar<br>  Upon his sense, so high his head he bears<br>  Above them, like a man constrain'd to walk<br>  Amid low tufts of poisonous herbs; he fronts<br>  The monarch, and thus 'gins his taunting strain: [115]<br>  "Unconqu'rable! whose conquering is the wolf's,<br>  That when the shifting battle rages yet,<br>  Steals to some desert corner of the field,<br>  And riots on the spoils.  Omnipotent!<br>  Aye, as a passive weapon, wielded now, [120]<br>  Now cast away contemptuous for the dust<br>  To canker and to rust around.  Supreme!<br>  O'er whom is Ruin on its vultures wings,<br>  Scoffing the bubble whereupon thou rid'st,<br>  And waiting Hengist's call to swoop and pierce [125]<br>  And dissipate its swoln and airy pride.<br>  Whose diadem of glory, sword of power,<br>  Yea, breath of life, at Hengist's wayward will,<br>  Cling to thee, ready at his beck to fade,<br>  And shiver and expire" -- "At Hengist's call! [130]<br>  At Hengist's beck!  at Hengist's!" -- the word chok'd,<br>  With eyes that dug into the Stranger's face,<br>  Yet so by wrath bewilder'd, they had lost<br>  Distinction, rose Caswallon.  From the wall<br>  A lance he seiz'd, huge as a pine-tree stem, [135]<br>  That on Blencathara stands sheer 'gainst heaven's storms:<br>  Far o'er all heads a long and rapid flight<br>  It cut along the air, till almost fail'd<br>  The sight to track it to its ponderous fall.<br>  Then taking on his throne his quiet seat, [140]<br>  "Back, back to Hengist, say my lance flies thus,<br>  Bid him o'ercast it, then come here again<br>  To menace at Caswallon" -- "Soft and weak,<br>  (Pursued the unwondering Stranger) know'st thou not,<br>  There is a strength, that is not of the arm, [145]<br>  Nor standeth in the muscles sinewy play?<br>  It striketh, but its striking is unseen,<br>  It wieldeth, what it wieldeth seeming yet<br>  Sway'd by its own free motion.  King, I saw,<br>  Thou stepp'st not, speak'st not, but obedient still [150]<br>  To Hengist's empire, thou'rt a dog that hunts<br>  But as thy master slips thee on his game,<br>  A bridled steed, that vaunteth at his own<br>  His rider's prowess." -- "Hah! I know thee now,<br>  Insolent outcast, Samor!" -- "And I thee, [155]<br>  Self-outcast, once a Briton -- oh thou fall'n<br>  When most thou seem'st exalted, oh most base<br>  When most ennobled, a most pitiful slave<br>  When bearing thee most lordly!  Briton once,<br>  Ay, every clod of earth that makes a part [160]<br>  Of this isle's round, each leaf of every tree,<br>  And every wave of every streamlet brook,<br>  Should look upon thee with a mother's glance.<br>  And speak unto thee with a mother's voice.<br>  But thou, most impious and unnatural son, [165]<br>  Hast sold thy mother to the shame and curse<br>  Of foreign lust, hast knit a league to rend<br>  And sever her, most proud if some torn limb<br>  Be cast thee for thy lot". -- Then rose again<br>  Caswallon, from his brow the crown took off, [170]<br>  And placing it in Samor's hand -- "I read<br>  Thy purpose, and there's fire in't, by my throne!<br>  Now, Samor, place that crown upon my head,<br>  Do me thy homage, kneeling, as thy king,<br>  And thou and I, we'll have a glorious tilt [175]<br>  At these proud Saxons.  Turn not off; may boys<br>  Gild their young javelins in Caswallon's blood,<br>  And women pluck me by the beard, if e'er<br>  On other terms I league with thee." -- The crown<br>  Samor received, and Samor look'd to heaven, [180]<br>  And Samor bow'd his knee, -- "Almighty God,<br>  If thine eternal thunderbolts are yet<br>  Unweary of their function dire, if earth<br>  Yet, yet have not exhausted and consum'd<br>  Thy flame-wing'd armoury of wrath, reserve [185]<br>  Some signal and particular revenge<br>  For this man's head, so this foul earth shall learn,<br>  Ere doomsday, that the sin, whose monstrous shape<br>  Doth most offend thy nice and sensitive sight,<br>  Is to bear arms against our native land. [190]<br>  Make thou of him a monumental ruin,<br>  To publish in the ages long remote,<br>  That sometimes is thy red right hand uplift<br>  Against the living guilty." -- And to earth,<br>  Upleaping, Samor dash'd the crown; the gems [195]<br>  Lay starry on the pavement white.  On high<br>  Caswallon the rear'd sword of justice swung,<br>  Heavy with death, above th' Avenger's head.<br>  But he -- "Caswallon, hold thine hand, here, here<br>  Thy warrant for my safety, by thy son [200]<br>  A poniard given, upon his heart to wreak<br>  All evil done myself."  With bosom bare<br>  Stood Malwyn by the Avenger's side.  But he<br>  Viewing that downy skin, empurpled o'er<br>  With youth's light colouring, and his constant mien, [205]<br>  Cast down the dagger, and "Fall what fall may,<br>  Excellent boy, my hand shall still be white<br>  From blood of thine." -- Like wild-boar in his rush<br>  Baffled, or torrent check'd, Caswallon paus'd --<br>  "Now, Christian, where learnt thou the art to wrest [210]<br>  My vengeance from me?  Go, go I may strike<br>  If the fit fire me. -- By Andraste, boy,<br>  Boy Malwyn, there's thy father in thy blood.<br>  Hah, Samor, thou hast 'scap'd me now, erewhile<br>  I'll make a footstool of thy neck, to mount [215]<br>  On Britain's throne: alive or dead, I'll have<br>  A knee as supple, and a front as low<br>  From thee, as any of my milk-fed slaves:<br>  Go, go." -- And Malwyn led the Avenger forth<br>  Along the dull and sleepy shore of Ouse, [220]<br>  Till all Caer Ebranc's sounds flagg'd on his ear,<br>  And all its towers had dwindled from his sight.<br>  Ere parting, Malwyn clasp'd his hand, and tears<br>  Hung in his eyelids. -- "Oh, thou know'st not yet<br>  How Hengist sways my father's passive mind; [225]<br>  My sister, my sweet Lilian, she whose sight<br>  Made mine eyes tremble, whom I've stol'n to see,<br>  Despite my father's stern command, asleep<br>  With parted lips, and snowy breathing skin,<br>  Scarce knew she me, her brother, her knew I [230]<br>  So only that my spirit yearn'd to mix<br>  With her's in fondness, she, even she, the soft<br>  The innocent, a wolf had lov'd her, she<br>  Hath felt the drowning waters o'er her close,<br>  Fair victim of an hellish sacrifice." [235]<br>  After a troubled silence, spake the Chief:<br>  "Malwyn, my Christian pupil, God will give<br>  The lov'd on earth another meeting place;<br>  Adieu, remember, Vengeance, Vigilance." --<br>  <br>  The Spring had made an early effort faint, [240]<br>  T' encroach upon the Winter's ancient reign;<br>  And she had lur'd forth from the glittering earth<br>  The snowdrop and pale cowslip, th' elder tree<br>  And hawthorn their green buds shot out, yet fear'd<br>  T' entrust the rude air with their dainty folds. [245]<br>  A fresh green sparkled where the snow had been,<br>  And here and there a bird on the bare spray<br>  Warbled a timorous welcome, and the stream<br>  Of Eamont, as rejoicing to be free,<br>  Went laughing down its sunny silvering course. [250]<br>  <br>  The only wint'ry thing on Eamont's shore<br>  Is human; powerless are the airs that touch<br>  To breathing and to kindling the dead earth,<br>  Powerless the dewy trembling of the sun,<br>  To melt around the heart of Vortimer<br>  The snow that flakes and curdles there -- that bank,<br>  That little bank of fair and cherish'd turf,<br>  Whereon his head reclines, ah, doth not rest!<br>  By its round swelling, likest were a grave,<br>  Save that 'twere brief and narrow for all else [260]<br>  But fairy, or those slender watery shapes<br>  That dance beneath the stream.  Yet there the Spring<br>  Hath dropp'd her first, her tenderest bloom; the airs<br>  Find the first flowery odours on that spot;<br>  Cowslip is there, and primrose faint and pale, [265]<br>  The daisy and the violet's blue eyes,<br>  Peeping from out the shaking grass.  The step<br>  Of Samor wakens the pale slumberer there,<br>  He lifts his lean hands up, and parts away<br>  The matting hair from o'er his eyes, which look [270]<br>  As though the painful sunlight wilder'd them,<br>  With stony stare that saw not; save that lay<br>  A shepherd's wallet by his side, had seem'd<br>  That foot of man ne'er ventur'd here; all sounds<br>  Were strange and foreign, save the pendant arms [275]<br>  Swinging above the heavy knolling sound.<br>  But Samor's presence made a sudden break<br>  Upon his miserable flow of thought;<br>  He motion'd first with bony arm, then spake.<br>  "Away, away, thou'rt fearful, thou'lt disturb, [280]<br>  Away with thy arm'd head and iron heel,<br>  She will not venture, while thy aspect fierce<br>  Haunts hereabout, she cannot brook a sound,<br>  Nor anything that's rude, and dark, and harsh,<br>  Nor any voice, nor any look but mine; [285]<br>  She will not come up, if thou linger'st here;<br>  Hard and discourteous man, why seek to keep<br>  My own, my buried from me? why prevent<br>  The smiling intercourse of those that love?" --<br>  "Sad man, what mean'st thou?" -- "Speak not, but begone, [290]<br>  I tell thee, she's beneath, I laid her there,<br>  And she'll come up to me, I know she will,<br>  Trembling and slender, soft and rosy pale.<br>  I know it, all things sound, and all things smile,<br>  As when she wont to meet me." -- "Woful youth, [295]<br>  The dead shall never rise but once." -- "And why?<br>  The primrose that was dead, I saw it shed<br>  Its leaves, and now again 'tis fresh and fair;<br>  The swallow, fled on gliding wing away,<br>  Like a departing spirit, see, it skims [300]<br>  The waters; the white dormouse, that went down<br>  Into its cave, hath been abroad; the stream,<br>  That was so silent, hark!  its murmuring voice<br>  Is round about us; Lilian too, to meet<br>  The voices and the breathing things she lov'd, [305]<br>  Amid the sunshine and the springing joy<br>  Will rise again." -- "Kind Heaven, I should have known,<br>  Though rust embrown'd yon breast-plate, and yon helm,<br>  I should have known, though furrowy sunk and wan,<br>  That face, though wreck'd and broken, that tall form;<br>  Prince Vortimer!  in maiden or in child, [311]<br>  Fancies so sick and wild had been most sad,<br>  But in a martial and renowned chief,<br>  Might teach a trick of pity to a fiend.<br>  Oh, much abus'd!  much injur'd!  well, too well [315]<br>  Hath that fell man the deed of evil wrought." --<br>  "Man, man!  then there is man, whose blood will flow,<br>  Whose flesh will quiver under the keen steel,<br>  Samor!" -- And up he leap'd, as though he flung<br>  Like a dead load, the dreamy madness off. [320]<br>  "Samor!  thou tranquil soul!  that walk'st abroad<br>  With thy calm reason, and thy cloudless face<br>  Unchangeable, as a cold midnight star:<br>  Thou scarce wilt credit, I have found a joy<br>  In hurling stones down on that glassy tide, [325]<br>  And with an angry and quick-dashing foot,<br>  Breaking the senseless smoothness, that methought<br>  Smiled wickedly upon me, and rejoic'd<br>  At its own guilt, and my calamity.<br>  But oh, upon a thing that feels and bleeds, [330]<br>  And shrieks and shudders, with avenging arm<br>  To spring!  Where is't and who?  good Samor tell." --<br>  And Samor told the tale, and thus -- "Brave youth,<br>  Not only from yon narrow turf, come up<br>  From Britain's every hill, and glen, and plain, [335]<br>  Deep voices that invoke thee, Vortimer,<br>  To waken from thy woful rest.  Thy arm<br>  No selfish, close, and singular revenge<br>  Must nerve and freshen; in thy country's cause,<br>  Not in thy own, that fury must be wreak'd." [340]<br>  <br>  His answer was the brandishing his sword,<br>  Which he had rent down from th' o'erhanging bough,<br>  And the infuriate riot of his eye.<br>  <br>  "Oh, perilous your hazard," still went on<br>  Samor, "ye foes of freedom, ye take off [345]<br>  Heaven's bonds from all our fiercer part of man,<br>  Ye legalize forbidden thoughts, the thirst<br>  Of blood ye make a glory, give the hue<br>  Of honour and self-admiration proud,<br>  To passions murky, dark, unreconcil'd: [350]<br>  The sterm and Pagan vengeance sanctify<br>  T' a Christian virtue, and our prayers, that mount<br>  Unto the throne of God, though harshly toned<br>  With imprecations, take their flight uncheck'd."<br>  <br>  But Vortimer upon the grassy bank<br>  Had fall'n, "Not long, sweet spirit, oh not long,<br>  Shall violets be wanting on thy grave." --<br>  <br>  Yet unaccompanied the Avenger past, --<br>  As though the wonted dark and solemn words,<br>  "Vengeance and Vigilance," had fix'd him there, [360]<br>  Prince Vortimer remains by Eamont side.<br>  <br>  Samor!  the cities hear thy lonely voice,<br>  Thy lonely tread is in the quiet vale,<br>  Thy lonely arm, amid his deep trench'd camp,<br>  The Saxon hears upon some crashing helm [365]<br>  Breaking in thunder and in death.  But thee,<br>  Why see I thee by Severn side?  what soft<br>  And indolent attraction wiles thee on,<br>  Even on this cold and gusty April day,<br>  To the sad desert of thy ancient home? [370]<br>  Why mingle for thyself the wormwood cup?<br>  Why plunge into the fount of bitterness?<br>  Or why, with sad indulgence, pamper up,<br>  Wilful the moody sorrow, and relax<br>  Thy high-strung spirit?  Oh, so near, no power [375]<br>  Hath he to pass from those old scenes away,<br>  He must go visit every sport belov'd,<br>  And think on joys, no more to be enjoy'd.<br>  <br>  Ruin is there, but ruin slow and mild,<br>  The spider's wandering web is thin and gray [380]<br>  On roof and wall, here clings the dusky bat,<br>  And, where his infants voices us'd to sound,<br>  The owlet's sullen flutter and dull chirp<br>  Come o'er him; on his hospitable hearth<br>  The blind worm and slow beetle crawl their round. [385]<br>  Yet is not little, light, and trivial thing,<br>  Without its tender memory; first with kiss,<br>  Long and apparent sweet, the primrose bed<br>  He visits, where that graceful girl is laid.<br>  Then roves he every chamber; eye, and ear, [390]<br>  And soul, all full of her, that is not there:<br>  Emeric haunts everywhere, there's not a door<br>  Her thin form hath not glided through, no stone<br>  Upon the chequer'd marble where her foot<br>  Hath never glanc'd, no window whence her eyes [395]<br>  Have never gaz'd for him; the walls have heard<br>  Her voice; her touch, now deathly cold, hath been<br>  Warm on so many things; there hangs, even now,<br>  The lute, from whence those harmonies she drew,<br>  So spherelike sweet, they seem'd to drop from heaven.<br>  There, where the fox came starting out but now, [401]<br>  There, circled with her infants, did she sit:<br>  And here the bridal couch, the couch of love,<br>  A little while, and then the bed of death.<br>  And lo that holy scroll of parchment, stamp'd [405]<br>  With many a sentence of the word of God,<br>  Still open, Samor could not choose but read<br>  In large and brilliant characters emblaz'd,<br>  The Preacher's "Vanity of vanities."<br>  <br>  How like is grief to pleasure!  here to stay [410]<br>  One day, one night, to see the eve sink down<br>  Into the water, with its wonted fall<br>  'Tis strange temptation -- and to gather up<br>  Sad relics.  And the visionary night!<br>  How will its airy forms come sliding down, [415]<br>  Here, where is old familiar footing all,<br>  'Tis strange temptation. -- But the White-horse flag<br>  Past waving o'er his sight, at once he thought<br>  Of that seal'd day of destiny, when his foot<br>  Should trample on his neck, and burst away. [420]<br>  <br>  Oh secret traveller o'er a ruin'd land,<br>  Yet once more must I seek thee 'mid the drear,<br>  The desolate, the dead.  On Ambri plain,<br>  On Murder's blasted place of pride.  Might seem<br>  At distance 'twas a favour'd meadow, bright [425]<br>  With richer herbage than the moorland brown<br>  Around it, the luxurious weeds look'd boon,<br>  And glanc'd their many-colours fleck'd with dew.<br>  Seen nearer, scattter'd all around appear'd<br>  Few relics of the sumptuous feast, the wrecks [430]<br>  Of lifeless things, that gaily glitter'd still,<br>  While all the living had been dark so long.<br>  Fragments of banners, and pavilion shreds,<br>  Of broken goblet here and there, or ring,<br>  Or collar on that day how proudly worn! [435]<br>  <br>  A stol'n and hurried burying had there been;<br>  Here had the pious workman, as disturb'd<br>  At his imperfect toil, left struggling out<br>  A hand, whose bleach'd bones seem'd even yet to grasp<br>  The earth, so early, so untimely left. [440]<br>  And here the gray flix of the wolf, here black<br>  Lay feathers of the obscene raven's wing,<br>  Shewing, where they had marr'd the fruitless toil.<br>  And uncouth stones bore here and there a name,<br>  Haply the vaunted heritage of kings. [445]<br>  <br>  It was a sad and stricken place; though day<br>  Was in the heaven, and the fresh grass look'd green,<br>  The light was wither'd, nor was silence there<br>  A soothing quiet; busy 'twas, and chill<br>  And piercing, rather absence of strong sound, [450]<br>  Than stillness, like the shivering interval<br>  Between the pauses of a passing bell.<br>  <br>  Oh Britain!  what a narrow place confines<br>  Thy powerful and thy princely!  that gray earth<br>  Was what adorn'd and made thee proud: the fair, [455]<br>  Whose beauty was the rapture of thy maids,<br>  The treasure of thy mothers: and the brave,<br>  Whose constant valour was thy wall of strength:<br>  The wealthy, whose air-gilding palace towers<br>  Made thee a realm of glory to detain [460]<br>  The noon-day sun in his career; thy wise,<br>  Whose grave and solemn argument controll'd<br>  Thy councils, and thy mighty, whose command<br>  Was law in thy strong cities,  Beauty, wealth,<br>  Might, valour, wisdom, mingled and absorb'd [465]<br>  In one cold similarity of dust,<br>  One layer of white and silent ashes all.<br>  The air breathes of mortality; abroad<br>  A spirit seems to hover, pouring in<br>  Dim thoughts of Doomsday to the soul; steal up [470]<br>  Voiceless sensations of eternity,<br>  From the blank earth.  Oh, is it there beneath<br>  Th' invisible everlasting?  or dispers'd<br>  Among its immaterial kindred free,<br>  The elements?  Oh man!  man!  fit compeer [475]<br>  Of worms and angels, trodden under foot,<br>  Yet boundless by the infinite expanse<br>  Of ether!  mouldering and immutable!<br>  <br>  But thou, Avenger, in that quiet glebe,<br>  How many things are hid, once link'd to thee [480]<br>  By ties more gentle than the coupling silk,<br>  That pairs two snowy doves!  hands used to meet<br>  In brotherly embrace with thine, and hearts<br>  Wherein thy image dwelt, clear, changeless, full<br>  As the Spring moon upon a crystal lake, [485]<br>  Faces in feast, in council, and in fight,<br>  That took their colouring from thine.  And thou<br>  Alone art breathing, moving, speaking here,<br>  Amid the cold, the motionless, the mute!<br>  <br>  Among that solemn multitude of graves [490]<br>  One woman hath her dwelling: round and round<br>  She wanders with a foot that seems to fear<br>  That it is treading over one belov'd.<br>  She seems to seek what she despairs to find.<br>  There's in her eye a wild enquiring roll, [495]<br>  Yet th' eye is stony.  Oft she stops to hear,<br>  Then, as in bitter disappointment, shakes<br>  Her loose hair, and again goes wandering on.<br>  She shriek'd at Samor's presence, and flung up<br>  Her arms, and in her shriek was laughter.  "Thou!<br>  What dost thou with that face above the earth, [501]<br>  Thou should'st be with the rest!" -- "My friend's soft bride,<br>  The dainty Evelene!" -- "That's it, the name<br>  Wherewith the winds have mock'd me every morn,<br>  And every dusky eve -- or was it then? [505]<br>  Aye then it was, when I was wont to sleep<br>  On a soft bed, and when no rough winds blew<br>  About me, when I ever saw myself<br>  Drest glitt'ringly, and there was something else<br>  Then, which there is not now." -- "Thy Elidure, [510]<br>  Ah!  houseless widow!" -- "Hah!  thou cunning man,<br>  'Twas that, 'twas that, and thou canst tell me too<br>  Where they have laid him -- well thou canst, I know<br>  There's deep connexion 'twixt my grief and thee.<br>  Thou, thou art he that wakest sleepers up, [515]<br>  And send'st them forth along the cold bare heath,<br>  To seek the dark and disappearing.  There<br>  Sound howlings at the midnight bleak, and blasts<br>  Shivering and fierce.  And there come peasant boors<br>  That bring the mourner bread, and weave the roof [520]<br>  Above her, of the brown and rustling fern,<br>  But never sounds the voice, or comes the shape<br>  She sought for.  Oh, my wakings and my sleeps<br>  How exquisite they were, upon his breast<br>  I slept, and when I woke there smil'd his face." [525]<br>  <br>  Even as the female pigeon to her nest,<br>  All ruffled by rude winds and discompos'd,<br>  Returning, with full breast sits brooding down,<br>  And all sinks smooth around her and beneath:<br>  So when the image of departed joy [530]<br>  Revisited the heart of that sad wife,<br>  Settled to peace its wayward and distraught,<br>  Sweetly she spake, and unconfus'dly heard,<br>  Of him the low, the undistinguish'd laid,<br>  Of Samor's friend, her bridegroom, Elidure. [535]<br>  And somewhat of her pale and tender bloom<br>  With a faint flourishing enliven'd up<br>  The wither'd and the sunken in her cheek;<br>  But when again alone, o'er heart and brain<br>  Flash'd back the wandering, recommenc'd the search<br>  Ever with broken questionings, and mute [540]<br>  Lip-parted listenings, pauses at each grave,<br>  As though it were her right, where lay her lord,<br>  That some inherent consciousness should start<br>  Within her; though 'tis nature's law, that one [545]<br>  Cold undistinguished silence palls the dead,<br>  Yet, yet 'tis hard and cruel not to grant<br>  One low sound, even the likeness of a sound,<br>  To tell her where to lay her down and die.<br>  Sure there are spirits round her, yet all leagued [550]<br>  T' abuse and lead astray, and hid, even his,<br>  Pitiless as the rest, with jealous care<br>  Concealing its felt presence.  Ghostly night<br>  Wafts her no dusk intelligence; the day<br>  Shews nothing with its broad and glaring rays. [555]<br>  <br>  <br>  <b> <h3 align="CENTER">BOOK X.</h3> </b>   But thou from North to South hast rang'd the isle,<br>  From Skiddaw to the Cornwall sea-beat rocks,<br>  One icy face of desolation cold,<br>  One level sheet of sorrow and dismay,<br>  Avenger!  thou hast travers'd, hast but held [5]<br>  Companionship with mourners and with slaves.<br>  <br>  Upon the northern rocks of Cornwall meet<br>  Th' Avenger and the Warrior; thus spake he --<br>  "How name ye yon strong castle on the rock?"<br>  "Tintagel, the Prince Gorlois' towers." -- "And whose<br>  Yon soldiers cresting with their camp the shore, [11]<br>  And yon embattled navy on the sea,<br>  Rounding their moony circle?" "Mine." -- "And thou?"<br>  "Methinks, most solemn questioner, the helm<br>  Might well proclaim Pendragon." -- "No, the brow, [15]<br>  Whereon that scaly blazon us'd to glow,<br>  Had ne'er been girding with unnatural siege<br>  A British castle, while all Britain lay<br>  In chains beneath the Stranger." -- "What art thou,<br>  That beardest in thy high and taunting vein [20]<br>  The Princes of the land?" -- "A Prince." -- "Thus arm'd<br>  And thus attir'd!" -- "Misjudging, must thou learn<br>  The actions are the raiment of the man,<br>  Better to serve my country in worn weeds<br>  And dinted arms like mine, than 'gainst her sons [25]<br>  To lace a golden panoply.  This rust,<br>  'Tis Saxon blood, for thine, its only praise<br>  Is its bright stainlessness.  Look not, fierce Prince,<br>  As from my veins its earliest spots should fall,<br>  'Tis Britain barbs the arrows that I speak, [30]<br>  And makes thy heart its mark." -- "What man or more<br>  Thus fires and freezes, angers and controls<br>  With the majestic valour of his tongue,<br>  The never yet controll'd, and bears the name<br>  Of Britain, like a shield before him, broad [35]<br>  And firm against my ripe and bursting wrath?<br>  Samor!  come, honour'd warrior, to my arms;<br>  Oh shame to see, and seeing not to know<br>  The noblest of our isle." -- "No arms may fold<br>  Samor within them, but a Briton's; thou [40]<br>  By this apostate war disown'st the name,<br>  And leaguest dark alliance with her foes."<br>  <br>  "Ah, then thou know'st not, in yon rock is mew'd<br>  The crafty kite that hath my dove in thrall.<br>  My dove, my bride, my sweet Ignera; her [45]<br>  That Gorlois with his privy talon swoop'd,<br>  The gentle, the defenceless, and looks down<br>  From his air-swinging eyrie on my wrath,<br>  That like the sea against the rooted rock,<br>  Lashes and roars in vain." -- "Thy bride!" -- "My bride,<br>  By holy words in saintly chapel spoke; [51]<br>  And all before the twilight meeting stolen,<br>  Upon the shelly beach, when came my bark<br>  Sliding with smooth oar through the soundless spray<br>  From the Armoric shore, and vows so fond [55]<br>  The unfelt waters crept up round our feet;<br>  All after rapturous union undisturb'd,<br>  Her father's blessing on our bridal couch,<br>  Promise of infant pledges, all o'erthrown,<br>  All wither'd by that Gorlois, that low worm [60]<br>  I were too proud to tread on heretofore;<br>  He with some cold and antiquated plea<br>  Of broken compact by the sire, away<br>  Reft with a villain stealth th' ill-guarded gem,<br>  And hoards it in his lone and trackless cave." [65]<br>  <br>  "A darker and more precious theft has been:<br>  This Britain hath been stolen, this fair isle,<br>  This land of free-born Christian men become<br>  The rapine of fierce Heathens.  Uther, hear,<br>  Hear, son of Constantine!  most dear the ties [70]<br>  Of wedlock earthly woven, yet seal'd by God;<br>  But those that link us to our native land.<br>  Are wrought out from th' eternal adamant<br>  By the Almighty.  Oh thy country's call<br>  Loud with a thousand voices drowns the tone [75]<br>  Of sweet-complaining even from wife belov'd --<br>  Forego the weaker, Uther, and obey<br>  The stronger duty." -- "Bloodless man and cold,<br>  Or wrong I thee; perchance the Saxon holds<br>  Thy Emeric, and my claims must cede to thine, [80]<br>  Even as all beauties to that peerless star." --<br>  <br>  Spare, Uther, spare thy taunting, she is safe,<br>  Briton or Saxon harm not her." -- "'Tis well,<br>  Fair tidings!  but thy shuddering brow looks white."<br>  "There's a cold safety, Uther, with the dead, [85]<br>  There is where foes disturb no more, the grave."<br>  "Pardon me, friend -- oh pardon -- but my wife,<br>  She too will seek that undisturbed place,<br>  Ere yield to that pale craven's love; if false<br>  She dare not live, and yet, oh yet she lives." [90]<br>  <br>  Uprose the Avenger, and his way he took<br>  To where the rock broke off abrupt and sheer.<br>  Before him yawn'd the chasm, whose depth of gloom<br>  Sever'd the island Castle from the shore:<br>  The ocean waves, as though but newly rent [95]<br>  That narrow channel, tumbled to and fro,<br>  Rush'd and recoil'd, and sullenly sent up<br>  An everlasting roar, deep echoed out<br>  From th' underworking caverns; the white gulls<br>  Were wandering in the dusk abyss, and shone [100]<br>  Faint sunlight here and there on the moist slate.<br>  The Castle drawbridge hung aloof, arm'd men<br>  Pac'd the stern ramparts, javelins look'd out<br>  From embrasure and loop-hole, arbalist<br>  And bowstring loaded lay with weight of shaft [105]<br>  Menacing.  On the dizzy brink stood up<br>  Th' Avenger, like a Seraph when absolv'd<br>  His earthly mission, on some sunny peak<br>  He waits the gathering cloud, whereon he wont<br>  To charioteer along the azure space; [110]<br>  In vain he waits not, under his plum'd feet,<br>  And round about his spreading wings it floats,<br>  And sails off proudly with its heavenly freight.<br>  Even thus at Samor's call down heavy fell<br>  The drawbridge, o'er the abyss th' Avenger springs; [115]<br>  Tintagel's huge portcullis groaning up<br>  Its grooves gives way; then up the jealous bridge<br>  Behind him leaps, the gate falls clashing down.<br>  <br>  Half wonder, and half fear, Pendragon shook<br>  The terrors of his crest, and gasping stood, [120]<br>  As when a hunter is gone in to brave<br>  The bear within his shaggy den, down peers<br>  His fellow through the dusk, and fears to see<br>  What his keen eyes strain after.  But elate<br>  Appear'd upon the rampart that tall Chief, [125]<br>  Seeming on th' outpour'd garrison to cast<br>  Words potent as the fabled wizard's oils,<br>  With the terrific smoothness of their fire<br>  Wide sheeting the hush'd ocean; th' arbalist<br>  Discharg'd its unaim'd bolt, the arrow fell [130]<br>  From the slack bowstring; careless of his charge,<br>  The watchman from his turret lean'd, o'er all<br>  Bright'ning and stilling the high language spread,<br>  Giving a cast of pride to vulgar brows,<br>  Shedding o'er stupor and thick-breathing awe [135]<br>  A solemn hue of glory: Far it spread<br>  Beyond the sphere of sound, th' indignant brow,<br>  The stately waving of the arm discours'd,<br>  Flow'd argument from every comely limb,<br>  And the whole man was eloquence.  From cliff, [140]<br>  From bark gaz'd Uther's soldiery, one voice<br>  Held in suspense the wild and busy war,<br>  And on the motion of his lips, the fate<br>  Of two strong armies hung.  Anon the gate<br>  Flew up, the bridge lay shuddering o'er the chasm. [145]<br>  <br>  Forth Samor comes, a Lady by his side,<br>  And Gorlois in the garb of peace behind.<br>  Tremblingly she came gliding on, and smooth,<br>  As the west wind o'er beds of flowers, a child<br>  Was with her: the cool freshness of the air [150]<br>  Seem'd o'er her marble cheek a flush unus'd<br>  To breathe, and human faces o'er her threw<br>  A modest, faint disturbance.  Uther rush'd<br>  To meet her, ere he came her failing frame<br>  Seem'd as it sougth some breast to sink upon, [155]<br>  Though feebly resolute, that none but his<br>  Should be the chosen resting place.  But he<br>  Severe withheld her. -- "Can the snowdrop bloom<br>  Untainted on the hemlock bank, near thee,<br>  Igerna, long hath trail'd a venomous plant, [160]<br>  Hast thou the sullying influence scap'd?" -- She strove<br>  To work displeasure to her brow, the joy,<br>  The fondness would not give it place; she held<br>  Her boy on high, she pointed from the lines<br>  Of his soft face to Uther's, with appeal [165]<br>  Half rapture, half reproach, and cast herself<br>  With timid boldness on her rightful couch,<br>  Her husband's bosom, that receiv'd her in,<br>  Even as the opening clouds an angel home<br>  Returning.  But the joyous boy relax'd [170]<br>  His features to a beautiful delight;<br>  To the fierce Dragon on his father's helm<br>  Lifting his sportive hand, and smoothing down<br>  The horrent scales, and looking with glad eye<br>  Into the fiery hollow of his jaws. [175]<br>  <br>  Mute lay the armies, the pale Gorlois wrought<br>  His features to a politic joy, alone<br>  Stood Samor and aloof, he stood in tears.<br>  Samor, amid the plain of buried men<br>  Tearless, and in his own deserted home, [180]<br>  In tears unveil'd before th' assembled camp;<br>  It was so like a meeting after death,<br>  That union of the husband and the wife,<br>  So ghostly, so unearthly.  Thus shall meet<br>  The disembodied, Emeric and himself, [185]<br>  Not with rude rocks their footing, the cold airs<br>  And cloudy sunshine of this world around.<br>  But all of life must intervene, and all<br>  The long dark grave mysterious; yet even here<br>  It was a sweet impossibility, [190]<br>  Wherewith at times his soul mad dalliance held,<br>  An earthly, bodily, sensible caress,<br>  Even long and rapturous, as that hanging now<br>  On Uther's neck from soft Igerna's arms.<br>  <br>  Upon the silence burst a voice, that cried [195]<br>  "Arthur," whereat the child his sport broke off<br>  With that embossed serpent, and stretch'd out<br>  His arms, where, on the fragment of a rock,<br>  Stood Merlin.  "Arthur, hail!  hail, fatal Boy,<br>  Bright arrow from the bow of Destiny, [200]<br>  Go forth upon thy fiery course!  the steeds<br>  Are in the meadows, that shall bear thee forth,<br>  Thee and thy barded chivalry!  the spears<br>  Are forg'd wherewith in tourney and in fight<br>  Ye shall o'erbear the vaunting Saxon!  shields [205]<br>  Are stamping with your bright devices bold;<br>  And Bards are leaning on their high-strung harps,<br>  Awaiting thee, to flower out in their boon<br>  And ripe fertility of song.  Go forth,<br>  Strong reaper in the harvest of renown, [210]<br>  Arthur!  the everlasting Lord of Fate<br>  Hath summon'd thee to thy immortal race!"<br>  <br>  The infant clapp'd his hands, Pendragon flung<br>  Aloft his scaly bickering crest, her child<br>  Igerna folded to her heart, and wept. [215]<br>  And forward leap'd the Avenger to salute<br>  Snowdon's dark Prophet, Merlin was not there.<br>  <br>  Good fortune on good fortune followeth fast;<br>  Tidings come rapid of a Breton fleet<br>  Seen on the southern shore; the Chiefs are past [220]<br>  To where th' Archangel's Mount o'erlooks the sea.<br>  <br>  Oh go not to thy couch, thou bright hair'd Sun!<br>  Though Ocean spread its welcoming breast, yet pause<br>  'Mid that etherial architecture wrought<br>  Around thee by thine own creative light. [225]<br>  How broad the over-vaulting palace arch<br>  Spreads up the heavens with amethyst ciel'd, and hung<br>  With an enwoven tapestry of flame,<br>  Wav'd over by long banner and emblaz'd,<br>  Like hall of old barbaric Potentate, [230]<br>  With scutcheon and with shield, that now unfold,<br>  Now in their cloudy texture shift; and paved<br>  With watery mosaic rich, the waves<br>  Quick glancing, like a floating surface, laid<br>  With porphyry and crystal interwrought. [345]<br>  There's yet a sight, oh Sun!  to check awhile<br>  Thy setting; lo, the failing breezes lift<br>  The white wings of that fair Armoric fleet<br>  To catch the level lines of light; the oars<br>  Flash up the spray, that purples as it falls: [240]<br>  While wearing one by one, their armed freight<br>  They cast out on the surfy beach.  The Kings,<br>  King Emrys and Armoric Hoel meet<br>  Pendragon, Samor, and their band of chiefs.<br>  <br>  There meet they on the land's extremest verge [245]<br>  To conquer, to deliver, few, but strong,<br>  Strong in the sinews of the soul; as rose<br>  The giant wrestler from his mother's breast,<br>  Earth-born Anteus, his huge limbs refresh'd<br>  For the Herculean combat, so shall ye, [250]<br>  Kings, Chiefs, and Warriors, from your native soil<br>  Draw to the immortal faculties of mind<br>  A springtide everlasting and unchang'd.<br>  The armour of a holy cause outshines<br>  The iron or the knosped brass, and hopes [255]<br>  And memories to the home-returning brave<br>  Crowding from every speck of sacred earth,<br>  Outplead the trumpet's wakening blast, till leaps<br>  Vengeance to Glory's vanguard post, and leads<br>  The onset, and looks proudly down to see [260]<br>  The red blood deepening round her laving feet.<br>  <br>  Alas, that in your harvest of high thoughts,<br>  Thick set with golden promise of renown,<br>  The poppy seeds of envy and distrust<br>  Should take their baleful root.  Slow winds along [265]<br>  Gorlois, the sower of that noxious crop,<br>  Scattering it with careless toil; now stands<br>  By royal Emrys' side, now mines beneath<br>  Pendragon's towery soul, now sadly warns<br>  With cautious words and dark speech broken off, [270]<br>  Hoel, the crown'd Armorican; his looks<br>  Belying his feign'd confidence of speech,<br>  But half surmising fear, and killing hope<br>  By his cold care of keeping it alive.<br>  <br>  "Not that I love not, whom all love, admire [275]<br>  On whom the admiration of all hearts<br>  Falls with such free profusion, 'tis no shame<br>  For us mean lamps before great Samor's light<br>  To wane and glimmer in our faint eclipse.<br>  Yet whence this fettering of all eyes and hearts? [280]<br>  This stern unsocial solitude of fame?<br>  True, from that fatal banquet 'scap'd he, true<br>  Undaunted hath he rov'd the isle, nor doubt<br>  From some high purpose, that 'twere rash for us<br>  To search our with our dim and misty sight; [285]<br>  Nor think, King Emrys, I thy crown assert<br>  Unstably set upon thy royal brow,<br>  But there's a dazzling in its jewel'd round<br>  Might tempt a less self-mastering grasp.  Who holds<br>  The souls of men in thraldom with his tongue, [290]<br>  Makes bridges grow before him, stony walls<br>  Break up to give him way, -- I speak not now<br>  In vengeance of Tintagel, 'twas a deed<br>  Most worth my richest praise, that made me friend<br>  To brave Pendragon.
