But ambition wreck'd [295]<br>  The angels, and the climbing soul of man<br>  Hath sinn'd for meaner gain than Britain's throne." --<br>  <br>  So one by one he wound his serpent coil<br>  Around the Chieftains souls; and inly breath'd<br>  The creeping venom.  But Pendragon's heart, [300]<br>  Too fiery or too noble to suspect,<br>  In Samor's teeth flung fierce th' oppressive doubt:<br>  Th' Avenger's tranquil smile was like the change<br>  Of aspect in a green and lofty tree,<br>  Touch'd by the wings of some faint breeze, nor shakes [305]<br>  The massy foliage, nor is quite at rest,<br>  While languidly the undisturbing air<br>  Falls away and expires.  "Will Emrys hold<br>  At midnight on St. Michael's Mount, his pomp<br>  Of coronation?  Samor will be there." [310]<br>  "At midnight!" -- "Aye, the fires will gaily blaze,<br>  The silent air is meet for solemn oaths." --<br>  The night is starless, soft and still, the heavens<br>  O'erwoven with a thin and rayless mist;<br>  A long low heavy sound of breaking surge [315]<br>  Roams down the shore, and now and then the woods<br>  Flutter and bend with one short rush of wind.<br>  The tide hath risen o'er the stony belt,<br>  That to the mainland links the Mount, where meet<br>  Even now the Chieftains, ocean all around, [320]<br>  On every side the white and moaning waves.<br>  On the bare summit, 'neath the cope of heaven,<br>  The conclave stands, bare, save a lofty pile<br>  Of wood compacted like funereal pyre<br>  Of a departed hero in old time<br>  On some Ægean promontory rear'd,<br>  Or by the Black Inhospitable Sea.<br>  <br>  The crown is on king Emry's head, his hair<br>  Is redolent with the anointing oil.<br>  "Hail, King of Britain!" -- Samor cried, and "Hail!"<br>  Replied that band of heroes; Hail! the shores [330]<br>  Echoed, from bark and tent came pealing up<br>  The universal Hail, the ocean waves<br>  Broke in with their hoarse murmur of applause.<br>  <br>  "Air, earth, and waters, ye have play'd your part, [335]<br>  There's yet another elemnt," -- cried aloud<br>  Samor, and in the pyre he cast a brand.<br>  A moment, and uprush'd the giant fire,<br>  Piercing the dim heavens with its blazing brow,<br>  And on the still air shakings its red locks. [340]<br>  There by its side the Vassals and their King,<br>  Motionless on their shadows huge and dun,<br>  Show'd like destroying Angels, round enwrap'd<br>  In their careering pomp of flame; far flash'd<br>  The yellow midnight day o'er shore and sea: [345]<br>  The waves now ruddy heav'd, now darkly plung'd,<br>  Upon the rocks, within the wavering light<br>  Strong featur'd faces fierce, and hard-lin'd forms<br>  Broke out and disappear'd; the anchor'd fleet<br>  Were laving their brown sides in rainbow spray. [350]<br>  No sound was heard, but the devouring flame,<br>  And the thick plashing waters. -- "Keep your faith,<br>  (Cried Samor) ye eternal hills, and ye<br>  Heaven-neighbouring mountains!" -- Eastward far anon<br>  Another fire rose furious up, behind [355]<br>  Another and another: all the hills<br>  Each behind each held up its crest of flame;<br>  Along the heavens the bright and crimson hue<br>  Widening and deepening travels on, the range<br>  O'erleaps black Tamar, by whose ebon tide [360]<br>  Cornwall is bounded, and on Heytor rock,<br>  Above the stony moorish source of Dart,<br>  It waves a sanguine standard; Haldon burns,<br>  And the Red City glows a deeper hue; [NOTE 14]<br>  And all the southern rocks, the moorland downs [365]<br>  In those portentous characters of flame<br>  Discourse, and bear the glaring legend on,<br>  Even to the graves on Ambri plain, where woke<br>  That pallid woman, and rejoic'd, and deem'd<br>  'Twas sent to guide her to the tomb she sought. [370]<br>  Fast flash they up, those altars of revenge,<br>  As the snake-tressed Sister torch-bearers,<br>  Th' Eumenides, from the Tartarian depths<br>  Were leaping on from hill to hill, on each<br>  Leaving the tracks of their flame-dropping feet. [375]<br>  Or as the souls of the dead fathers, wrapt<br>  In bright meteorous grave-clothes, had arisen,<br>  And each sate crowning his accustom'd hill,<br>  Silent and radiant: or as th' isle devote<br>  Had wrought down by her bold and frequent guilt [380]<br>  Th' Almighty's lightning shafts, now numberless<br>  Forth raining from the lurid reeking clouds,<br>  And smiting all the heights.  On spreads the train,<br>  Northward it breaks upon the Quantock ridge,<br>  It reddens on the Mendip forests dark, [385]<br>  It looks into the cavern'd Cheddar cliffs,<br>  The boatman on the Severn mouth awakes<br>  And sees the waters rippling round his keel<br>  In spots and streaks of purple light, each shore<br>  Ablaze with all its answering hills; the streams [390]<br>  Run glittering down Plinlimmon's side, though thick<br>  And moonless the wan night: and Idris stands<br>  Like Stromboli or Ætna, where 'twas feign'd<br>  E'er at their flashing furnace wrought the Sons<br>  Of Vulcan, forging with eternal toil [395]<br>  Jove's never idle thunderbolts.  And thou,<br>  Snowdon, the king of mountains, art not dark<br>  Amid thy vassal brethren gleaming bright.<br>  Is it to welcome thy returning Seer,<br>  That thus above thy clouds, above thy snows [400]<br>  Thou wear'st that wreathed diadem of fire,<br>  As to outshine the pale and winking stars?<br>  O'er Menai's waters blue the gleaming spreads,<br>  The Bard on Mona's secret grove beholds<br>  A glitter on his harp-strings, and looks out [405]<br>  Upon the kindling cliffs of Penmanmawr.<br>  Is it a pile of martyrdom above<br>  Clwyd's green vale?  beside the embers bright<br>  Stands holy Germain, as a Saint new come<br>  From the pure mansions of beautitude, [410]<br>  The centre of a glory, that spreads round<br>  Its film of thin pellucid gold.  Nor there<br>  Pauses the restless Messenger, still on<br>  Vaults it from rock to rock, from peak to peak.<br>  Far seen it shimmer'd on Caer-Ebranc wall, [415]<br>  And Malwyn blew a bugle blast for joy.<br>  The sun uprising sees the dusk night fled<br>  Already from tall Pendle, and the height<br>  Of Ingleborough, sees Helvellyn cast<br>  A meteor splendour on the mountain lakes, [420]<br>  Like mirrors of the liquid molten brass.<br>  The brightest and the broadest and the last,<br>  There flakes the beacon glare, and in the midst<br>  Dashing the ruddy sparkles to and fro<br>  With the black remnant of pine-tree stem, [425]<br>  Stands arm'd from head to foot Prince Vortimer.<br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> BOOK XI.</b> </h3>   Mighty in thy endurance, in revenge<br>  Mightier!  thou shak'st thy dusky patience off,<br>  Oh Britain!  as a snake its wither'd skin,<br>  That boastful to the sunshine coils and spreads<br>  In bright and cruel beauty.  Not in vain [5]<br>  Have those wild beacons rear'd their fires, thou wak'st,<br>  The slumber falls from thee, as dewdrops shed<br>  From the morn-kindling falcon's wing.  On hill,<br>  In vale, in forest and in moor, in field<br>  And city, like the free and common air, [10]<br>  Like the wide-spreading golden hue of dawn,<br>  Ranges the boundless passion uncontroll'd.<br>  The "Vigilance," hath drop'd absorb'd away<br>  From the fierce war-cry, one portending word<br>  "Vengeance," rides lonely upon all the winds. [15]<br>  <br>  Alas, delicious Spring!  God sends thee down<br>  To breathe upon his cold and perish'd works<br>  Beauteous revival; earth should welcome thee,<br>  Thee and the West wind, thy smooth paramour,<br>  With the soft laughter of her flowery meads, [20]<br>  Her joys, her melodies.  The prancing stag<br>  Flutters the shivering fern, the steed shakes out<br>  His mane, the dewy herbage silver-webb'd<br>  With frank step trampling; the wild goat looks down<br>  From his empurpling bed of heath, where break [25]<br>  The waters deep and blue with crystal gleams<br>  Of their quick leaping people: the fresh lark<br>  Is in the morning sky, the nightingale<br>  Tunes evesong to the dropping waterfall.<br>  Creation lives with loveliness, all melts [30]<br>  And trembles into one mild harmony.<br>  Man, only harsh and inharmonious Man,<br>  Strews for thy delicate feet the battle field,<br>  Makes all thy smooth and flowing airs to jar<br>  With his hoarse trumpetings, scares thy sweet light [35]<br>  With gleams of violent and angry brass.<br>  <br>  Away!  it is a yearly common joy,<br>  A rapture that ne'er fails the solemn Sun<br>  In his eternal round, the blossoming<br>  And fragrance of the green resolving earth. [40]<br>  But a fresh springtide in the human soul,<br>  A nation from its wintry trance set loose,<br>  The bursting ice of servitude, the bloom<br>  Of freedom in the wither'd mind obscure,<br>  The bleakness of the heart discomfited, [45]<br>  And over the bow'd shape and darkling brow<br>  The flowering out of faded glories, sounds<br>  Of cheering and of comfort to the rent<br>  And broken by the tyrannous northern blast,<br>  These are earth's rich adornings, these the choice [50]<br>  Of nature's bounteous, and inspiring shows.<br>  Therefore the young Sun with his prime of light<br>  Shall beam on ensigns; the blithe airs shall waft<br>  Jocund the lofty pealing battle words;<br>  And not unwelcome, fierce crests intercept [55]<br>  The spring-dews from the thirsty soil; the brass<br>  For vestment the admiring earth shall wear<br>  More proud than all her flowery robe of green.<br>  <br>  In all the isle was flat subjection tame,<br>  In all the isle, hath Freedom rear'd her, plum'd [60]<br>  With terror, sandal'd with relentlessness:<br>  Her march like brazen chariots, or the tramp<br>  Of horsemen in a rocky glen; and clouds<br>  Of javelins in her front, and in her rear<br>  Dead men in grisly heaps, dead Saxons strewn [65]<br>  Upon their trampled White Horse banners: them<br>  Her fury hath no time to scorn, no pause<br>  To look back on her deathful deeds atchiev'd,<br>  While aught remains before her to atchieve.<br>  Distract amid the wide spread feast of blood, [70]<br>  The wandering raven knows not where to feed,<br>  And the gorg'd vulture droops his wing and sleeps.<br>  <br>  War hath the garb of holiness, bear proof,<br>  Thou vale of Clwyd, to our cold late days,<br>  By the embalming of tradition named, [75]<br>  Maes Garmon, of that saintly Bishop.  He<br>  His gray thin locks unshaken, his slow port<br>  Calm as he trod a chapel's rush-strewn floor,<br>  Comes foremost of his Christian mountaineers,<br>  Against th' embattled Pagans fierce array. [80]<br>  By the green margin of the stream, the band<br>  Of Arngrim glitter in the morning light.<br>  Their shadowy lances line the marble stream<br>  With long and level rules of trembling shade;<br>  The sunshine falling in between in streaks [85]<br>  Of brightness.  They th' unwonted shew of war<br>  Behold slow winding down the wooded hill.<br>  <br>  "Now by our Gods," cried Arngrim, "discontent<br>  To scare our midnight with their insolent fires,<br>  They break upon our calm and peaceful day." [90]<br>  But silent as the travel of the clouds<br>  At breathless twilight, or a flock that winds,<br>  Dappling the brown cliff with its snowy specks,<br>  Foldward along the evening dews, a bell<br>  Now and then tinkling, faintly shrill, come on [95]<br>  Outspreading on the meadow the stern band<br>  Of Britons with their mitred Captain; front<br>  Oppos'd to front they stand, and spear to spear.<br>  Then Germain clasp'd his hands and look'd to heaven,<br>  Then Germain in a deep and solemn tone [100]<br>  Cried "Alleluia!" answer was flung back:<br>  From cliff and cavern, "Alleluia," burst;<br>  It seem'd strong voices broke the bosom'd earth,<br>  Dropt voices from the clouds, and in the rush<br>  Of waters was an human clamour, far [105] [NOTE 15]<br>  Swept over all things in its boundless range<br>  The scattering and discomfiting appeal:<br>  'Twas shaken from the shivering forest leaves,<br>  Ceaseless and countless, lifeless living things [110]<br>  Multiplied "Alleluia," all the air<br>  Was that one word, all sounds became that sound,<br>  As the broad lightning swallows up all lights,<br>  All quench'd in one blue universal glare.<br>  <br>  On rush'd the Britons, but 'gainst the flying foes, [115]<br>  Quick smote the Britons, but no breast plate clove<br>  Before them, then the ignominous death<br>  First through the back found way to Saxon hearts.<br>  <br>  Oh, Suevian forests!  Clwyd's vale beholds<br>  What ye have never witnessed, Arngrim's flight -- [120]<br>  Fleet huntsman, thou art now the deer, the herd,<br>  Whereof thou wert the prime and lofty horn'd,<br>  Are falling fast around thee, th' unleash'd dogs<br>  Of havock on their reeking flanks, and thee,<br>  The herdsman of the meek and peaceful goats, [125]<br>  Thee, the soft tuner of the reedy flute<br>  Beside Nantfrangon's stony cataract,<br>  Mordrin pursues.  So strong that battle word,<br>  Its holy transmutation and austere<br>  Works in the soul of man, the spirit sheathes [130]<br>  In the thrice folding brass of valour, swells<br>  The thin and lazy blood t' a current fierce<br>  And torrent like, and in the breast erewhile<br>  But open to the tremulous melting airs<br>  Of passions gentle and affections smooth, [135]<br>  Plants armed hopes and eagle-wing'd desires.<br>  Therefore that youth his downy hand hath wreath'd<br>  In the strong Suevian's knotted locks, drawn up<br>  Like a wrought helm of ebon; therefore fix<br>  His eyes, more us'd to swim in languid light, [140]<br>  With an implacable and constant stare<br>  Down on the face of Arngrim, backward drawn,<br>  As he its withering agony enjoy'd;<br>  And therefore he whose wont it was to bear<br>  The many sparkling crystal, or the cup [145]<br>  Of dripping water lily from the spring<br>  To the blithe maiden of his love, now shakes<br>  A gory and dissever'd head aloft,<br>  And bounds in wild ovation down the vale.<br>  <br>  But in that dire and beacon haunted night [150]<br>  King Vortigern his wonted seat had ta'en<br>  Upon Caermerdhyn's topmost palace tower.<br>  There, the best privilege of greatness fall'n,<br>  He saw not, nor was seen: there wrapt in gloom,<br>  'Twas his soul's treasur'd luxury and choice joy [155]<br>  To frame out of himself and his drear state,<br>  Dark comfortable likenesses, and full<br>  And frequent throng'd they this wild midnight.  All<br>  Cloudy and indistinct lay round; the sole<br>  Dull glimmering like to light was what remain'd [160]<br>  Of day, just not so utterly extinct<br>  And quench'd, as yet to shew splendour had been,<br>  And was not; that dusk simile of himself<br>  Delighted, royal once, now with a mock<br>  And mimic of his lustre haunted.  Why, [165]<br>  Why should not human glory wane, since clouds<br>  Put out the immortal planets in the sky?<br>  Why should not crowns have seasons, since the moon<br>  Hath but her hour to queen it in the heavens?<br>  Why should not high and climbing souls be lost [170]<br>  In the benighting shroud of the world's gloom?<br>  Lo, one inglorious, undistinguish'd night<br>  Gathers the ancient mountains in its train,<br>  While e'er the dunnest and most turbulent clouds<br>  Thicken upon the stateliest; but beneath [175]<br>  The lowly and contented waters lie<br>  Asleep upon their weedy banks, yet they<br>  Have all the faint blue brightness that remains.<br>  Then moodier the fantastic humour grown,<br>  Stoop'd upon mean and trivial things, them too [180]<br>  Wrought to his wayward misanthropic scope.<br>  Amid the swaying, and disturbed air<br>  The rooks hung murmuring on the oak-tree tops,<br>  As plaining their uneasy loftiness.<br>  While, solitary as himself, the owl [185]<br>  Sate calling on its deaf and wandering mate.<br>  Him at that sound seiz'd merriment, that made<br>  The lip drop, the brow writhe, "Howl on," he cried,<br>  "Howl for thy dusky paramour," -- and turn'd<br>  To where Rowena's chamber casements stood, [190]<br>  Void, silent, dark of their once-brilliant lights.<br>  <br>  Sudden around 'gan spire the mountain tops<br>  Each with its intertwisted sheaf of flame,<br>  South, North, and East and West, fire everywhere,<br>  Everywhere flashing and tumultuous light. [195]<br>  Then gaz'd the unking'd, then cried out the fallen,<br>  "Now, by my soul, when comets gaze on kings<br>  Even from the far and vaulting heavens, 'tis faith<br>  There's hollowness beneath their tottering thrones;<br>  But when they flash upon our earth, and stare [200]<br>  Close in our faces, 'tis ripe time and full<br>  For palaces to quake and royal tombs<br>  To ope their wide and all-receiving jaws.<br>  What is't to me?  ye menace at the Great!<br>  Ye stoop not to be dangerous and dread, [205]<br>  Oh haughty and mysterious lights!  to thrones<br>  Low and despis'd like mine; in earlier days<br>  Vortigern would have quail'd, he mocks you now.<br>  Ye are not of the heavens, I know, I see,<br>  Discomfiters of darkness, Conquerors [210]<br>   Of midnight, ye are of the earth.  Why stands<br>  Caermerdhyn and the realm of Dyfed black<br>  Amid this restless multitude of flame?<br>  'Tis not for idle or for fruitless show<br>  That with such splendid violation Man [215]<br>  Infringeth on stern nature's laws, and rends<br>  From night her consecrate and ancient pall;<br>  Samor, thy hand is there!  and Vortigern<br>  Hath not yet learnt the patience cold and tame<br>  To be outblaz'd and stifled thus." -- Down past [220]<br>  The Monarch from his seat; few minutes fled,<br>  And lo, within that Palace all look'd red,<br>  And hurried with a deep confusing glare:<br>  And over it a vaulting dome of smoke<br>  Surging arose and vast, till roaring out [225]<br>  Columns of mounting fire sprung up, and all<br>  Whelm'd in one broad envelopment of flame,<br>  Stood; as when in heroic Pagan song<br>  Apollo to his Clarian temple came;<br>  At once the present Godhead kindled all [230]<br>  Th' elaborate architecture, glory-wreath'd<br>  The pillars rose, the sculptur'd architrave<br>  Swam in the liquid gold, the Worshipper<br>  Within the vestible of marble pure,<br>  Held up his hand before his blinded eyes, [235]<br>  And so ador'd: but th' unconsuming fire<br>  Innoxious rang'd th' unparching edifice.<br>  But ne'er was Palace or was Monarch seen<br>  More in that city, one a smouldering heap<br>  Lay in its ashes white; how went the King [240]<br>  And whither, no one knew, but He who knows<br>  All things.  'Twas frequent in the vulgar tale,<br>  None saw it, yet all knew them well that saw, [NOTE 16]<br>  At midnight manifest a huge arm came<br>  Forth from the welkin; once it wav'd and twice, [245]<br>  And then it was not: but a bolt thrice fork'd,<br>  Each fork a spike of flame, burst on the roof,<br>  And all became a fire, and all fell down<br>  And smoulder'd, even as now the shapeless walls<br>  Lie in scorch'd heaps and black.  At that same hour [250]<br>  A dark steed and a darker rider past,<br>  With speed bemocking mortal steed, or man,<br>  Down the steep hill precipitous: 'twas like<br>   In shape and hue black Favorin, on whose back<br>  King Vortigern was wont to ride abroad; [255]<br>  Like, surely not the same, for fire came out<br>  From under his quick hoofs, and in his breath,<br>  And sulphurous the blasted foot-tracks smelt,<br>  Some dinted deep in the hard rock, some seared<br>  On meadow grass, where never since have dews [260]<br>  Lain glittering, never the fresh verdure sprung.<br>  <br>  Now is the whole Isle war.  But I must crave<br>  Pardon from those in meaner conflict slain,<br>  Or conquerors; Poesy's fair treasure house<br>  Contains not all the bright and rich, that gem [265]<br>  The course of humankind; in heaven alone<br>  Preserves enroll'd th' imperishable brass,<br>  In letters deep of amaranthine light,<br>  All martyrs to their country and their God.<br>  <br>  Oh that my spirit, holding the broad glass [270]<br>  Of its invention, might at once condense<br>  All rays of glory from the kindling Isle<br>  Full emanating, as of old 'tis famed<br>  The philosophic Syracusan caught<br>  The wide diverging sunbeams, by the force [275]<br>  Of mind creating to himself a right<br>  And property in nature's common gifts,<br>  And domineering the free elements.<br>  He that heaven-seiz'd artillery pour'd forth<br>  To sear the high beaks of the 'sieging fleet, [280]<br>  That burnt, unknowing whence, 'mid the wet waves.<br>  <br>  So I the fine immortal light would pour<br>  Abroad, in the long after-time to beam<br>  A consecrate and vestal fire, to guide<br>  Through danger's precipices wild, the slopes [285]<br>  Sleepy and smooth of luxury and false bliss,<br>  All lovers of their country.  They my song<br>  Embosoming within their heart of heart,<br>  Like mine own Samor should bear on, too strong<br>  To perish, and too haughty to despair. [290]<br>  They happier, he uprearing on the sand<br>  A Pharos, steady for a while to stem<br>  The fierce assaulting waves, in after times<br>  To fall; they building for eternity<br>  Britain's rock-founded temple of renown. [295]<br>  <br>  In the Isle's centre is a champain broad,<br>  Now broken into cornfield and smooth mead,<br>  Near which a hill, now with the ruin'd towers<br>  Of Coningsborough (from that fight of Kings<br>  Nam'd in old Saxon phrase), soars crested, Dune [300]<br>  Skirts with her azure belt the level plain.<br>  <br>  Morn dawn'd with all her attributes, the slow<br>  Impearling of the heavens, the sparkling white<br>  On the webb'd grass, the fragrant mistiness,<br>  The fresh airs with the twinkling leaves at sport, [305]<br>  And all the gradual and emerging light,<br>  The crystalline distinctness settling clear,<br>  And all the wakening and the strengthening sound.<br>  <br>  There dawn'd she on a battle-field superb.<br>  The beauty that is war's embellishment, [310]<br>  The splendour under whose quick-glancing pall<br>  Man proudly moves to slay and to be slain,<br>  How wonderful!  In semicircle huge,<br>  Round that hill foot, the Saxon camps his strength,<br>  A many-colour'd dazzling cirque, more rich [315]<br>  Than the autumnal woods, when the quick winds<br>  Shake on them broken sunlight, than the skies<br>  When thunder clouds are bursting into light,<br>  And rainbow skirted hangs each fold, or fring'd<br>  With liquid gold, so wav'd that crescent broad [320]<br>  With moving fire, bloom'd all the field with brass:<br>  Making of dread volumptuousness, the sense<br>  Of danger in deep admiration lost --<br>  Oh beauteous if that morning had no eve!<br>  <br>  The Eastern horn, his tall steeds to his car [325]<br>  Harness'd, whose scythes shone newly burnish'd, held<br>  Caswallon; he his painted soldiery,<br>  Their naked breasts blue-gleaming with uncouth<br>  And savage portraitures of hideous things,<br>  Human and monstrous terribly combin'd, [330]<br>  Array'd; himself no armour of defence<br>  Cumber'd, as he were one Death dare not slay,<br>  A being from man's vulgar lot exempt,<br>  Commission'd to destroy, yet dangerless<br>  Amid destruction, against whom war shower'd [335]<br>  All its stor'd terrors, but still baffled back<br>  Recoil'd from his unwounded front serene.<br>  <br>  The centre were the blue-eyed Germans, loose<br>  Their fierce hair, various each strong nation's arms,<br>  A wild and terrible diversity [340]<br>  In the fell skill of slaughter, in the art<br>  Of doing sacrifice to death.  Some helm'd,<br>  Whose visors like distended jaws appear'd<br>  Of sylvan monster, some in brinded furs<br>  Wrapt shaggy, on whose shoulders seem'd to ramp [345]<br>  Yet living the fix'd claws; with cross-bows some,<br>  Some with long lances, some with falchions curv'd.<br>  The Arian, wont to make the sable night<br>  A pander to his terrors, in swarth arms [NOTE 17]<br>  He bursting from the forest, when the shades [350]<br>  Were deepest, like embodied gloom advanc'd,<br>  Shap'd for some dreadful purpose, now he mov'd<br>  Unnatural 'mid the clear and golden day.<br>  Here Hengist, Horsa there amid the troop<br>  Wound their war-horses; he his weapon fell [355]<br>  Shook, a round ball of iron spikes chain'd loose<br>  To a huge pike-stave, like a baleful star,<br>  Aye gleaming devastation in its sweep;<br>  Hengist begirt with that fam'd falchion call'd<br>  The "Widower of Women;" over all [360]<br>  The fatal White Horse in the banner shone.<br>  Round to the left Argantyr with the Jutes<br>  And Anglians; these for Offa's slaughter wild<br>  T' exact the usurious payment of revenge;<br>  He sternly mindful of that broken fight [365]<br>  By Wye's clear stream, and his defrauded sword<br>  Of its hope-promis'd banquet, Samor's blood.<br>  Above the multitude of brass the heights<br>  Were crowded with the wives and mothers, they [NOTE 18]<br>  With their known presence working shame of flight, [370]<br>  And the high fear of being thought to fear.<br>  With them the spoils of Britain, vessels carv'd,<br>  Statues, and vestments of the Tyrian dye,<br>  Standards with antique legend scroll'd of deeds<br>  Done in old times, and gorgeous arms, and cups [375]<br>  And lamps, and plate, or by fantastic art<br>  Minister'd to fond luxury's wayward choice,<br>  Or consecrate to th' altar use of God.<br>  <br>  And there the Saxon Gods, the wood and stone<br>  Whereto that people knelt, and deified [380]<br>  Their own hands work; the Father of the race<br>  Woden, all arm'd and crown'd; the tempest Lord,<br>  The thunder-shaking Thor, twelve radiant stars [NOTE 19]<br>  His coronet, and sceptred his right hand.<br>  He on his stately couch reclining; fierce [385]<br>  In his mysterious multitude of signs,<br>  Arminsul; and th' Unnameable, he fix'd [NOTE 20]<br>  On his flint pedestal, his skeleton shape<br>  Garmented scantly in a winding sheet,<br>  And in his hand a torchblaze, meet to search [390]<br>  Earth's utmost, while in act to spring, one hand<br>  Upon his head, upon his shoulder one,<br>  His faithful Lion ramp'd in sculptur'd ire.<br>  <br>  Southward, with crescent its out-stretching horns<br>  Circling the foe, lay stretch'd the British camp; [395]<br>  The centre held King Emrys, on the right<br>  Pendragon, on the left th' Armoric King,<br>  With all his tall steeds and brave riders, they<br>  The fathers of that fam'd chivalric race<br>  Of knights and ladies, glorious in old song, [400]<br>  White handed Iseult, Launcelot of the Lake,<br>  Chaste Perceval, that won the Sangreal quest.<br>  <br>  But every where and in all parts alike<br>  The Avenger held his post; all heard his voice,<br>  All felt his presence, all obey'd his sway. [405]<br>  As western hurricane whirls up from earth,<br>  And bears where'er it will, the loose-sheaf'd corn,<br>  The fluttering leaves, the shatter'd forest boughs,<br>  Even so his spirit seiz'd and bore along,<br>  And swept with it those proud brigades.  Nor there [410]<br>  Was not young Malwyn, he his helmet wore<br>  Light shadow'd by an eagle plume, so sued<br>  His sire, lest in the wildering battle met<br>  Their cars should clash in impious strife, nor sought<br>  The father more obedience from the son, [415]<br>  For Britain and with Samor fix'd to war.<br>  And in his brown and weather beaten arms<br>  Came Vortimer, a pine-tree stem his mace<br>  That clove the air with desultory sweep.<br>  But by the river brows'd a single steed, [420]<br>  Sable as one of that poetic pair,<br>  On the fair plain of Enna, in the yoke<br>  Of Pluto, when Proserpina let fall<br>  From her soft lap her flowers, and mourn'd their loss<br>  Lavish, nor for herself reserv'd her tears. [425]<br>  The horseman, not unlike that ravisher,<br>  Wore kingly aspect, and his step and mien<br>  Were as his realm were in a gloomier clime,<br>  Amid a drearier atmosphere, 'mid things<br>  Sluggish and melancholy, slow and dead. [430]<br>  As though disclaimed by each, and claiming none,<br>  He lay, with cold impartial apathy<br>  Eying both armies, as their fates to him<br>  Were equal, and not worth the toil of hope.<br>  <br>  But over either army silence hung, [435]<br>  Silence long, heavy, deep, as every heart<br>  Were busied with eternity; all thoughts<br>  Were bidding farewell to the Sun, whose rise<br>  They saw, whose setting they might never see,<br>  And all the heavens were thinly overdrawn [440]<br>  With light and golden clouds, as though to couch<br>  The angels and the spirits floating there,<br>  While Heaven the lucid hierarchy pour'd forth<br>  To view that solemn spectacle beneath,<br>  A battle waged for freedom and for faith. [445]<br>  <br>  First rose a clamour and a crowding rush<br>  On the hill side, and an half-stifled cry,<br>  "The Prophetess!  the Prophetess!" was heard.<br>  Upon a waggon, 'mid her idol Gods,<br>  She of the seal'd lip and the haunted heart, [450]<br>  The aged Virgin sate; her thin gray hair [NOTE 21]<br>  And hollow eyes in a strange sparkling steep'd:<br>  Twice in the memory of the oldest spake<br>  Her voice, when Gothic Alaric had set<br>  His northern ensign on Rome's shatter'd walls, [455]<br>  That day along the linden shadow'd Elbe<br>  She went, with bitter smile and broken song<br>  That mock'd at grandeur fall'n and pride in dust.<br>  Once more, when Vortigern in that fam'd feast<br>  Crown'd the fierce Hengist; in the German woods [460]<br>  She roam'd, with lofty and triumphal tone,<br>  Shrieking of sceptres dancing in her sight,<br>  And Woden's sons endiadem'd that rose<br>  And swept and glitter'd past her.  Now with eye<br>  Restless, and churning lip she sate, and thrice [465]<br>  She mutter'd -- "Flight!  Flight!  Flight!"  Then look'd she out<br>  Upon the orient Sun, and cried, "Down!  down!" --<br>  The westward turn'd she, and withdrew her hand,<br>  From dallying with her loose and hanging chin,<br>  And beckon'd to the faint remaining haze [470]<br>  Of twilight.  "Back, fair darkness, beauteous gloom,<br>  Back!"  Still the Sun came on, the shades dispell'd.<br>  Then rose she up, then on the vacant space<br>  Between both armies fix'd her eye; half laugh,<br>  Half agony her cheek relax'd. -- "I see, [475]<br>  I see ye, ye Invisible!  I hear,<br>  Soundless, I hear ye!  Choosers of the slain!<br>  Ye of the white forms hors'd on thunder clouds!<br>  Ye of Valhalla!  colourless as air,<br>  As air impalpable!  wind on and urge [480]<br>  Your sable and self-govern'd steeds; They come,<br>  They whom your mantling hydromel awaits,<br>  Whose cups are crown'd, the guests of this night's feast.<br>  They come, they come, for whom the Gods shall leap<br>  From their cloud thrones, and ask ye whom ye bring<br>  In stern troops crowding to their secret joy." [486]<br>  She shook her low dropt lip, and thus went on:<br>  "The bow is broken, and the shafts are snapt;<br>  The lance is shiver'd, and the buckler rent;<br>  The helm is cloven, and the plumes are shed; [490]<br>  The horse hath founder'd, and the rider fallen;<br>  The Crown'd are crownless, kingdomless the Kings;<br>  The Conquerors conquer'd, and the Slayers slain;<br>  One falls not, but he shall not stand, the axe<br>  Shall glean th' imperfect harvest of the sword; [495]<br>  The scaffold drinks the lees of battle's cup;<br>  And one is woundless amid myriad wounds,<br>  And one is wounded where there is but one.<br>  Ho, for the broad-horn'd Elk that leads the herd!<br>  Ho, for the Pine that tops the shattering wood!<br>  Ho, for the Bark that Admirals all the fleet!<br>  The herd is scatter'd, and the Elk unscath'd,<br>  The wood is levell'd, upright is the Pine,<br>  The fleet is wreck'd, the Admiral on the waves.<br>  That Elk is in himself a sacrifice, [505]<br>  That Pine shall have a storm its own, that Bark<br>  Shall perish in a solitary wreck,<br>  A sacrifice of shame!  a storm of dread!<br>  A bitter ignominious solitude!" --<br>  <br>  She had not ended, when a single steed [510]<br>  Burst furious from the British line, with flight<br>  That had a tread of air, and not of earth.<br>  Fierce and direct he whirl'd to the hot charge<br>  His youthful Rider.  Upright sate the Boy<br>  Arthur, at first with half reverted look, [515]<br>  As to his mother to impart his joy,<br>  His transport.  Early, oh fame-destin'd Child,<br>  Put'st thou thy sickle in the field of fame.<br>  Over his head a dome of fiery darts<br>  And cross-bow bolts vault o'er th' encumber'd air. [520]<br>  Yet forward swept the child his rapid charge,<br>  And all at once to rescue all the Chiefs<br>  Rush'd onward; Uther's dragon seem'd to sear<br>  The winds with its hot waving, Emrys struck<br>  His coursers reeking flanks, his weapon huge [525]<br>  Rear'd Vortimer, and Malwyn's wheels 'gan whirl.<br>  And on the other side Argantyr tall,<br>  Hengist and Horsa, all the titled brave,<br>  Burst from their tardy lines, that vast behind<br>  Came rolling in tumultuous order on; [530]<br>  As when at spring time under the cold pole<br>  Two islands high of ice warp heavy and huge<br>  Upon the contrary currents, first th' assault<br>  The promontories break, till meet the whole<br>  With one long crash, that wakes the silence, there [535]<br>  Seated since time was born, far off and wide<br>  Rock'd by the conflict fierce old ocean boils.<br>  <br>  Still th' upright Child seem'd only to rejoice<br>  In the curvettings of his wanton steed,<br>  And in the mingled dazzling of bright arms. [540]<br>  But over him a shield is spread, before<br>  A sword is wav'd, on every side the shield<br>  Dashes rude death aside, whirls every where<br>  The rapid and unwearied sword; the rein<br>  Of the fleet steed hath Samor grasp'd, and guides [545]<br>  Amid the turmoil.  As when the eagle sire<br>  Up in the sunshine leads his daring young,<br>  Sometimes the dusk shade of his wing spreads o'er,<br>  And soft and broken in through the thick plumes<br>  Gleams the unblinding splendour.  So secure [550]<br>  Wag'd that fair Child his early war.  But wild<br>  The wavering fray rock'd to and fro, and burnt<br>  Like one huge furnace the quick-flashing plain.<br>  Ever as 'twere the same the Apostle saw<br>  In the Apocalypse, Death's own pale steed, [555]<br>  Over the broad fight shook the White Horse, spread<br>  Where'er its gleaming lighten'd the dun gloom,<br>  Steamy and vast the curdling slaughter pools.<br>  And such confusion burst around of lines<br>  Mingling and interchanging, Valour found [560]<br>  No space for proud selection, forc'd to strike<br>  What cumber'd and obstructed its free path,<br>  To hew out through a mass of vulgar life<br>  A passage to some princely foe; twice met<br>  Horsa and Vortimer, Argantyr twice [565]<br>  Smote at Pendragon, but the whirlpool fierce<br>  Asunder swept them, and the deep of war<br>  Swallow'd them; many a broad and shapeless chasm<br>  Was rent in either battle, but new fronts<br>  Rush'd in, and made the shiver'd surface whole. [570]<br>  The sun was shut out by a sphere of dust<br>  That wrapt the tumult, 'twas no sight for Heaven<br>  That rending and defacing its prime work,<br>  That waste of man, its masterpiece.  But far<br>  Th' Avenger had borne off the Child, his steed [575]<br>  First drew his breath before Igerna's tent.<br>  <br>  With her soft face upon the dust she lay,<br>  Struggling to hush her own lament, in hope<br>  From the fierce din of war might haply come<br>  Some sound of cheer and comfort; but when full [580]<br>  It rush'd upon her hearing, loud she shriek'd<br>  To drown the very noise she strove to hear.<br>  But when her Child's voice sounded, she look'd up<br>  With a cold glance which said, "That sound I've heard<br>  Every sad moment since he went, my soul [585]<br>  Is sick of self-deception, will not trust<br>  Again, to be again beguil'd."  She saw,<br>  And forc'd a sportive look to her sad face<br>  To lure him to her snowy arms.  While he<br>  Back to the battle, as a scene of joy, [590]<br>  Look'd waywardly, she clasp'd him to her breast<br>  With a fond anger, and both smil'd and wept.<br>  A moment Samor gaz'd on her, and -- "All<br>  All have their hopes, and all those hopes fulfill'd,<br>  But I, this side the grave no hope for me [595]<br>  And no fulfillment." -- Fast as sight could track<br>  The battle felt him in its thousand folds.<br>  <br>  But the undistinguish'd and chance-mingled fight<br>  Brook'd not young Malwyn; he his virgin shield<br>  Disdain'd mean blood should stain: where Hengist fought<br>  He swept, the Saxon saw the eagle plume. [601]<br>  And turn'd aloof, and on some other head<br>  Discharg'd the blow for him uprear'd.  But he<br>  Next plung'd where Horsa's starlike weapon shone,<br>  Disastrous, shaking ruin, yet even that [605]<br>  Glanc'd aside from the eagle plume.  The Boy<br>  Utter'd a wrathful disappointed cry,<br>  And 'gainst Argantyr drove his car.  He paus'd,<br>  And cried aloud, "The eagle plume," and plung'd<br>  Elsewhere for victims.  That Pendragon heard, [610]<br>  Even as he toil'd the third time to make way<br>  Amid' the circling slain to the Anglian crest,<br>  And taunting thus, -- "Methinks the eagle plume<br>  Hath some few feathers of the dove, so soft<br>  Spreads its peace-breathing influence."  But the Youth,<br>  "Ha, Father!  thus, thus guil'st thou to a faint [616]<br>  And infamous security thy son?<br>  Thus enviest thou a noble foe?  thus guard'st<br>  With a base privilege from peril?  Off<br>  Coward distinction!  off, faint hearted sign!"<br>  And helm and plume away he rent, his hair [620]<br>  Curl'd down his shoulders, radiant on his brow<br>  The beauty of his anger shone, the pride<br>  Of winning thus a right to glorious death.<br>  Then set he forth on his bold quest again [625]<br>  Impatient.  Him Prince Vortimer beheld<br>  Sweeping between himself and Horsa, met<br>  Their sea-shore fight by Thanet to renew;<br>  But something of his sister in his face,<br>  Something of Lilian harden'd and grown fierce, [630]<br>  As that ungodly creed were true, and she<br>  Familiar to rude deeds of blood, had come<br>  One of Valhalla's airy sisters hence<br>  To summon him she lov'd. That gleam of her,<br>  That though ungentle and unfeminine touch, [635]<br>  Exquisite, in mid air his rugged mace<br>  Suspended; but fierce Horsa on the Boy,<br>  Just on his neck, let fall the fatal spikes,<br>  And him the affrighted steeds bore off.  But then<br>  Began a combat over which Death seem'd [640]<br>  To hover, as of one assur'd, in hope<br>  Of both for victims at his godless shrine.<br>  <br>  Then wounded and bareheaded Malwyn urged<br>  On Hengist his remaster'd steeds, the scythe<br>  Ras'd his majestic war horse.  But aside [645]<br>  He sprung, and flank'd the chariot; long the strife,<br>  Long, though unequal, like a serpent's tongue<br>  Vibrated Malwyn's battle axe, twice bow'd<br>  The Monarch to his saddle bow. -- 'Twas fame<br>  More splendid, thus with Hengist to have fought [650]<br>  Than to have conquer'd hosts of meaner men.<br>  Heavy at length and fatal glided in<br>  The wily Chief's eluding falchion stroke;<br>  Fast flew the steeds, the master lay behind,<br>  Dragging with his face downward, still the reins [655]<br>  Cling in his cold and failing fingers, trail<br>  His neck and spread locks in the humid dust,<br>  His sharp arms character the yielding sand.<br>  On fly they, him at length deserting mute<br>  And gasping on the bank, their hot hoofs plunge [660]<br>  Into the limpid Dune, and to the wood<br>  Rove on.  It chanc'd erewhile that thither came<br>  To freshen with the water his spent steeds,<br>  And lave the clogging carnage from his wheels,<br>  Caswallon, he his huge and weary length [665]<br>  Cast for brief rest upon the bank; a groan<br>  Came from a helmless head that in the grass<br>  Lay undistinguish'd.  "'Tis a Briton," cried<br>  Caswallon, "cast the carrion off to feed<br>  The dogs and kites, that thus irreverent breaks [670]<br>  Upon its monarch's rest."  Even as a flower,<br>  Poppy or hyacinth, on its broken stem,<br>  Languidly raises its encumber'd head,<br>  And turns it to the gentle evening sun,<br>  So feebly rose, so turn'd that Boy his face [675]<br>  Unto the well-known voice; twice rais'd his head,<br>  Twice it feel back in powerless heaviness;<br>  Even at that moment from the dark wood came,<br>  Lured by their partners in the stall and field,<br>  His chariot coursers, heavily behind [680]<br>  Dragging the vacant car, loose hung the reins,<br>  And mournfulness and dull disorder slack'd<br>  The spirit of their tread.  Caswallon knew,<br>  And he leap'd up; the Boy his bloodless lips<br>  With a long effort opened. -- "Was it well, [685]<br>  Father, at this my first, my earliest fight,<br>  To mock me with a baffled hope of fame?<br>  Well was it to defraud me of my right<br>  To noble death?" -- and speaking thus he died<br>  <br>  Above him his convuls'd unconscious hands [690]<br>  Horribly with his rough black beard at play,<br>  Wrenching and twisting off the rooted locks,<br>  Yet senseless of the pain, the Father lean'd.<br>  Then leap'd he up, with cool and jealous care<br>  Within his chariot plac'd the lifeless corpse, [695]<br>  And with his lash fierce rent the half-unyok'd<br>  Half-harness'd steeds; disorderly and swift<br>  As with their master's ire instinct they flew,<br>  Making a wide road through the hurtling fray.<br>  Briton or Saxon, friend or foe alike, [700]<br>  Kinsman or stranger, one wide enmity<br>  'Gainst general humankind, one infinite<br>  And undistinguishing lust of carnage fill'd<br>  The Master and the Horses; so wild groans<br>  Follow'd where'er he moved, 'twas all to him, [795]<br>  So slaughter dripp'd and reek'd from the chok'd scythes.<br>  The low lay mow'd like the spring grass, down swept<br>  On th' eminent, like lightning on the oaks,<br>  His battle axe, each time it fell, each time<br>  A life was gone, each time a hideous laugh [710]<br>  Shone on the Slayer's cheek and writhing lip;<br>  As in the Oriental wars where meet<br>  Sultan and Omrah, under his broad tower<br>  Moves stately the huge Elephant, a shaft<br>  Haply casts down his friendly rider, wont [715]<br>  To lead him to the tank, whose children shar'd<br>  With him their feast of fruits: awhile he droops<br>  Affectionate his loose and moaning trunk:<br>  Then in his grief and vengeance bursts, and bears<br>  In his feet's trampling rout and disarray [720]<br>  To either army, ranks give way, and troops<br>  Scatter, while swaying on his heaving back<br>  His tottering tower, he shakes the sandy plain.<br>  <br>  Meanwhile had risen a conflict high and fierce<br>  For Britain's royal banner; Hengist here, [725]<br>  Argantyr, the Vikinger, Hermingard,<br>  And other Chiefs.  But there th' Armoric King,<br>  Emrys, and Uther, with the Avenger stood,<br>  An iron wall against their inroad; turn'd<br>  Samor 'gainst him at distance heard and seen, [730]<br>  The car-borne Mountaineer, then Uther met<br>  Argantyr, Hengist and King Emrys fought,<br>  The rest o'erbore King Hoel; one had slain<br>  The standard bearer, and all arms at once<br>  Seiz'd as it fell, all foreign and all foes. [735]<br>  When lo, that sable Warrior, that retir'd<br>  And careless had look'd on, upon his steed<br>  And in the battle, like a thundercloud<br>  He came, and like a thundercloud he burst,<br>  Black, cold, and sullen, conquering without pride [740]<br>  And slaying without triumph; three that grasp'd<br>  The standard came at once to earth, while he<br>  Over his head with kingly motion sway'd<br>  The bright redeemed ensign, and as fell<br>  The shaken sunlight radiant o'er his brow, [745]<br>  Pride came about him, and with voice like joy<br>  He cried aloud, "Arles!  Arles!" -- and shook his sword,<br>  "Thou'st won me once a royal crown, and now<br>  Shalt win a royal sepulchre." -- The sword<br>  Perform'd its fatal duty, down they fell [750]<br>  Before him, Jute and Saxon, nameless men<br>  And Chieftains; what though wounds he scorn'd to ward,<br>  Nor seem'd to feel, show'r'd on him, and his blood<br>  Ooz'd manifest, still he slew, still cried, "Arles!  Arles!"<br>  Still in the splendour the wav'd standard spread [755]<br>  Stood glorying the arm'd darkness of his form;<br>  Stood from his wounded steed dismounted, stood<br>  Amid an area of dead men, himself<br>  About to die, none daring an assault,<br>  He powerless of assailing.  But the crown [760]<br>  That on the flag-staff gleam'd, he wrench'd away,<br>  And on his crest with calm solicitude<br>  Plac'd it, then planting 'mid the high-heap'd slain<br>  The standard, to o'ercanopy his sleep,<br>  As one upon his nightly couch of down [765]<br>  Composes quietly his weary head,<br>  So royally he laid him down to die. --<br>  <br>  But now was every fight broke off, a pause<br>  Seiz'd all the battle, one vast silence quench'd<br>  All tumult; slain and slayer, life and death [770]<br>  Possess'd one swoon of torpor, droop'd and fail'd<br>  All passions, pride, wrath, vengeance, hate, dismay,<br>  All was one wide astonishment: alone<br>  Two undistracted on each other gazed,<br>  Where helpless in their death-blood they lay steep'd, [775]<br>  The ebbing of each other's life, the stiff<br>  Damp growing on of death; till in a groan<br>  Horsa exhausted his fierce soul: then came<br>  A momentary tinge, soft and subdued<br>  As of affections busy at his heart, [780]<br>  On Vortimer's expiring brow, his lip<br>  Wore something of the curl men's use, when names<br>  Belov'd are floating o'er the thought, the flowers<br>  On that lone grave made fragrant his sick sense,<br>  And Eamont murmured on his closing ear. [785]<br>  <br>  But he, whose coming cast this silence on<br>  Before it, as the night its widening shade,<br>  Curtaining nature in its soundless pall,<br>  An atmosphere of dying breath, where'er<br>  He moved, his drear envelopment, his path [790]<br>  An element of blood: so fleet, so fast<br>  The power to fly seem'd wither'd, ere he came,<br>  Men laid them down and said their prayers and look'd<br>  For the quick plunging hoofs and rushing scythes:<br>  As when the palsied Universe aghast [795]<br>  Lay, all its tenants, even Man, restless Man,<br>  In all his busy workings mute and still,<br>  When drove, so poets sing, the Sun-born youth<br>  Devious through heaven's affrighted signs, his Sire's<br>  Ill-granted chariot, him the Thunderer hurl'd [800]<br>  From th' empyrean headlong to the gulph<br>  Of the half-parch'd Eridanus, where weep<br>  Even now the Sister Trees their amber tears<br>  O'er Phaeton untimely dead.  And now<br>  Had the Avenger reach'd the path of death, [805]<br>  And stood in arms before the steeds, they came<br>  Rearing their ireful hoofs to dash him down;<br>  But with both hands he seiz'd their foaming curbs,<br>  Holding them in their spring with outstrech'd arm<br>  Aloft, and made their lifted crests a shield [810]<br>  Against their driver.  He with baffled lash<br>  Goaded their quivering flanks, but that strong arm<br>  Held them above avoiding, their fore-hoofs<br>  Beat th' unhurt air, and overspread his breast,<br>  Like a thick snow-shower, the fast falling foam. [815]<br>  Then leap'd Caswallon down, back Samor hurl'd<br>  Coursers and chariot, and, "Now," cried aloud,<br>  "Now, King of Britain, in the name of God<br>  I tender thee a throne, two yards of earth<br>  To rot on, and a diadem, a wreath [820]<br>  Of death-drops for thy haught aspiring brow.<br>  <br>  "There, there, look there," Caswallon cried, his hand<br>  Stretch'd tow'rd his son, and in a frantic laugh<br>  Broke out, and echoed. -- "Diadems and thrones!"<br>  With rigid finger pointing at the dead. [825]<br>  <br>  A moment, and the fury burst again;<br>  Down came the ponderous battle axe, from edge<br>  To edge it rived the temper'd brass, as swift<br>  As shot-stars the thin ether; but the glaive<br>  Of Samor right into his bosom smote. [830]<br>  Like some old turret, under whose broad shade<br>  At summer noon the shepherd oft his flock<br>  Hath driven, and in the friendly cool rejoic'd,<br>  Suddenly, violently, from its base<br>  Push'd by the winter floods, he fell; his look [835]<br>  Yet had its savage blasphemy: he felt<br>  More than the blow, the deadly blow, the cries<br>  Of joy and triumph from each army sent,<br>  Taunting and loud; to him to die was nought,<br>  He could not brook the shame of being slain. [840]<br>  But other thoughts arose; hardly he crept<br>  To where dead Malwyn from the car hung down,<br>  Felt on his face the cold depending hand,<br>  And with a smile half joy, half anguish died.<br>  <br>  Th' Avenger knelt, his heart too full for prayer, [845]<br>  Knelt, and held up his conquering sword to heaven,<br>  Yet spake not.  But the battle, as set free,<br>  Its rugged game renew'd, nor equal now<br>  Nor now unbroken, Flight and shameful Rout<br>  Here scattered, Victory there and Pride array'd, [850]<br>  And mass'd in comely files and full square troops<br>  Bore onward.  Mountaineer and German break<br>  Around the hill foot, and like ebbing waves<br>  Disperse away.  Argantyr, Hengist move<br>  In the recoiling flood reluctant.  Them [855]<br>  Nought more resembled, than two mountain bulls<br>  Driven by the horse and dog and hunter spear,<br>  Still turning with huge brow and tearing up<br>  The deep earth with their wrathful stooping horns.<br>  <br>  But as the hill was opened, from the top [860]<br>  Even to the base arose a shriek and scream,<br>  As when some populous Capital besieg'd,<br>  Sees yawning her wide-breached wall, and all<br>  Her shatter'd bulwarks on the earth, so wild,<br>  So dissonant the female rout appear'd [865]<br>  Hanging with fierce disturbance the hill side.<br>  Some with rent hair ran to and fro, some stood<br>  With silent mocking lip, some softly prest<br>  Their infants to their heart, some held them forth<br>  As to invite the foe, and for them sued [870]<br>  The mercy of immediate slaughter.  Some<br>  Spake fiercely of past deeds of fame, some sang<br>  In taunting tone old songs of victory.  Wives<br>  With eye imploring and quick heaving breast<br>  Look'd sad allusions to endearments past; [875]<br>  Mothers, all bashfulness cast down, rent down<br>  Their garments, to their sons displaying bare<br>  The fountains of their infant nourishment,<br>  Now ready to be plough'd with murtherous swords.<br>  Some knelt before their cold deaf Gods, some scoff'd<br>  With imprecation blasphemous and shrill [881]<br>  Their stony and unwakening thunders.  Noise<br>  Not fiercer on Cithæron side, th' affright<br>  Not drearier, when the Theban Bacchic rout,<br>  Their dashing cymbals white with moonshine, loose [885]<br>  Their tresses bursting from their ivy crowns,<br>  And purple with enwoven vine-leaves, led<br>  Their orgies dangerous.  In the midst the Queen<br>  Agave shook the misdeem'd Lion's head<br>  Aloft, and laugh'd and danc'd and sung, nor knew [890]<br>  That lion suckled at her own white breast.<br>  <br>  But Elfelin that Prophetess her seat<br>  Chang'd not, nor the near horror could recall<br>  Her eye from its strange commerce with th' unseen;<br>  There had she been, there has she been in smiles [895]<br>  All the long battle; just before the spear<br>  Or falchion drank a warrior's life-blood, she<br>  Audible, as an high-tribunal'd judge,<br>  Spake out his name, and aye her speech was doom.<br>  <br>  Nor long the o'erbearing flight enwrapt thy strength,<br>  Argantyr, thou amid the shattering wreck [901]<br>  Didst rise, as in some ruinous city old,<br>  Babylon or Palmyra, magic built,<br>  A single pillar yet with upright shaft<br>  Stands, 'mid the wide prostration mossy and flat, [905]<br>  Shewing more eminent.  Past the Saxon by,<br>  And look'd and wonder'd, even that he delay'd;<br>  Cried his own Anglians. -- "King, away, away!"<br>  First came King Hoel on, whose falchion clove<br>  His buckler, with a wrest he burst in twain [910]<br>  The shivering steel; came Emrys next, aside<br>  His misaim'd blow he shook; last Uther, him<br>  His war horse, by Argantyr's beam-like spear<br>  Then first appall'd, bore in vain anger past.<br>  <br>  From his late victory in proud breathlessness [915]<br>  Slow came the Avenger, but Argantyr rais'd<br>  A cry of furious joy, "Long sought, late found,<br>  I charge thee, by our last impeded fight,<br>  I charge thee, give me back mine own, my sword<br>  Is weary of its bathes of vulgar blood,<br>  And longs in nobler streams to plunge; with thine<br>  I'll gild and hang it on my Father's grave,<br>  And his helm'd ghost in Woden's hall shall vaunt<br>  The glories of his son."  "Generous and brave,<br>  When last we met, I shrunk to see my sword [925]<br>  Bright with God's sunlight, now with dauntless hand<br>  I lift it, and cry On, in the name of God."<br>  <br>  They met, they strove, as with a cloud enwrapt<br>  In their own majesty; their motions gave<br>  Terror even to their shadows; round them spread [930]<br>  Attention like a sleep.  Flight paus'd, Pursuit<br>  Caught up its loose rein, Death his furious work<br>  Ceas'd, and a dreary respite gave to souls<br>  Half parted; on their elbows rear'd them up<br>  The dying, with faint effort holding ope [935]<br>  Their dropping eyelids, homage of delight<br>  War from its victims thus exacting.  Mind<br>  And body engross'd the conflict.  Men were seen<br>  At distance, for in their peculiar sphere,<br>  Within the wind and rush of their quick arms [940]<br>  None ventur'd, following with unconscious limbs<br>  Their blows, and shrinking as themselves were struck.<br>  Like scatter'd shiverings of a scath'd oak lay<br>  Fragments of armour round them, the hard brass<br>  Gave way, and broke the fiery temper'd steel, [945]<br>  The stronger metal of the human soul,<br>  Valour, endur'd, and power thrice purified<br>  In Danger's furnace fail'd not.  Victory, tired<br>  Of wavering, to those passive instruments,<br>  Look'd to decide her long suspense.  Behold [950]<br>  Argantyr's falchion, magic wrought, his sires<br>  So fabled, by the Asgard dwarfs, nor hewn<br>  From earthly mines, nor dipp'd in earthly fires,<br>  Broke short.  Th' ancestral steel the Anglians saw,<br>  Sign of their Kings, and worship of their race, [955]<br>  Give way, and wail'd and shriek'd aloud.  The King<br>  Collected all his glory as a pall<br>  To perish in, and scorn'd his sworded foe<br>  To mock with vain defence of unarm'd hand.<br>  The exultation and fierce throb of hope [960]<br>  Yet had not pass'd away, but look'd to death<br>  As it had look'd to conquest, death so well,<br>  So bravely earn'd to warrior fair as life:<br>  Stern welcoming, bold invitation lured<br>  To its last work the Conqueror's sword.  Him flush'd<br>  The pride of Conquest, vengeance long delay'd, [966]<br>  Th' exalted shame of victory won so slow,<br>  So toilsomely; all fiery passions, all<br>  Tumultuous sense-intoxicating powers<br>  Conspir'd with their wild anarchy beset [970]<br>  His despot soul.  But he -- "Ah, faithless sword,<br>  To me as to thy master faithless, him<br>  Naked at his extreme to leave, and me<br>  To guile of this occasion fair to win<br>  Honour or death from great Argantyr's arm." [975]<br>  <br>  "Christian, thy God is mightiest, scorn not thou<br>  His bounty, nor with dalliance mock thy hour,<br>  Strike and consummate!" -- "Anglian yes, my God,<br>  Th' Almighty, is the mightiest now and ever,<br>  Because I scorn him not, I will not strike." -- [980]<br>  So saying, he his sword cast down.  "Thus, thus<br>  Warr'st thou?" the Anglian cried, "then thou hast won.<br>  I, I Argantyr yield me, other hand<br>  Had tempted me in vain with that base boon<br>  Which peasants prize and women weep for, life: [985]<br>  To lord o'er dead Argantyr fate might grant,<br>  He only grants to vanquish him alive,<br>  Only to thee, well nam'd Avenger!"  Then<br>  The Captive and the Conqueror th' armies saw<br>  Gazing upon each other with the brow [990]<br>  Of high arch'd admiration; o'er the field<br>  From that example flow'd a noble scorn<br>  Of slaughtering the defenceless, mercy slak'd<br>  The ardour of the fight.  As the speck'd birch<br>  After a shower, with th' odour of its bark [995]<br>  Freshens the circuit of the rain-bright grove;<br>  Or as the tender argent of Love's star<br>  Smiles to a lucid quiet the wild sky:<br>  So those illustrious rivals with the light<br>  Of their high language and heroic act [1000]<br>  Cast a nobility o'er all the war.<br>  That capture took a host, none scorn'd to yield,<br>  So loftily Argantyr wore the garb<br>  Of stern surrender, none inclin'd to slay,<br>  When Samor held the signal up to spare. [1005]<br>  <br>  But where the Lord of that dire falchion nam'd<br>  The Widower of Women?  He, the Chief<br>  Whose arms were squadrons, whose assault the shock<br>  Of hosts advancing?  Hath the cream-blanch'd steed,<br>  Whom the outstripped winds pant after, borne away<br>  His master, yet with hope uncheck'd, and craft [1011]<br>  Unbaffled, th' equal conflict to renew?<br>  Fast flew the horse, and fierce the rider spurr'd,<br>  That horse that all the day remorseless went<br>  O'er dead and dying, all that Hengist slew [1015]<br>  All he cast down before him.  Lo, he checks<br>  Suddenly, startingly, with ears erect,<br>  Thick tremor oozing out from every pore,<br>  His broad chest palpitating, the thick foam<br>  Lazily gathering on his dropping lip: [1020]<br>  The pawing of his uplift forefoot chill'd<br>  To a loose hanging quiver.  Nor his Lord<br>  Less horror seiz'd; slack trembled in his left<br>  The bridle, with his right hand dropt his sword,<br>  Dripp'd slowly from its point the flaking blood [1025]<br>  Of hundreds, this day fall'n beneath its edge.<br>  <br>  For lo, descended the hill side, stood up<br>  Right in his path the Prophetess, and held<br>  With a severe compassion both her arms<br>  Over her head, and thus -- "It cannot be, [1030]<br>  I've cried unto the eagle, air hath none;<br>  I've sued unto the fleet and bounding deer,<br>  I've sought unto the sly and mining snake;<br>  There's none above the earth, beneath the earth,<br>  No flight, no way, no narrow obscure way. [1035]<br>  I've call'd unto the lightning, as it leaped<br>  Along heaven's verge, it cannot guide thee forth;<br>  I've beckon'd to the dun and pitchy gloom,<br>  It cannot shroud thee; to the caves of earth<br>  I've wail'd and shriek'd, they cannot chamber thee."<br>  <br>  He spoke not, mov'd not, strove not: man and steed,<br>  Like some Equestrian marble in the courts [1042]<br>  Of Emperors; that fierce eye whose wisdom keen<br>  Pierc'd the dark depths of counsel, hawk-like-roved,<br>  Seizing the unutter'd thoughts from out men's souls,<br>  Wrought order in the battle's turbulent fray [1046]<br>  By its command, on the aged Woman's face<br>  Fix'd like a moonstruck idiot.  She upright<br>  With strength beyond her bow'd and shrivell'd limbs<br>  Still stood, and murmur'd low, "Why com'st thou not,<br>  Thou of the Vale?  thou fated, come!  come!  come!"<br>  <br>  The foes o'ertook, he look'd not round, their tramp<br>  Was round him, still he mov'd not; violent hands<br>  Seiz'd on him, still the enchanted falchion hung<br>  Innocent as a feather by his side.  [1055]<br>  They tore him from his steed, still clung his eyes<br>  On her disasterous face; she fiercely shriek'd<br>  Half pride at her accomplish'd prophecy,<br>  Half sorrow at Erle Hengist's fall, then down<br>  Upon the stone that bore her, she fell dead. [1060]<br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  <h3 align="CENTER"><b> Book XII.</b> </h3>   Oh, Freedom, of our social Universe<br>  The Sun, that feedest from thy urn of light<br>  The starry commonwealth, from those mean lamps<br>  Modestly glimmering in their sphere retir'd,<br>  Even to the plenar and patrician orbs, [5]<br>  That in their rich nobility of light,<br>  Or golden royalty endiadem'd,<br>  Their mystic circle undisturb'd round thee<br>  Move musical; but thou thy central state<br>  Preserving, equably the fair-rank'd whole [10]<br>  In dutiful magnificence maintain'st,<br>  And stately splendour of obedience.  Earth<br>  Wonders, th' approval of th' Almighty beams<br>  Manifest in the glory of the work.<br>  Though sometimes drown'd within the red eclipse [15]<br>  Of tyranny, or brief while by the base<br>  And marshy exhalations of low vice<br>  And popular license madden'd thou hast flash'd<br>  Disastrous and intolerable fire;<br>  Yet ever mounting hast thou still march'd on [20]<br>  To thy meridian throne; my waxen wing<br>  Oh, quenchless luminary!  may not soar<br>  To that thy dazzling and o'erpowering noon;<br>  Rather the broken glimpses of thy dawn<br>  Visiteth, when thy orient overcast [25]<br>  A promise and faint foretaste of its light<br>  Beam'd forth, then plung'd its cloud-slak'd front in gloom.<br>  <br>  Even with such promise dost thou now adorn<br>  Thy chosen city by the Thames, where holds<br>  Victorious Emrys his high Judgment court. [30]<br>  Thither the long ovation hath he led,<br>  Amid the solemn music of rent chains,<br>  The rapture of deliverance, where he past<br>  Earth brightening, and the face of man but now<br>  Brow-sear'd with the deep brand of servitude, [35]<br>  To its old upright privilege restor'd<br>  Of gazing on its kindred heaven.  The towns<br>  Gladden'd amid their ruins, churches shook<br>  With throngs of thankful votaries, till 'twas fear [NOTE 22]<br>  Transport might finish Desolation's work, [40]<br>  And bliss precipitate the half moulder'd walls.<br>  'Tis fam'd, men died for joy, untimely births<br>  Were frequent, as the eager mothers prest<br>  To show their infants to the brightening world.<br>  They that but now beheld the bier-borne dead [45]<br>  With miserable envy, past them by<br>  Contemptuously pitying, as too soon<br>  Departed from this highly gifted earth.<br>  So they the Trinobantine City reach'd:<br>  Without the walls, close by the marge of Thames, [50]<br>  The synod of the Conquerors met; a place<br>  Solemn and to the soul discoursing high.<br>  Here broad the bridgeless Thames, even like themselves<br>  Thus at their flush and high tide of renown,<br>  Swell'd his exulting waters.  There all waste [55]<br>  The royal cemetery of Britain lay,<br>  The monuments, like their cold tenantry,<br>  Mouldering, above all ruin as beneath,<br>  A wide profound, drear sameness of decay.<br>  Upon the Church of Christ had heavily fallen [60]<br>  The Pagan desolation, hung the doors<br>  Loose on their broken and disused hinge,<br>  And grass amid the checquer'd pavement squares<br>  Was springing, and along the vacant choir<br>  The shrill wind was God's only worshipper. [65]<br>  <br>  Even where they met, through the long years have sate<br>  In Parliament our nation's high and wise.<br>  There have deep thoughts been ponder'd, strong designs<br>  On which the fate of the round world hath hung.<br>  Thence have the emanating rays of truth, [70]<br>  Freedom, and constancy, and holiness<br>  Flow'd in their broad beneficence, no bound<br>  Owning but that which limits this brief earth,<br>  Brightening this misty state of man; the winds<br>  That thence bear mandates to th' inconstant thrones [75]<br>  Of Europe, to the realms of th' orient Sun,<br>  Or to the new and ocean-sever'd earth,<br>  Or to the Southern cocoa-feather'd isles,<br>  Are welcome, as pure gales of health and joy.<br>  Still that deep dwelling underneath the earth [80]<br>  Its high and ancient privilege maintains,<br>  Dark palace of our island's parted Kings,<br>  Earth-ciel'd pavilion of our brave and wise,<br>  Whose glory ere it swept them off, hath cast<br>  A radiance on the scythe of Death.  Disus'd [85]<br>  For two long heathen ages, it became<br>  The pavement of our sumptuous minster fair,<br>  That ever and anon yet gathers in<br>  King, Conqu'ror, Poet, Orator, or Sage<br>  To her stone chambers, there to sleep the sleep [90]<br>  That wakens only at the Archangel's trump.<br>  <br>  First in the synod rose King Emrys; he<br>  The royal sword of justice from his side<br>  Ungirding, plac'd it in the Avenger's hand,<br>  And led him to the judgment-seat.  He shrunk, [95]<br>  And offer'd back the solemn steel. -- "Oh!  King,<br>  Judge and Avenger!  who shall reconcile<br>  The discord of those titles, private wrongs<br>  Will load my partial arm, and drag to earth<br>  The unsteady balance.  Only God can join [100]<br>  And blend in one the Injur'd and the Judge."<br>  But as a wave lifts up and bears along<br>  A stately bark, so the acclamation swell<br>  Floated into the high Tribunal throne<br>  Reluctant Samor: on his right the King [105]<br>  Sate sceptred, royal Uther on the left.<br>  While all around the assembled Nation bask'd<br>  In his effulgent presence.  'Twas a boast<br>  In after ages this day to have seen<br>  Him whom all throng'd to see; memory of him, [110]<br>  Every brief notice of his mien and height<br>  Became an heir-loom; mothers at the font<br>  Gave to their babes his name, and e'er that child<br>  Was held the staff and honour of the race.<br>  <br>  So met the Nation in their judgment Hall, [115]<br>  Its pavement was the sacred mother earth,<br>  Its roof the crystal and immortal heavens.<br>  <br>  Then forth the captives came, Argantyr first,<br>  Even with his wonted loftiness of tread:<br>  Nature's rich heraldry upon his brow [120]<br>  Emblazing him of those whose scorn the world<br>  Bears unasham'd, by whom to be despis'd<br>  Is no abasement.  Men's eyes rang'd from him<br>  To Samor, back to him -- in wonder now<br>  Of conquest o'er such mighty foe, now lost [125]<br>  The wonder in their kindred Conqueror's pride.<br>  Then said the Anglian -- "Wherefore lead ye here?"<br>  The sternness of his questioning appall'd<br>  All save the Judge. -- "What Briton," he replied,<br>  "Witnesseth aught against the Anglian Chief?" -- [130]<br>  Thereat was proclamation, East and West<br>  And North and South: the silent winds came back<br>  With wings unloaded: so that noble mien<br>  Wrought conquest o'er man's darkest passions, hate,<br>  And doubt, and terror, so the Captive cast [135]<br>  His yoke on every soul, and harness'd it<br>  Unto his valiant spirit's chariot wheels.<br>  <br>  Then spake the stately and tribunal'd Judge --<br>  "Anglian Argantyr!  Britain is not wont<br>  T' inflict upon a fair and open foe [140]<br>  Aught penal but defeat; her warfare bows<br>  Beneath her feet but tramples not; her throne<br>  Hath borne the stormy brunt of thy assault,<br>  And dash'd it off, and thus she saith, "Return,<br>  Return unto thy German woods, nor more, [145]<br>  Once baffled, vex our coasts with fruitless war.<br>  And thy return shall be to years remote<br>  Our bond and charter of security;<br>  A shudder and cold trembling at our name<br>  Shall pass with thee, the land that hath spurn'd back [150]<br>  Argantyr's march of victory, shall be known<br>  T' eternal freedom consecrate.  Your ships<br>  Shall plough our seas, but turn their timorous prows<br>  Aloof, while on the deck the Sea King points<br>  To our white cliffs, and saith -- 'The Anglian thence [155]<br>  Retreated, shun the unconquerable shore." --<br>  So nevermore shall my hot warhorse bathe<br>  In British waters, nor my falchion meet<br>  The bold resistance of a British steel,<br>  So wills the Conqueror, thus the Conquer'd swears." [160]<br>  <br>  Thus spake Argantyr; sudden then and swift<br>  Loftier shot up his brow, prophetic hues<br>  Swam o'er his agitated features, words<br>  Came with a rush and instantaneous flow. --<br>  <br>  "I tell thee, Briton, that thy sons and mine [165]<br>  Shall be two meeting and conflicting tides,<br>  Whose fierce relentless enmity shall lash<br>  This land into a whirlpool deep and wide,<br>  To swallow in its vast insatiate gulph<br>  Her peace and smooth felicity, till flow [170]<br>  Their waters reconcil'd in one broad bed,<br>  Briton and Anglian one in race and name.<br>  'Tis written in the antient solemn Runes,<br>  'Tis spoken by prophetic virgin lips.<br>  Avenger, thou and I our earthly wars [175]<br>  Have ended, but my spirit yet shall hold<br>  Noble, inexorable strife with thine.<br>  It shall heave off its barrow, burst its tomb,<br>  And to my sons discourse of glorious foes<br>  In this rich Island to be met: my shade [180]<br>  Shall cross them in their huntings, it shall walk<br>  The ocean paths and on the winds, and seize<br>  Their prows, and fill their sails, and all its voice<br>  And all its secret influences urge<br>  To the White Isle; their slumbers shall not rest, [185] [NOTE 23]<br>  Their quiet shall be weariness, till lull'd<br>  Upon the pillow of success repose<br>  The high, the long hereditary feud."<br>  <br>  So saying, he the bark that lay prepar'd<br>  With sail unfurl'd, ascended.  She went forth [190]<br>  Momently with quick shadow the blue Thames<br>  Darkening, then leaving on its breast a light<br>  Like silver.  The fix'd eyes of wondering men<br>  Track'd his departure, while with farewell gleam<br>  The bright Sun shone upon his brow, and seem'd [195]<br>  A triumph in the motion of the stream;<br>  So loftily upon its long slow ebb<br>  It bore that honour-laden bark. -- Nor pause,<br>  Lo, in the presence of the Judgment Court<br>  The second Criminal; pride had not pass'd [200]<br>  Nor majesty from his hoar brow; he stood<br>  With all except the terror of despair,<br>  Consciously in fatality's strong bonds<br>  Manacled, of the coming death assur'd,<br>  Yet fronting the black future with a look [205]<br>  Obdurate even to scornfulness.  He seem'd<br>  As he heard nought, as though his occupied ears<br>  Were pervious to no sound, since that dim voice<br>  Of her who speaking died, the silver hair'd,<br>  The Prophetess, that never spake untrue: [210]<br>  As ever with a long unbroken flow<br>  Her song was ranging through his brain, and struck<br>  Its death-knoll on his soul.  Nor change had come<br>  Since that drear hour to eye or cheek; the craft,<br>  The wisdom that was wont to make him lord [215]<br>  Over the shifting pageant of events,<br>  Had given its trust up to o'er-ruling fate,<br>  And that stern Paramount, Necessity,<br>  Had seal'd him for her own.  Amid them all<br>  He tow'r'd, as when the summer thunderbolt [220]<br>  'Mid a rich fleet some storm-accustom'd bark<br>  Hath stricken, round her the glad waters dance,<br>  Her sails are full, her strong prow fronts the waves;<br>  But works within the irrevocable doom,<br>  Wells up her secret hold th' inundant surge, [225]<br>  And th' heavy waters weigh her slowly down.<br>  <br>  For the arraignment made the Judge a sign,<br>  And the first witness was a mighty cry,<br>  As 'twere the voice of the whole Isle, as hills<br>  And plains and waters their abhorrence spake; [230]<br>  Hoarse harmony of imprecation seem'd<br>  To break the ashy sleep of ruin'd towns,<br>  And th' untomb'd slumbers of far battle vales.<br>  As if the crowd about the Judgment Court<br>  Did only with articulate voice repeat [235]<br>  What indistinct came down on every wind.<br>  Then all the near, the distant, sank away,<br>  Only a low and melancholy tone,<br>  Like a far music down a summer stream<br>  Remain'd; upon the lull'd, nor panting air [240]<br>  Fell that smooth snow of sound, till nearer now<br>  It swell'd, as clearer water-falls are heard<br>  When midnight grows more still.  A funeral hymn,<br>  It pour'd the rapture of its sadness out,<br>  Even like a sparkling soporific wine. [245]<br>  But now and then broke from its low long fall,<br>  Something of martial and majestic swell,<br>  That spake its mourning o'er no vulgar dead.<br>  <br>  Lo to the royal burying place, chance borne<br>  Even at his solemn time, or so ordain'd [250]<br>  From their bright-scuteheon'd biers their part to bear<br>  In this arraignment, came King Vortigern,<br>  And th' honour'd ashes of his Son.  But still<br>  And voiceless these cold witnesses past on,<br>  Unto the place of tombs.  Along the Thames [255]<br>  Far floated into silence the spent hymn:<br>  And one accusing sound arose from them,<br>  The heavy falling of their earth to earth.<br>  <br>  One female mourner came behind the King,<br>  Half of her face the veil conceal'd, her eyes [260]<br>  Were visible, and though a deadly haze<br>  Film'd their sunk balls, she sent into the grave,<br>  Following the heavy and descending corpse,<br>  A look of such imploring loveliness,<br>  A glance so sad, so self-condemning, all, [265]<br>  (So softly, tremulously it appeal'd)<br>  Might wonder that the spirit came not back<br>  To animate for the utterance that she wish'd<br>  Those bloodless lips; forgivenss it was plain<br>  She sought, and one so beauteous to forgive, [270]<br>  The dead might almost wake.  And she sate down,<br>  Leaning her cheek upon a broken stone<br>  (Once a King's monument) as listening yet<br>  Th' acceptance of her prayers: nor cloister'd Nun<br>  Hath ever since mourning her broken vows, [275]<br>  And his neglect for whom those vows she broke,<br>  Come to the image of her Virgin Saint<br>  With such a faded cheek and contrite mien,<br>  As her who by those royal ashes sate.<br>  <br>  But lo, new witnesses; a matron train [280]<br>  In flowing robes of grief came forth, the wives<br>  And mothers of those nobles foully slain<br>  At the Peace Banquet, them the memory yet<br>  Seem'd haunting of delicious days broke off.<br>  On Hengist, even a captive, dared not they [285]<br>  Look firmly, as their helpless loneliness<br>  Spake for them, they their solitary breasts<br>  Beat, wrung their destitute cold hands, and pass'd.<br>  <br>  Arose the mitred Germain, glanc'd his hand<br>  From that majestic criminal, where lay [290]<br>  The ruins of God's church, and so sate down.<br>  <br>  But Samor look'd upon the mourner train,<br>  As though he sought a face that was not there,<br>  That could not be, soft Emeric's. -- "I have none,<br>  I only none to witness of my wrongs." -- [295]<br>  So said he, but he shook the softness off,<br>  On the tribunal rose severe, and stood<br>  Erect before the multitude.  "Thou, King,<br>  And ye, assembled People of this Isle,<br>  If that I speak your sentence right, give in [300]<br>  Your sanction of Amen.  Here stands the man,<br>  Who two long years laid waste with fire and sword<br>  Your native cities and your altar shrines:<br>  Here stands the man, who by slow fraud and guile<br>  Discrown'd your stately Monarch, Vortigern: [305]<br>  Here stands the man, hath water'd with your blood<br>  The red and sickening herbage of your land:<br>  Here stands the man, that to your peaceful feast<br>  Brought Murther, that grim seneschal, and drugg'd<br>  With your most noble blood your friendly cups." [310]<br>  <br>  And at each charge came in the deep Amen,<br>  Even like the sounds men hear on stormy nights,<br>  When many thunders are abroad.  Nought moved<br>  Stood Hengist, if emotion o'er him pass'd,<br>   'Twas likest an elate contemptuous joy [315]<br>  And glorying in those lofty worded crimes.<br>  Then, "Saxon Hengist, as thy sword hath made<br>  Our children fatherless, so fatherless<br>  Must be thy children!  And Amen knoll'd back, [NOTE 24]<br>  As a plague visited Metropolis [320]<br>  Mourning the wide and general funeral, tolls<br>  From all her towers and spires the bell of death.<br>  <br>  "Thy children fatherless!  not so -- not so" --<br>  Rose with a shriek that Woman by the grave,<br>  And she sprang forth, as from beneath the earth, [325]<br>  As a partaker of, no mourner near<br>  That kingly coffin.  Veil fell off, and band<br>  Started, through her bright tresses her pale face<br>  Glitter'd, like fine-chaf'd ivory set in gold.<br>  Between the Criminal and Judge her stand [330]<br>  Rowena took; him as she saw and knew<br>  Flush'd a sick rapture o'er her face and neck,<br>  A fading rose-hue, like eve's parting light<br>  On a snow bank; but from her marble brow<br>  She the bright-clustering hair wip'd back, and thus, [335]<br>  "Samor, the last time thou this brow beheld'st<br>  The moonlight was upon it, since that hour<br>  The water hath flow'd o'er it, holy sign<br>  Hath there been left by Christian hand, and I<br>  Thy creed have learnt, and one word breathes it all, [340]<br>  Mercy." -- "But Justice is God's attribute,<br>  Lady, as well as mercy, Man on earth<br>  Must be Vicegerent of both stern and mild,<br>  Lest over-ramping Evil set its foot<br>  Upon the prostrate world.  The doom is said, [345]<br>  The doom must be." -- "Ha!  Man with heart of clay,<br>  To answer with that cold and stedfast mien;<br>  Oh, I'll go back and sue the dead again,<br>  There's more forgiveness in the cold deaf corpse,<br>  Than the warm keen-ear'd living.  From that vault [350]<br>  I felt sweet reconcilement stealing up,<br>  That turn'd my tears to honey dew, here all,<br>  All sullen and relentless on me glares.<br>  I ask not for myself, not for myself,<br>  The ice of death is round my heart, there long [355]<br>  I've felt the slow consuming prey, I feel<br>  The trembling ebb of my departing life.<br>  That hoary head, though granted to my prayers,<br>  Shall never rest upon my failing knee,<br>  The father that ye give me back (I feel [360]<br>  Ye give him, thou that bear'st the Avenger's name,<br>  I know thee by a milder character)<br>  That father cannot long be mine; his hands<br>  May lay me in the grave, his eyes may weep,<br>  For they can weep, although ye think it not; [365]<br>  Those hands ye deem for ever blood-embrued,<br>  I've felt them fondling with my golden hair,<br>  When with gay childish foot I danc'd to meet<br>  His far resounding horn.  That horn shall sound,<br>  But on my deaf and earth-clos'd ears no more, [370]<br>  No more." -- "Rowena, when a Nation speaks,<br>  The irrevocable sentence cannot change."<br>  <br>  Then up her fair round arm she rais'd, and wrapt<br>  Like a rich mantle round her: her old pride<br>  As the poetic Juno in the clouds [375]<br>  Walking in her majestic ire, while slow<br>  Before her th' azure-breasted peacocks draw<br>  Her chariot. -- "Tell me, thou that sit'st elate,<br>  And ye, who call yourselves this British realm,<br>  By what new right ye judge a German King, [380]<br>  Where are your charters, where your scrolls of law<br>  Whose bright and blazon'd titles give ye power<br>  To pass a doom on crowned head?  Down, down,<br>  Ye bold Usurpers of the Judgment seat,<br>  Insolent doomers of a sacred life, [385]<br>  Beyond your sphere to touch, your grasp to seize."<br>  <br>  "Lady, we judge by the adamantine law,<br>  That lives within the eternal soul of man,<br>  That God-enacted charter, "Blood for blood."<br>  <br>  Exhausted she sank down upon her kness, [390]<br>  Her knees that fainted under her. -- "Ye can,<br>  Ye will not shew unto a woman's eyes<br>  That bloody consummation, not to mine.<br>  Oh, thou that speakest in that brazen tone<br>  Implacable, the last time thou and I [395]<br>  Discours'd, thy voice was broken, tender, soft,<br>  Remember'st thou?
