'twas then as it had caught<br>  The trembling of the moonlight, that lay round<br>  With rapturous disquiet bathing us.<br>  Remember'st thou?" -- "Almost the Judgment sword<br>  Fell from the Avenger's failing hand, but firm [401]<br>  He grasp'd it, and with eyes to heaven upturn'd,<br>  "Oh, duty, duty, why art thou so stern?"<br>  Then, "Lady, lo, the headsman with his steel;<br>  To that dark Priest 'tis given to sacrifice [405]<br>  The victim of to day -- depart!  depart!<br>  Colours may flow too deep for woman's sight,<br>  And sounds may burst too drear for woman's ear."<br>  <br>  Stately as lily on a sunshine bank,<br>  Shaken from its curl'd leaves the o'ercharging dew, [410]<br>  Freshens and strengthens its bow'd stem, so white<br>  So brightening to a pale cold pride, a faint<br>  And trembling majesty, Rowena sate.<br>  On Hengist's dropping lip and knitted brow<br>  Was mockery at her fate-opposing prayer, [415]<br>  And that was all.  But she -- "Proud-hearted Men,<br>  Ye vainly deem your privilege, your right,<br>  Prerogative of your high-minded race,<br>  The glory of endurance, and the state<br>  Of strong resolving fortitude.  Here I, [420]<br>  A woman born to melt and faint and fail,<br>  A frail, a delicate, dying woman, sit<br>  To shame ye."  She endur'd the flashing stroke<br>  Of th' axe athwart her eyesight, and the blood<br>  That sprung around her she endur'd: still kept [425]<br>  The lily its unbroken stateliness,<br>  And its pellucid beauty sparkled still,<br>  But all its odours were exhal'd -- the breath<br>  Of life, the tremulous motion was at rest;<br>  A flower of marble on a temple wall, [430]<br>  'Twas fair but lived not, glitter'd but was cold.<br>  While from the headless corpse t' its great account<br>  Went fiercely forth the Pagan's haughty soul.<br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  <br>  Notes:<br>  <br>  <a> [1] Gibbon, Chap. 31.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [2] Whitaker, Hist. of Manchester.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [3] Lewis, Hist. of Britain.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [4] He is so decorated by the Welsh Poets.  See Translation of the Brut of Tysilio, by Peter Roberts.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [5] Proprium gentis, equorum quoque præsagia ac monitus experiri: publicè aluntur iisdem nemoribus ac lucis.  Candidi, et nullo mortali opere contacti, quos pressos sacro curru sacerdos ac rex vel princeps civitatis comitantur, hunnitusque ac fremitus observant. Tac. Germ.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [6] Insigne gentis obliquare crinem, nodoque substringere -- In altitudinem quandam et terrorem, adituri bella, compte, ut hostium oculis, ornantur. Tac. Germ. 38.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [7] Cimbri, parva nunc civitas, sed gloriâ ingens. Tac. Germ.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [8] Primum cana salix, madefacto vimine, parvam<br>  Texitur in puppim, cæesoque induta juvenco,<br>  Vectoris patiens tumidum super emicat amnem:<br>  Sic Venetus stagnante Pado, fusoque Britannus<br>  Navigat oceano.         LUCAN.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [9] Homo autem quem sors immolandum obtulerat, in fontem qui ad locum sacrificiorum scaturiebat vivus immergebatur: qui si facile efflaret animam, faustum renunciabant sacerdotes votum: moxque inde ereptum in vicinum nemus, quod sacrum credebant, suspendentes, inter Deos translatum affirmabant. Quo factum erat, ut beatum se crederet, qui eo immolatione e vivis excaderet.  Accidit nonnunquam reges ipsos simili sorte delectos victimari.  Quod quia faustissimum regno libamen aestimabatur, totius populi multitudo cum summa congratulatione tam insignes victimas prosequebantur.  Eniumvero sic defunctos non omnino mori, sed tam illos quam se ipsos immortales esse. Olaus Magnus, Book 3. cap. 6.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [10] Salisbury - Sarisburga, qu. Caesaris burga.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [11] De pace denique ac bello plerumque in conviviis consultant; tanquam nullo magis tempore aut ad simplices cogitationes pateat animus, aut ad magnas incalescat. Tac. Germ.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [12] Or, Dimetia, i.e. South Wales.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [13] York.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [14] Caer ruth, Exeter.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [15] Holinshed, Book 5, Chap. 6<br>  <br>  </a><a> [16] Henry Huntingdon, Hist.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [17] Ceterum Arii super vires, quibus enumeratos paullo ante populos antecedunt, truces, insitae feritati arte ac tempore lenocinantur: nigra scuta, tincta corpora: atras ad proelia noctes legunt: ipsaque formidine atque umbra feralis exercitus terrorum inferunt, nullo hostium sustinente novum ac velut infernum aspectum: nam primi in omnibus proeliis oculi vincuntur. - Tac. Ger. c. 43.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [18] --et in proximo pignora: unde feminarum ululatus audiri, unde vagitus infantium; hi cuique sanctissimi testes, hi maximi laudatores.  Ad matres, ad conjuges vulnera ferunt: new illae numerare, aut exigere plagas pavent.  Cibosque et hortamina pugnantibus gestant.  Tac. Germ. <br>  <br>  </a><a> [19] Verstegan<br>  <br>  </a><a> [20] Verstegan<br>  <br>  </a><a> [21] Vetere apud Germanos more, quo plerasque feminarum fatidicas, et augescente superstitione, arbitrantur deas. Hist. 4-61.<br>  <br>  </a><a> [22] Then did Aurelius Ambrosius put the Saxons out of all other parts of the land, and repaired such cities, towns, and also churches, as by them had been destroyed or defaced, &amp; c.
